# Subsonic Ultra Green Aircraft Research: Phase IV Final Report – Volume I Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report

Neal A. Harrison, Krishna Hoffman, David S. Lazzara Boeing Research and Technology, Huntington Beach, CA

Eric Y. Reichenbach Boeing Research and Technology, St. Louis, MO

Anthony J. Sclafani, Christopher K. Droney Boeing Commercial Airplanes, Long Beach, CA

# NASA STI Program . . . in Profile

Since its founding, NASA has been dedicated to the advancement of aeronautics and space science. The NASA scientific and technical information (STI) program plays a key part in helping NASA maintain this important role.

The NASA STI program operates under the auspices of the Agency Chief Information Officer. It collects, organizes, provides for archiving, and disseminates NASA’s STI. The NASA STI program provides access to the NTRS Registered and its public interface, the NASA Technical Reports Server, thus providing one of the largest collections of aeronautical and space science STI in the world. Results are published in both non-NASA channels and by NASA in the NASA STI Report Series, which includes the following report types:

TECHNICAL PUBLICATION. Reports of completed research or a major significant phase of research that present the results of NASA Programs and include extensive data or theoretical analysis. Includes compilations of significant scientific and technical data and information deemed to be of continuing reference value. NASA counter-part of peer-reviewed formal professional papers but has less stringent limitations on manuscript length and extent of graphic presentations.   
TECHNICAL MEMORANDUM. Scientific and technical findings that are preliminary or of specialized interest, e.g., quick release reports, working papers, and bibliographies that contain minimal annotation. Does not contain extensive analysis.   
CONTRACTOR REPORT. Scientific and technical findings by NASA-sponsored contractors and grantees.

CONFERENCE PUBLICATION. Collected papers from scientific and technical conferences, symposia, seminars, or other meetings sponsored or co-sponsored by NASA.   
SPECIAL PUBLICATION. Scientific, technical, or historical information from NASA programs, projects, and missions, often concerned with subjects having substantial public interest.   
TECHNICAL TRANSLATION. English-language translations of foreign scientific and technical material pertinent to NASA’s mission.

Specialized services also include organizing and publishing research results, distributing specialized research announcements and feeds, providing information desk and personal search support, and enabling data exchange services.

For more information about the NASA STI program, see the following:

Access the NASA STI program home page at http://www.sti.nasa.gov   
• E-mail your question to help@sti.nasa.gov   
Phone the NASA STI Information Desk at 757-864-9658   
Write to: NASA STI Information Desk Mail Stop 148 NASA Langley Research Center Hampton, VA 23681-2199

# Subsonic Ultra Green Aircraft Research: Phase IV Final Report – Volume I Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report

Neal A. Harrison, Krishna Hoffman, David S. Lazzara Boeing Research and Technology, Huntington Beach, CA

Eric Y. Reichenbach Boeing Research and Technology, St. Louis, MO

Anthony J. Sclafani, Christopher K. Droney Boeing Commercial Airplanes, Long Beach, CA

National Aeronautics and Space Administration

Langley Research Center Hampton, Virginia 23681-2199

Prepared for Langley Research Center under Contract NNL16AA04B

# Abstract

This design report summarizes work performed by the Boeing Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research (SUGAR) team from December 2016 through June 2018.

In Phase IV of the SUGAR program, Boeing is working to improve the productivity of a TTBW configuration by updating the Mach 0.745 vehicle concept to operate efficiently at Mach 0.80. Due to the aerodynamic and structural design challenges associated with adapting the design to a higher speed, design tasks have been broken into several subtasks. The information in this report (Volume I) documents the development of a Mach 0.80 vehicle outer mold line, maturation of TTBW structural concepts, and presents a preliminary high-lift system assessment. Results from these studies were integrated into an aircraft-level performance code that estimates the benefits of TTBW technology, both on its own and as a part of the SUGAR integrated technology suite.

The aerodynamic performance results presented herein detail the high cruise efficiency of the TTBW concept operating at $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ . Results from the RANS code OVERFLOW indicate that the TTBW is aerodynamically well suited for operation at the design $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ transonic cruise Mach number. Aircraft-level performance predictions show that the TTBW offers a $7 . 2 \%$ improvement in fuel burn (per seat) for an economic mission of 900nm, and a $9 . 0 \%$ improvement in fuel burn (per seat) for a 3500nm mission with full passenger payload, relative to an aspect ratio 13 conventional tube and wing configuration of equivalent technology. Integration of the TTBW performance benefits into the SUGAR technology suite shows a $5 5 . 9 \%$ reduction in fuel burn (for the economic mission) relative to the reference (SUGAR Free) aircraft, which is a consistently sized traditional cantilever wing, single-aisle transport typical of the 2008 fleet. This benefit does not include the performance improvement potential of hybrid-electric systems studied in earlier phases of the SUGAR program

Structural concept development work completed in Phase IV has identified several strut attachment concepts that are worth additional development activity – an inboard strut attachment swivel joint concept (which enables the strut to carry axial loads while avoiding strut bending induced by wing twist during flight) significantly simplifies the construction of the main strut. An outboard offset strut attachment concept holds significant potential for further reductions in wing-strut interference drag.

Based on work performed to date on Phase IV and the previous phases of the SUGAR program, the TTBW concept continues to remain a viable concept for significantly improving the performance of future aircraft. High-fidelity aerodynamic analysis of the Mach 0.80 TTBW aircraft has shown fuel burn improvement that has exceeded initial expectations despite the increase in cruise Mach number. Structural concept maturation has continued to refine the TTBW structure, and has found no obvious ‘show-stoppers’ to date. Therefore, the potential benefit of improved performance through the use of a very high aspect ratio strut-braced wing continues to warrant additional study.

# Acknowledgements

This project and report reflect the combined efforts of the Boeing SUGAR team. The coordinated effort of this team has produced this report. Funding for this effort was provided by the NASA Fundamental Aeronautics Program, through the Subsonic Fixed Wing Project followed by the Advanced Air Vehicles Program, through the Advanced Air Transport Technology Project. # Special Data Rights Assertion

Boeing and NASA have agreed to restrict certain intellectual property created during this design effort as Boeing Special Data Rights (SDR). In particular, data shedding light on airfoil, wing, or high lift system design methods and geometry as well as pressure data plotted with scales are considered sensitive. As a result, a number of the figures generated during the aerodynamic design process described in this report are considered as part of the Special Data Rights. The figures identified under that classification are included in a separate special data rights appendix; Boeing has agreed to share those figures with NASA under the Special Data Rights classification and they are not intended for distribution to the broader public. Figures in the SDR appendix will

be labelled with a separate numbering scheme from those in the main body of the report. In the instance that there is both an unlimited rights version of a figure in the main report and a corresponding special data rights version of the same figure in the appendix then that will be noted in the accompanying text in this report.

# 1.0 Introduction

In 2009-2010, Boeing conducted the Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research (SUGAR) study for NASA. In this study, Boeing identified and analyzed advanced concepts and technologies for aircraft that would fly in the 2030-2035 timeframe. Large possible improvements in fuel consumption, emissions, and noise were identified and roadmaps developed for key technologies. Recommendations for future work were made in the Boeing Phase I Final Report (1), including a more detailed consideration of hybrid-electric gas turbine propulsion and a comprehensive, multidisciplinary study of the high aspect-ratio transonic truss-braced wing (TTBW) configuration. These research objectives were pursued during subsequent, focused Phase II efforts addressing TTBW design space exploration (2), hybrid-electric propulsion evaluation (3), and aeroelastic analysis and test (4).

After multidisciplinary optimization using a design environment developed by Phase II partner Virginia Tech, a TTBW configuration was selected promising significant reductions in fuel consumption relative to the present industry standard identified in the Phase I report. The selected design had a maximum range cruise Mach number of 0.70 as it was optimized for fuel reduction without regard to the economic benefits of productivity. It was also determined that airport compatibility could be achieved even for very high-span designs through the use of a folding wing. Detailed aeroelastic analysis and test in the NASA Transonic Dynamics Tunnel (TDT) determined that aeroelastic impacts to TTBW designs are both manageable and analytically predictable, and that the wing weight necessary to prevent flutter does not significantly erode the aerodynamic benefit of increased span.

The conclusion of the Phase II activities spawned several new research areas that would further mature the TTBW. These included a detailed, high-fidelity aerodynamic design effort to determine achievable levels of interference between the primary components and to reduce uncertainty in configuration performance estimates. In addition, preliminary analysis was proposed to extend research up to Mach 0.80. These tasks were the formulation of a Phase III effort.

In Phase III, high speed lines were developed for Mach 0.745. High fidelity analysis, results validation via a wind tunnel test at the NASA Ames Research Center Unitary Plan Wind Tunnel (UPWT) 11-Foot Transonic Wind Tunnel (11-ft TWT) aided in reducing the uncertainty of predicted aircraft performance. The interference drag of the wing-strut join was determined to be three counts (approximately $1 \%$ of total drag at cruise). The performance of the Mach 0.745 vehicle exceeded preliminary estimates by two counts while demonstrating an operable concept across a range of lift coefficients and Mach numbers. In addition to a baseline strut, an alternate strut concept was investigated. The alternate strut included an offset pylon to mitigate

aerodynamic interference in the channel flow formed between the wing and strut. This alternate strut appears to have improved buffet margin as compared to the baseline strut concept. However, uncertainty in buffet prediction methods, and the low maturity of a structural attachment concept have thus far prevented the adoption of this concept.

While the Mach 0.745 design was being finalized, a Mach 0.80 preliminary design was developed using conceptual methods. The Mach 0.80 configuration did show slightly higher block fuel per seat than the slower aircraft as was expected. The execution of the Phase III activities culminated with the following recommendations for future work:

1) Conduct high-fidelity aerodynamic design for a Mach 0.80 transonic truss-braced wing to enable increased fleet productivity.   
2) Undertake a dedicated buffet analysis and test effort to increase understanding and collect data for transonic buffet in truss-braced wing configurations.   
3) Investigate stiffness requirements for the strut-wing connection.   
4) Conduct initial planning for a possible transonic truss-braced wing demonstrator aircraft.

A Phase IV effort was initiated in July 2017 that focused on the development of a Mach 0.80 TTBW design (item one above). This study included the maturation of a Mach 0.80 high-speed outer mold line, as well as vehicle structural concept development. In addition, the study also included updates to the aerodynamic performance predictions of the TTBW concept and a preliminary assessment of high-lift performance. This material is presented in this technical volume.

In addition to the tasks described above, Phase IV funding was increased beyond the initial baseline task to include two transonic wind tunnel tests at the NASA Ames 11-ft TWT. The first of these tests was designed to provide risk reduction for the $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ testing by addressing model roughness-related challenges experienced during the first $\mathsf { M } { = } 0 . 7 4 5$ transonic wind tunnel test. The second test was used for aerodynamic validation of the Mach 0.80 OML (completed in August 2019). Phase IV funding was also increased to perform a detailed high-lift system design task, and conduct a high-lift performance validation test in the NASA LaRC 14- by 22-Foot Subsonic Tunnel (completed in November 2019). Documentation of the funded tasks in the project’s final reporting will be delineated as follows:

• Volume I: High-Speed Design Report   
• Volume II: High-Speed Test Report   
• Volume III: High-Lift System Design Report   
• Volume IV: High-Lift System Test Report

The material presented in this report summarizes the high-speed design the Mach 0.80 TTBW configuration. This includes detailed aerodynamic development of a $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ TTBW loft, configuration design, structural design (including aeroelastic assessment), and aerodynamic performance quantification. The results of this design work was used for the definition of a transonic wind tunnel model which was tested in the NASA Ames 11-ft TWT located at Moffett Field, CA. The results of that wind tunnel test will be documented in Volume II of the Phase IV final report series. In addition, the loft developed in this task served as the basis for the definition of a TTBW high-lift system, as documented in Volume III of the Phase IV final report series.

# 2.0 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing Configuration Design

A preliminary layout (Model 1104-001-NEW) was developed under Phase III (5). This configuration was further refined in the phase IV effort. This section will detail the finalized 1104- 001-RG configuration.

# 2.1 SUGAR TTBW Configuration Evolution

The evolution of the Mach 0.80 TTBW (Figure 1) began with a preliminary layout developed in the Phase III effort that pivoted from the Mach 0.745 vehicle knowledge. Only sweep and thickness changes were made to the planform and all other variables were held constant for consistency. The figure and following paragraphs summarize the work performed and the configuration evolution executed in this effort.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 1 Phase IV Configuration Evolution。
![](images/efa8f616953cc335b6eec79e2d99db1ba89315ecf044256de367343b7485ea76.jpg)  
Figure 1 - Phase IV Configuration Evolution.

The initial release of the 1104-001 configuration was based primarily on the 765-095-RH. The wing sweep was increased and it utilized reduced thickness-to-chord airfoils. The wing was lofted with new airfoils optimized for the Mach 0.80 cruise condition. The main and jury struts were initially lofted with chordwise scaled Mach 0.745 (Revision H) airfoils and then optimized for the Mach 0.80 condition.

Other than higher design speeds, all other requirements are identical for the two configurations. To provide reasonable commonality between designs, the wing planform remains unchanged other than sweep. Additionally, because two passenger aircraft with the same payload layout will tend to load the same (ignoring fuel load vectors), the aerodynamic center (AC) of the wing and the location of the landing gear are maintained at common fuselage stations relative to the Mach

0.745 configuration. No additional sweep was required for the horizontal and vertical, so the empennage is also common.

Revision A was developed bringing the aircraft to consistency with the 765-095-RJ OML and structural layouts. This was a minor change relative to the initial release. Following the Revision A update, the wing was designed in isolation (configuration 1104-001-RB), which provided a solid starting point with which to design the wing and strut in parallel. The development of the wing and strut design continued with manual revisions to the aircraft geometry to prepare the configuration for optimization. The final geometry from Revision C was used as the preliminary seed geometry for aerodynamic and aerostructural optimization, which constituted the majority of Revision D design investigations.

In addition, after the release of the Revision C geometry, a parallel design task was kicked off to investigate various high-lift system architectures. This study was the basis for a more detailed study of the TTBW’s high-lift system development, which will begin in earnest following the completion of the final high-speed OML.

Following the completion of the Revision C seed geometry, the high-speed lines development also examined an offset/curved strut concept, referred to as configuration 1104-001-RE. Investigations focused on both aerodynamic and structural design concepts. At the onset of the program planning, a design optimization task was laid out for the offset strut concept. This configuration (referred to as Revision F) was not initiated.

# 2.2 SUGAR Mach 0.80 Configuration Description

The final release of the Mach 0.80 aircraft geometry is referred to as configuration 1104-001-RG. This geometry represents a single member of a 737-class airplane family. It is a tube and wing configuration with a high mounted truss-braced wing, twin wing mounted engines, a T-tail empennage, and pylon mounted main landing gear. The 154 passenger dual class fuselage is a 6- abreast seating arrangement, and the lower lobe is bulk loaded.

# 2.2.1 Integration

The Mach 0.80 configuration walk-around, general arrangement drawing, and principal characteristics are shown in Figure 2, Figure 3, and Table 1, respectively. Table 2 shows the component axis system origins relative to the body axis.

* To Centroid of the Bogie Contact Patch

# 2.2.2 Wing

The wing is high aspect ratio, truss-braced, and designed for a 0.8 cruise Mach number. The wing is derived from the Mach 0.745 wing by adding 10 degrees sweep (refer to Figure 4) and reduced thickness-to-chord ratio. The chord distribution is identical to the Mach 0.745 wing. Like the Mach 0.745 wing, turbulent supercritical airfoils are used while natural laminar flow is bookkept in the conceptual aerodynamic buildup. The wing layout incorporates a fold at 118 feet of span to accommodate existing Code C gate and taxiway infrastructure.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 4 – Planform Change from Phase III to Phase IV。
![](images/312deb7af6a20e664ad4d8e0b71955c3a406f75923dd16d7667a0b7439a370fd.jpg)  
Figure 4 – Planform Change from Phase III to Phase IV.

The leading edge is populated with full span Krueger flaps broken by the engine pylon. The front spar accommodates a leading edge device chord ratio ranging from ten percent at the root to fifteen percent at the tip. The trailing edge of the wing is fitted with single slotted flaps extending from the side of body, past the outboard planform break, to the inboard side of an all-speed aileron. The all-speed aileron extends outboard to the wing fold. Outboard of the wing fold there is a low speed aileron, which is locked out at high speed. Spoilers are assumed to cover the entire flapped span. The control surface and high lift system layout is the result of trade-offs between low speed aerodynamics and stability and control. The Mach 0.80 planform is illustrated in Figure 5, and the projected reference quantities are shown in Table 3. It should be noted that the reference quantities are displayed for a planform with no leading or trailing edge breaks at the side-of-body.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 4 – Planform Change from Phase III to Phase IV。
![](images/354d5600789b124dba4da4c6f13bbbc816ca8f9e02f94c41615d5a3d7b9be9f3.jpg)  
Figure 5 – SUGAR Mach 0.80 (1104-001-RG) Wing Planform (Projected view, units are inches).

# 2.2.3 Main Strut and Jury Strut

The strut integration concept is shown in Figure 6. The landing gear is integrated into a landing gear pylon that protrudes rigidly from the fuselage. With the landing gear retracted, a sponson fairs over the landing gear. A strut is attached to the outboard end of the gear pylon and is affixed by pin joints. The outboard end of the strut element is attached primarily to the wing front spar also through a pinned attachment. The main strut element is stabilized under buckling load via a jury strut.

The MLG location is the same as the Revision J Mach 0.745 configuration, but the strut attachment at the wing is swept further aft. The resultant strut sweep is 2.88 degrees (aft), and the dihedral is 14.43 degrees. The main strut spar locations are defined in Table 4 and the corner points are shown in Figure 7.

The jury strut location relative to wing span has been updated from the previous phases of study (Section 4.1.3.4). The dihedral of the jury strut was updated to -72.6 degrees (outboard cant relative to wing). The attachments at the wing and main strut are pinned joints (axis aligned streamwise). The jury strut spar locations are defined in Table 5 and the corner points are shown in Figure 8. The jury strut attachments are also pinned connections with the pin axis aligned spanwise.

The jury strut has a linear chord distribution between defining airfoils. The defining airfoils for the jury strut are at Y locations, which correspond to the spar stations in Table 5. These locations are defined in the jury strut reference plane (see Figure 8). The defining airfoils for the main strut are at specified Y locations in the main strut reference plane (see Figure 7). Specified planform breaks in the main strut are defined in Table 4.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 6 – SUGAR Mach 0.80 Main Strut Structural Concept。

Figure 6 – SUGAR Mach 0.80 Main Strut Structural Concept.

# 2.2.4 Vertical Fin

The vertical tail planform is identical to that of the Mach 0.745 Revision J aircraft. It is a $1 3 4 . 6 7 \%$ scale version of a DC-9 twin-jet tail and is sized to provide a tail volume coefficient of 0.066. It is a two spar layout with a two surface simple hinged rudder. The front spar breaks and converges on the rear spar at the vertical tip where the horizontal stabilizer pivot is mounted. The horizontal jack screw mechanism is above the front spar. The vertical planform is illustrated in Figure 9, and the reference planform parameters are shown in Table 6.

# 2.2.5 Horizontal Stabilizer

The horizontal stabilizer planform is identical to that of the Revision J Mach 0.745 aircraft. It is a $1 0 0 . 2 5 \%$ scale version of a DC-9 twin-jet tail and is sized to provide a tail volume coefficient of 1.43. The stabilizer is trimmable with a pivot behind and below the rear spar and a jack screw acting in front of the front spar. The elevator is split into two separate segments spanwise. The horizontal planform is depicted in Figure 10, and the projected planform parameters are shown in Table 7.

# 2.2.6 Fuselage

The SUGAR TTBW fuselage is a single-aisle layout with three-by-three seating. The cargo compartment is bulk loaded and is not designed for containerized cargo. The cross-section is illustrated in Figure 11 and a Layout of Passenger Accommodations (LOPA) is shown in Figure 12. The LOPA and cross-section were defined in Phase I and have not changed.

# 2.2.7 Landing Gear

The Mach 0.80 aircraft landing gear design is identical to that of the Mach 0.745 aircraft. The high aspect ratio, high lift-curve slope SUGAR TTBW configuration allows for a low static ground line. The configuration uses a levered landing gear arrangement to enable this low stance. The landing gear attaches to the gear pylon, a stub wing-like structure that carries landing gear and strut loads. The gear pylon OML was updated to a lower profile, lower drag design that more closely encloses the main gear system and primary structure.

The Phase IV landing gear design is unchanged from Phase III. An overview of the kinematic arrangement is illustrated in Figure 13, and the ground footprint is shown in Figure 14 (not to scale).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 13 – SUGAR TTBW Landing Gear Layout, common for both Mach 0.。

Figure 13 – SUGAR TTBW Landing Gear Layout, common for both Mach 0.745 and Mach 0.80 configurations (765-095 RJ shown).

# 2.2.8 Propulsion

The SUGAR TTBW propulsion system design was not modified during the Phase IV effort. The Phase II gFan+ engine features a pressure ratio of 1.46, which was selected from a GE parametric study accounting for vehicle-level sensitivities. The selected pressure ratio sacrifices specific fuel consumption (SFC) but achieves a lighter weight, lower drag design relative to a higher bypass ratio engine. A conceptual layout of the Phase II gFan+ is shown in Figure 15, and a summary of the propulsion system key characteristics is provided in Table 8. The maximum range cruise Mach number of the Phase IV 1104-001-RG configuration is higher than the Phase III configuration by approximately 0.055 Mach. An SFC factor was applied to the engine dataset within the performance routine to adjust the design point of the engine accordingly.

# 3.0 Structural Analysis

Phase IV structural analysis work focused on two areas: 1) the development of design concepts for connections between the main strut and the sponson and wing to meet fail-safe and structural load requirements, and 2) refinement of the aeroelastic finite element model (FEM) for use in assessing joints and other structural features and for estimating structural weight.

# 3.1 Structural Fittings/Joints

A design concept was developed for a fail-safe swivel joint connection (shown in Figure 16) at the inboard end of the main strut, and for associated backup structure at the sponson. An offset attachment concept was developed for the outboard end of the main strut. The concepts are described in the following sections. The next step to mature the concepts will be to evaluate them in the vehicle FEM, and analyze them in more detail.

# 3.1.1 Inboard – Strut to Sponson

The Mach 0.80 main strut design methodology is similar to that of the Mach 0.745 configuration. For the Mach 0.745 configuration, the main strut front spar plane was aligned with the main landing gear (MLG) front bulkhead. The strut rear spar kicked aft in the strut-sponson fairing region to align with the MLG rear bulkhead.

For the Mach 0.80 configuration, the strut spars (shown in Figure 6 and Figure 7) remain nearly parallel for the entire length of the strut, except at the inboard end, where the spars are spliced with back-to-back (fail-safe requirement) triangular transition lug fittings and a pair of lug straps that are external to the triangular lug fittings. These lugs attach to a single swivel fitting that allows rotation about three axes, thus allowing the strut to carry axial loads while avoiding strut bending induced from wing twist as the wing lifts during flight, and simplifies the construction of the main strut.

The swivel joint is tied to a single set of continuous inboard/outboard oriented nested backup tension fittings that attach to a shear panel and doubler (fail-safe requirement). The shear panel extends inboard to the side of the fuselage. A fore-aft torque box interfaces with the inboard side of the strut swivel fitting and causes the strut fore-aft load component to react at the swivel joint. The torque box will also be used to house the MLG door hinge fittings.

The bending moment induced as the result of the offset between the vertical pivot of the swivel joint and the end pad of the backup primary tension fitting, the inboard/outboard axis double bolt reacts by extending inboard where it attaches to an integral rib on the outer (primary) tension fitting and to the nested inner tension fitting end pad. The rib/end pad vertical load causes a longitudinal beam that is located between the shear panel and the sponson upper fairing panel to react. The longitudinal beam extends forward to the MLG-well forward bulkhead, and aft to the MLG-well aft bulkhead.

Due to the critical nature of the swivel joint to flight safety, a bird splitter located on the forward side of the swivel joint, will be used to protect the joint integrity during a bird strike event.

To ensure the integrity of the swivel joint to carry the limit load in case of failure of any one of its component parts, illustrated in Figure 16, the following provisions are made:

The three swivel pivot joints each have one outer bolt and one inner bolt, with each bolt capable of carrying shear and bolt bending limit loads independent of the other.   
The three swivel pivot joint inner and outer bolts have separate nuts that are safety wired together to prevent the nuts from backing off.   
• A main swivel lug fitting and swivel clevis fitting are the primary components that transfer the strut axial load from the strut transition fittings and lug straps to the pivot cup vertical double bolts.   
• The pivot cup vertical double bolts then transfer the load through the inner and outer pivot cups into the inboard/outboard pivot cup double bolts.   
The inboard/outboard pivot cup double bolts transfer axial and bending loads to the backup nested tension fittings and then into a shear panel that closes out the top of the MLG wheel well and to a longitudinal beam that spans between the MLG-well forward closeout bulkhead and the MLG-well aft closeout bulkhead.   
• Due to the magnitude of the horizontal load component that needs to be reacted by the nested tension fittings, the nested tension fittings are continuous, interrupting the longitudinal torque box.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 16 – Main Strut Swivel Joint at Sponson。
![](images/c53e39334a746707109cac39651bf52a350e1c8e78a89af6f4a1a117b4530c6d.jpg)  
Figure 16 – Main Strut Swivel Joint at Sponson.

# 3.1.2 Outboard – Strut to Wing

The baseline sponson-strut-wing concept for the Mach 0.80 configuration, developed during SUGAR Phase III is shown in Figure 6. At the wing-strut intersection, the baseline strut axis aligns with the wing front spar at the wing planform break (the strut spars straddle the wing front spar). Also, the outboard end of the main strut incorporates lug fittings attached at the end of the straight strut front and rear spars. The baseline strut centerline intersects the wing lower surface at a shallow angle. The main strut attachments at the sponson and wing are pinned joints (axis aligned streamwise).

Due to the sensitivities of the vehicle performance to wing-strut aerodynamic interference and the higher cruise Mach number of 0.8, a trade study was initiated to further investigate the structural design of a curved beam (offset) strut attachment concept (Figure 17). This concept could potentially alleviate some of the aerodynamic interference resulting from the closer aerodynamic coupling present in the baseline strut attachment concept. The offset structural concept is synergistic with the aerodynamic investigations of an offset strut (configuration 1104- 001-RE) as detailed in Section 4.1.2.6. In addition to the aerodynamic benefits of the concept, the offset strut enables a more continuous wing structure as the baseline strut attachment concept requires a local ‘scalloping’ of the wing to reduce aerodynamic interference between the wing and strut. Since the offset strut concept is likely to have a higher component weight than the baseline strut, a detailed trade analysis will be required in the future to quantify the benefit at a system level.

In the offset strut attachment configuration the strut axis is repositioned so the strut spars align with the wing lower skin positioned between the wing front and rear spars. The strut incorporates curved lug fittings with lugs that interface with clevises attached to the wing lower surface. The interface of the strut with the wing is at a more vertical angle. The wing mounted clevises are fastened with tension bolts through the wing lower skin into wing internal backup structure. The resultant pinned joint with the axis aligned streamwise will require a very minimal fairing to cover the joint.

The curved beam configuration allows the fitting section to be minimal just below the lug, increasing in frontal width until the transition point (where the strut bending moment is highest). The strut section height of the spanwise section gradually tapers down to the attach pin joint located just outboard of the intersection of the jury strut with the main strut.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 17 – Main Strut Outboard Attachment Configuration。
![](images/f459c606f5c4580bd48d9d9d9d517f0a24e67bcd44dce8c77b0cd4411ce23b32.jpg)  
Figure 17 – Main Strut Outboard Attachment Configuration.

# 3.2 Aeroelastic Analysis

A detailed FEM of the Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing (TTBW) aircraft was produced. It was prepared for aeroelastic analysis, and sized for typical maneuver load conditions. Gust and flutter analyses were also considered since aeroelastic stability drove final sizing in previous phases of design.

# 3.2.1 Background

The structural design task for the Phase IV Mach 0.80 TTBW includes a refinement of the aeroelastic FEM, previously constructed using Boeing internal funds, for use in determining joint structural sizing and weight estimates. A brief description of the work completed using the previous FEM is provided in the following paragraphs.

Preliminary design of the Mach 0.80 TTBW aircraft was performed during Phase III of the SUGAR program. Leveraging this design, FEM-based aeroelastic and structural analyses were completed between July 2014 and January 2016 under Boeing internal funding. The results showed a moderate weight increase compared to the Mach 0.745 TTBW results from SUGAR Phase II, driven by a reduction in wing thickness associated with the higher cruise Mach number. The results represented early conceptual design estimates subject to change during subsequent detailed, coupled aerostructural design of the Mach 0.80 aircraft. The Mach 0.80 TTBW flutter speed was found to exceed minimum requirements, with no weight increase for flutter.

Under Phase IV, enhancements to the structural FEM focused on upgrades to the prior 2016 model. However, it was determined that the model lacked the integrity required to perform the analyses to Boeing standards. Therefore, Boeing took on the task of developing a new FEM for use in subsequent studies.

To complete the aeroelastic model for the enhanced FEM, updates were made to the Doublet Lattice Model (DLM), and mass properties. Initial aeroelastic analyses to check out the model were executed in parallel with the application of mass to the FEM, which was completed in June 2018. The details of the FEM enhancements are discussed next, and results of aeroelastic analysis and sizing optimization performed are presented.

# 3.2.2 Phase IV FEM Refinement

The improvements to the TTBW FEM included updating the wing and strut geometry to a recent configuration, the use of a detailed strut model, additions to the doublet lattice aerodynamic model and refinement of aerodynamic panels, application of new mass properties, an updated optimization setup, and better model organization to facilitate future studies in which components are modified. While more refined than the previous Mach 0.80 FEM, the new FEM is the next step, rather than the final stage of refinement. As the structural design and configuration mature, the FEM can evolve to be more accurate.

# 3.2.3 Configuration Geometry and Data

The structural model of the wing, strut, and jury was created using the 1104-001-RC_des05 configuration geometry. This configuration was selected based on the work schedule in 2017. Waiting for the finalized 1104-001-RC geometry would have been too much of a delay. The differences in the two configurations are structurally insignificant. The configuration geometry specified the OML and major features; the internal structure was defined by the analyst based on a typical rib spacing, and percent-chord locations of spars provided by the configuration designer.

The FEM updates ensured that the aerodynamic model matched the 1104-001-RC_des05 geometry of the wing, strut, and jury structure. The fuselage structural and aerodynamic models were not changed from the previous 2016 FEM, and were integrated with the new model of the wing and strut. The mass properties provided in 2018 were for the 1104-001-RC configuration.

# 3.2.4 Structural Idealization and Optimization Model

All structural elements of the wing, strut, and jury are of composite material except the titanium splitter plate in the strut. The material properties are listed in Table 9. The spar and rib caps, as well as spar stiffeners, are modeled with beam elements (Nastran PBEAM) to enable nonlinear analysis. The composite beam material has a Young's modulus (E) of 10,500,000 lbs/in2 , a shear modulus (G) of 4,038,500 lbs/in2 , a Poisson's ratio (ν) of 0.3, and a density of 0.067 lbs/in3. The skins, spar and rib webs, and spar intercostals, are laminates of composite material (Nastran PCOMP), with each ply consisting of orthotropic material with a modulus of elasticity in the fiber direction (E1) of 23,500,000 lbs/in2 , a modulus across the fiber direction (E2) of $1 , 6 0 0 , 0 0 0 | \mathsf { b s } / \mathsf { i n } ^ { 2 } ,$ , a Poisson's ratio of 0.34, an in-plane shear modulus (G12) of 800,000 lbs/in2, and a density of 0.067 lbs/in3.

Table 9 – Composite Material Properties.   

<table><tr><td colspan="3">1D Beam Laminate Stiffness</td></tr><tr><td>E</td><td>10,500,000</td><td>lb/in2</td></tr><tr><td>v</td><td>0.30</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>G</td><td>4,038,500</td><td>lb/in2</td></tr><tr><td>ρ</td><td>0.067</td><td>lbs/in3</td></tr></table>

<table><tr><td colspan="3">2D Element Lamina Stiffness</td></tr><tr><td>E1</td><td>23,500,000</td><td>Ib/in2</td></tr><tr><td>E2</td><td>1,600,000</td><td>Ib/in2</td></tr><tr><td>v</td><td>0.34</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>G12</td><td>800,000</td><td>Ib/in2</td></tr><tr><td>ρ</td><td>0.067</td><td>Ibs/in3</td></tr></table>

The skin laminates consist of plies in the 0, +/-45, and 90 degree directions with a stacking sequence of $+ 4 5 / - 4 5 / 9 0 / 0 / 9 0 / - 4 5 / + 4 5$ , which represents a balanced and symmetric layup. The intent is not to capture the actual layup, which would have many more plies in each orientation, but to model the total thickness of plies in each orientation, while maintaining symmetry and balance. Therefore, plies in the laminate model are equivalent to groups of plies in reality. In addition to the skin plies, the laminates for the stiffened panels of the wing, strut, and jury, include plies representing the panel stiffeners. The stiffeners or stringers consist of base flanges and webs forming a 'T' or blade stringer cross-section. The bases are represented by one ply, and the webs by another ply, in the laminate, as illustrated in Figure 18. In each of these plies, the discrete bases or webs are smeared into the homogenous ply by adjusting the orthotropic material properties of the ply to match the in-plane stiffness in each direction. The thickness of the ply matches the thickness of the base or height of the web, and the density of the material is adjusted so that the weight of the homogeneous ply matches that of the discrete members it represents.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 9 – Composite Material Properties。
![](images/85ecb111261ea97800bf09cac4f0d57bf5f4bb20058949a1f504237b6105c935.jpg)  
Figure 18 – Composite Laminate Representation of Stiffened Panels.

For the wing, strut, and jury, the stringer geometry was assumed to be the same, with a base width of 0.5 inch and a base thickness equal to half the thickness of the skin. The stringer web has a fixed thickness of 0.19 inch, and its height equals ten times the thickness of the skin. Stringer spacing is 5.0 inches, measured from center to center. This arrangement means that the stinger

cross sectional area scales up with skin thickness, keeping the stringer area equal to half the skin area, or equal to one third of the total area of the stringer and skin cross sections. The stringer area ratio is consistent with the Phase II study, but the stringer base width has been reduced while the stringer height has been increased from three times skin thickness to ten times skin thickness. This is more consistent with an optimized stringer as determined by the high fidelity buckling study completed for the Mach 0.745 vehicle. That this one stringer geometry is applicable to the wing, strut, and jury, and to skin panels, spar webs, and rib webs, is a simplifying assumption for the present maturity of the structural design. Other factors, like depth of the wing box, rib cutouts at stringer intersections, the concept for attaching the stringers to the skin, and stringer web crippling, may require different proportions for the stringer cross section, as well as different stringer spacing. For this stringer geometry, the material properties of the base ply are $\mathsf { E } 1 = 2 , 3 5 0 , 0 0 0 \mathsf { \Omega } | \mathsf { b } \mathsf { s } / \mathsf { i } \mathsf { n } ^ { 2 }$ , $\mathsf { E } 2 = 0 . 0 0 0 1 \ : \mathsf { I b s } / \mathsf { i n } ^ { 2 }$ , $\nu = 0 . 3 4$ , $\mathsf { G 1 2 } = 8 0 0 , 0 0 0 \mathsf { | b s / i n ^ { 2 } }$ , and density $= 0 . 0 0 6 7$ lbs/in3, and for the web ply are $\mathsf { E 1 } = 8 9 3 , 0 0 0 \mathsf { | b s / i n ^ { 2 } }$ , E2 = 0.0001 lbs/in2, $\nu = 0 . 3 4$ , ${ \sf G } 1 2 = 8 0 0 , 0 0 0$ ${ \| \mathsf { b } \mathsf { s } / \mathsf { i } \mathsf { n } ^ { 2 } } .$ , and density $= 0 . 0 0 2 5 5 \ : 1 6 / \mathrm { i n } ^ { 3 }$ .

Table 10 – Custom Material Properties for Stiffener Layers.   

<table><tr><td colspan="3">Stringer Base</td><td colspan="3">Stringer Web</td></tr><tr><td>E1</td><td>2,350,000</td><td>Ib/in2</td><td>E1</td><td>893,000</td><td>Ib/in2</td></tr><tr><td>E2</td><td>0.0001</td><td>Ib/in2</td><td>E2</td><td>0.0001</td><td>Ib/in2</td></tr><tr><td>v</td><td>0.34</td><td></td><td>v</td><td>0.34</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>G12</td><td>800,000</td><td>Ib/in2</td><td>G12</td><td>800,000</td><td>Ib/in2</td></tr><tr><td>ρ</td><td>0.0067</td><td>Ibs/in3</td><td>ρ</td><td>0.00255</td><td>Ibs/in3</td></tr></table>

The control surfaces on the wing are not sized, and are modeled only to transfer loads from the aerodynamic model into the wing box. They use a laminate similar to the stiffened panels of the wing skin, with fixed ply thicknesses for a total skin thickness of 0.1 inch, a stringer base thickness of 0.05 inch, and a stringer web height of 1.0 inch. The material properties are the same as those for the wing skins, except that density is zero for all the plies. The mass of the control surfaces is represented by nonstructural mass provided in the mass properties statement.

To represent the structural elements within the structural OML, correct element offsets are required. For the beam elements of the rib and spar caps, areas are small, so it is a reasonable simplification to neglect the offset, placing the neutral axis of the beam at the OML. In addition, the cross sectional geometries of the caps are not yet defined, without which the correct offset cannot be determined. Including beam offsets requires that the optimization model include links between beam cross-section design variables and the beam offsets, so that the offsets remain correct as the beam sizing changes. When the structural design has matured further, the beam offsets and other details can be included in the FEM. For the stiffened laminates of the skin panels, the presence of the smeared stiffener plies makes the laminate quite thick, so that if its thickness is centered on the OML plane, then the structure protrudes significantly past the OML.

Therefore, the skin panel elements are modeled with their normals pointing inward from the OML, and the offset is defined so that the bottom of the first ply in the laminate rests on the OML. From this location, the plies stack up in the direction of the element normal, that is, inward. In Nastran, this arrangement is achieved by specifying the Z0 offset as 0.0 on the PCOMP entries. The approach is illustrated in Figure 19 and Figure 20.

# Maintain Structural OML

Nodes are located on OML surface.   
Element normal vectors point inward.   
. Element thickness grows to, or shrinks from, the inside.
