![](images/891d962e07ff4f70585e2d79c28e3e7e6c690ee0ba06122fead5216fdbd93b35.jpg)

# PCOMP

• $Z 0 = 0 . 0$

# PSHELL

ZOFFS on CQUAD4/CTRIA3 linked to thickness design variable

图片摘要：该图主要展示 19 – Offsets for Nastran PCOMP and PSHELL Entries to Maintai。
![](images/09bd5f4056b811b2c3ef06be0a119500a4e3b3b1aaadcc14bf4ed41211347ed3.jpg)  
Figure 19 – Offsets for Nastran PCOMP and PSHELL Entries to Maintain OML during Sizing.   
Figure 20 – Element Normal Directions in the Wing Box.

The structural optimization model uses four types of design variables. For beam elements, the beam cross-sectional area is the design variable. The lower bound is 0.01 in2 and the upper bound is $5 . 0 \mathsf { i n } ^ { 2 }$ . For composite laminates, the design variables are the thicknesses of the 0, 45, and 90 degree plies, each bounded between 0.001 inch and 3.0 inches. Since the design variable for each orientation is the thickness of one ply, and the aforementioned stacking sequence includes one ply of 0-degree material, four plies of $+ / .$ - 45-degree material, and two plies of 90-degree material, the total skin thickness is the sum of the 0-degree design variable, four times the 45-degree design variable, and two times the 90-degree design variable. Figure 21 illustrates the total laminate thickness. The thickness of the stringer base ply is half of the total skin thickness, and the thickness of the stringer web ply is ten times the total skin thickness. For example, with a skin thickness of 0.2 inch, the total laminate thickness is 2.3 inches. The ply thickness design variables are continuous, while in reality, the laminate grows thicker by the addition of a discrete number of plies. Ideally, a scheme for 'ply integerization' should be applied during optimization, or as a correction after sizing, but this adds complexity and effort to the optimization process that may not be appropriate for the present maturity of the structural design. The assumption implicit in

omitting ply integerization is that the skins are thick enough that rounding up to the nearest integer multiple of ply thickness will not be a significant impact on weight.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 20 – Element Normal Directions in the Wing Box。
![](images/de00ac875e0a28edd915f50254cd9dba863738316bcdb29a60d7b28b191d560c.jpg)  
Figure 21 – Total Laminate Thickness for Balanced Stacking Sequence.

The responses and constraints for beam elements are the axial stress at each end of the beam. The stresses are constrained to 90,000 psi in compression and in tension. For composite laminates, the responses are major and minor composite strains at the extreme plies of the skin (plies 1 and 7 in the stacking sequence). These strains are constrained to 4500 microstrains (0.0045) in tension and in compression. In addition, the total laminate thickness is constrained to be above 0.16 inch, and the thickness of skin ply material in each orientation is constrained to be at least ten percent of the total skin thickness. These responses and constraints represent a basic set of checks for structural integrity, for an initial estimate of structural weight. As an aircraft design matures, many more checks are required.

In addition to the strength sizing described above, the wing, strut, and jury are optimized for buckling loads. The first nine buckling modes are constrained to have eigenvalues above 1.0, which means there is to be no buckling below the applied loads, which are ultimate loads. This constraint is intended for global buckling modes, as opposed to local panel or stiffener buckling, but because it is applied to all modes detected in the FEM, it can be conservative if local buckling modes are present. Some effort was put into the FEM development to eliminate local buckling modes, especially in structures that are not being sized, such as the titanium splitter and the sponson structure. In many aircraft, some structural members are allowed to be locally buckled at limit load. For the TTBW, global buckling of the strut and jury is the focus, and neither of these members should buckle below ultimate load. To properly address the issue of buckling at the local and global levels, the structural design criteria for the TTBW should be defined in greater detail.

The FEM is divided into design zones or property regions corresponding to the bays formed by ribs and spars. This allows panel thickness or beam cross sectional area to vary for each bay. The property regions in the wing, strut, and jury are indicated by different colors in Figure 22. The patterns are the same for the upper and lower sides of the structure. A good number of property regions allows the sizing to be tailored to the internal loads of each region, saving weight. As the structural concept matures, manufacturing considerations need to be included to ensure that variations between adjacent bays don’t result in unbuildable structure.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 21 – Total Laminate Thickness for Balanced Stacking Sequence。
![](images/3cea9290b3d3bb49024b9e5769fa385d3200d0d09b4598596b09c253c4e3f5c7.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 22 – Property Regions of the Wing, Strut, and Jury.

For the Mach 0.80 TTBW, the structural optimization is done entirely in Nastran. No local optimization is performed in HyperSizer, as was done for the Mach 0.745 TTBW in Phase II. This approach reflects the maturity of the Mach 0.80 structural design relative to the Mach 0.745 design. More work is needed on the Mach 0.80 design to refine structural concepts, practical limits on panel geometries, manufacturing approaches, and constraints at the global level, before detail is added at the local level through application of HyperSizer or other panel optimization tools. To move to the next level of refinement, design development is needed for the wing, strut, and jury, to identify the concepts and restrictions that apply to each of them. For future evolution of the TTBW FEM and optimization process, definition of the design and sizing criteria is critical.

# 3.2.5 Wing, Strut, and Jury Features in the FEM

The wing was modeled with a conventional layout of two spars, and streamwise ribs spaced at 30 inches. The rib spacing was adjusted to accommodate key features like the jury and strut attachments, the outboard wing fold, the front spar break, and the engine pylon attachment. Figure 23 shows a planform view of the wing structural layout. The front spar location was defined as 15 percent chord from the wing root to the front spar break, and then increasing linearly from 15 percent to 30 percent chord from the break to the wingtip. The rear spar was defined at 68 percent chord from the wing root to wingtip. The strut was modeled with a similar layout, shown in Figure 24, with streamwise ribs spaced at 30 inches, a front spar at 15 percent chord, and a rear spar at 70 percent chord. The front spar of the jury was defined at 10 percent chord and the rear spar at 80 percent chord, with streamwise (horizontal) ribs spaced at 13.75 inches. Figure 25 shows the structural layout of the jury.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 22 – Property Regions of the Wing, Strut, and Jury。
![](images/04d701a89683e3ae05ff42f2c8fa0f3e1200e27d4dca19a17ece24ef881928ba.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 23 – Wing Structural Layout.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 23 – Wing Structural Layout。
![](images/ff51799e9552c30b8fa99caba2a0a6fa45880052c9c20a84537b9ae47ddd3ebd.jpg)  
Figure 24 – Strut Structural Layout.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 24 – Strut Structural Layout。
![](images/1369940350784617ac303a257f6d3a891c795db7e0d3096ec4c5a83b3f22c38a.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 25 – Jury Structural Layout.

The spar locations and rib spacing in the wing, strut, and jury, are initial estimates, and require a design layout effort to better establish them. In fact, the internal structural layout itself would benefit from a structural and manufacturing study, as unconventional layouts (like a multispar arrangement) may have advantages for the unusually long and thin wing box of the TTBW, and the thin structures of the strut and jury.

The FEM representations of the connections of the strut to the sponson and wing do not reflect the latest designs described in the previous section, as these designs were developed after the structural FEM, and at the same time when the FEM was being developed for aeroelastic analysis. The modeling of the new joint concepts in the FEM, and the assessment of their impact on the sizing of the strut and other parts of the airframe, is a future task. The descriptions below are of the current structural connections in the FEM.

At the connection of the strut to the wing, auxiliary ribs and an intercostal spar were added to reinforce the wing. The connections link the front and rear spars of the strut to a wing rib, the strut front spar connecting between the wing front spar and the intercostal spar, and the strut rear spar attaching just aft of the intercostal spar, as shown in Figure 26. The attachments are modeled with multipoint constraints (Nastran RBE2 entries) and are aligned to form a pinned connection. The forward attachment links the fore-aft, lateral, and vertical translations, and the aft attachment links only the lateral and vertical translations. All rotations are free, however, the presence of two attachment points at each end of the strut prevents torsional rotation of the strut. Care was taken to ensure that the axis of the pinned connection between the strut and wing is parallel to the axis of the pinned connection between the strut and sponson, so that no bending loads are transmitted to the strut. The connection of the strut to the sponson is similar

to that of the strut to the wing, as shown in Figure 27, and the front and rear spar of the strut are connected to the front and rear spars (or bulkheads) of the sponson.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 26 – Connection of Strut to Wing (Skins Removed for Clarity)。
![](images/0ace2fd13a64681454e97e44e2e19065a38267baa7eff46350d07fb8baaf45b4.jpg)  
Figure 26 – Connection of Strut to Wing (Skins Removed for Clarity).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 26 – Connection of Strut to Wing (Skins Removed for Clarity)。
![](images/4ead46a73395d9e5dc63fdf3e76521e8954c79e64f53596ea61e6aac5ced40f1.jpg)  
Figure 27 – Connection of Strut to Sponson (Strut Skin Removed for Clarity).

The connection of the jury to the wing links the front and rear spars of the jury to a single point on a wing rib, as shown in Figure 28. The attachment links three translations, fore-aft, lateral (normal to the plane of the jury, which is slightly canted), and vertical (in the plane of the jury), as well as rotation about the vertical axis (in the plane of the jury). Rotations about a fore-aft axis, and rotations in the plane of the jury, are free. The connection of the jury to the strut is similar, attaching the front and rear spars of the jury to a single point on an auxiliary rib in the strut. This attachment links the three translations, along axes parallel to that of the jury-wing connection, but leaves all rotations free. The effect of the attachments at the ends of the jury is

to prevent any bending or torsional loads from entering the jury, but without leaving the jury free to twist about its lengthwise axis.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 27 – Connection of Strut to Sponson (Strut Skin Removed for 。
![](images/22347785755f8e38e1a65bd7adf7a41ee74f35adf291dd36f2ea5f3f9f5f8a76.jpg)  
Figure 28 – Connections of the Jury to the Strut and Wing (Skins Removed for Clarity).

The control surfaces - flaps, flaperon, aileron - are modeled as three-dimensional structures but they are not realistic and only serve to transfer loads from the aerodynamic control surfaces in the doublet lattice model into the wing box. In the aerodynamic model, the control surfaces are accurately modeled, with correct dimensions and hinge lines in the correct locations. The structural control surfaces were modeled somewhat arbitrarily within the OML of the wing. The control surfaces are connected to the wing box at inboard and outboard hinges, which are modeled with RBE2 entries, as shown in Figure 29. The inboard hinge rigidly links all six degrees of freedom, while the outboard hinge leaves translation along the hinge line, and rotation about the hinge line, free. The effect is that control surface hinge moments are reacted at the inboard hinge. There is no flexibility in the hinge model, as such, data were not available during model development. Therefore, dynamic modes of vibration or flutter, which include control surface movements due to hinge flexibility, will not be correctly captured by this model. This is yet another feature that can be refined when the vehicle design matures and the required data are available.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 28 – Connections of the Jury to the Strut and Wing (Skins Re。
![](images/39b9f781a3c44729efb754b17b317e6b7da53e36f830326dc5b22592a1dd3d12.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 29 – Control Surface Hinge Connections (Upper Skins Removed for Clarity).

For the engine, nacelle, and pylon, grid points at which weight and aerodynamic loads are applied are connected to the wing using interpolation constraint elements (Nastran RBE3 entries). The RBE3 element attaches to wing grids located at skin and rib intersections, between the front spar and rear spar. The RBE3 element reacts with all rigid body deflections at the independent grid, where loads are applied, but adds no stiffness to the model at the dependent grids.

The wing fold is not represented in the structural FEM. Because the joint needs to carry the same loads as the wing box, the load paths are unchanged by omitting the joint. The weight impact of the joint is captured by adding nonstructural masses at the fold location to represent the joint. When the detailed design of the joint is defined, a better representation can be added to the FEM.

To protect the strut from a bird strike, a titanium splitter is formed at its leading edge by two surfaces extending from the top and bottom of the front spar to a line offset one inch aft of the leading edge of the strut. The titanium panels of the splitter are not optimized, and their fixed sizing was given enough stiffness to prevent local buckling below the applied loads. It is assumed that the jury strut will withstand a bird strike without a splitter due to its narrow leading edge.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 29 – Control Surface Hinge Connections (Upper Skins Removed 。
![](images/a4aac3ab168e4cadacd7f0d8e71e373989557308fdff36bc7daf18b318f1173e.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 30 – Titanium Splitter (Yellow/Brown) in Strut Leading Edge (Upper Skin Removed for Clarity).

# 3.2.6 Center Body/Sponson FEM

The sponson connects the root of the strut to the fuselage. It includes upper skin, two spars, ribs, and a fuselage bulkhead, as shown in Figure 31. These members are not included in structural sizing optimization. The sponson is modeled with representative properties, including stiffened panels modeled as composite laminates similar to those in the wing. Although no gear loads were included in the current study, the sponson also reacts gear loads and a gear beam model is attached to the sponson. Gear conditions are not critical for the structure sized in the current study and are not expected to impact results. The properties in the sponson structure were adjusted to avoid local buckling modes under the applied loads.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 30 – Titanium Splitter (Yellow/Brown) in Strut Leading Edge 。
![](images/dfaf4364d000d96384babe9c4ebc5ffd93bb8ba721442df695cd2af6b5ceebe3.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 31 – Sponson Structural Model.

In the present study, as in past TTBW aeroelastic studies, the focus is on the wing, strut, and jury, with the fuselage providing structural support, carrying payload and other weight, and the tail trimming the vehicle. As structural concepts are developed for strut-to-sponson joints, and design details are defined to accommodate the joints, landing gear, and associated back up structure, the sponson and center fuselage structure will need to be included in structural optimization.

# 3.2.7 Finite Element Model Integration

The fuselage and tail model from the 2016 FEM was used in the refined FEM. It consists of beam models of the forward fuselage, aft fuselage, and empennage, with a three dimensional barrel section model of the center fuselage to support the wing and strut models with the correct boundary conditions. The forward fuselage and aft fuselage attach to the barrel section using an RBE3 elements, which add no stiffness to the centerbody. The beam models are nearly rigid, and serve to support the aerodynamic surfaces of the fuselage and empennage, and transfer loads into the barrel section of the center fuselage. The goal of the finite element analysis was to size the wing, strut, and jury; hence the assumption was made that the dynamics of the forward and aft fuselage and tail would not significantly impact the wing dynamics. The barrel section, shown in Figure 32, is modeled with representative properties for aluminum structure. The fuselage and tail beams, as well as the barrel section, have no material density, and their mass properties are represented by concentrated masses derived from the vehicle weight statement. Neither the beam nor barrel sections of the fuselage are included in structural sizing optimization.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 31 – Sponson Structural Model。
![](images/7b26aa3af4ee335b38091d6616251866c65314b69fc54686e1a50dc74280aef6.jpg)  
Figure 32 – Fuselage Barrel Section Structural Model.

In the development of the 2016 FEM, the barrel section was lengthened to accommodate the wing and strut positions for the Mach 0.80 vehicle. During development of the refined FEM, the tail model was adjusted to match the geometry of the 1104-001-RC configuration. The integrated FEM is shown in Figure 33.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 32 – Fuselage Barrel Section Structural Model。
![](images/00d9cccb78deec4cd7d7043ed0bc817b06d9790c716bffccc68ac5b28841bc17.jpg)  
Figure 33 – Integrated Vehicle Finite Element Model.

The wing is attached to the centerbody in three ways. The first is the primary vertical connection between the wing and fuselage, and occurs at the wing front and rear spars. Here, a framemounted post extends up from the fuselage and attaches to a trap panel. The trap panel extends from the post to the front and rear spars of the wing at the side-of-body rib.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 33 – Integrated Vehicle Finite Element Model。
![](images/acbe0832e1c14f297be83d6ef9179c7496d82b6e056646e2fae72d704a7adab7.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 34 – Primary Vertical Connection between Wing and Body.

The second is the primary fore-aft connection between the wing and fuselage. Here, the grids along the fuselage wing cutout are connected, in the fore-aft direction only, to the closest grids on the wing’s lower skin.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 34 – Primary Vertical Connection between Wing and Body。
![](images/d7666fd2e48f58c65aadb30b2b9b872dec933a5ea7dd2c5076ea3f384335eaaf.jpg)  
Figure 35 – Primary Fore-Aft Connection between Wing and Body.

The third is the primary lateral connection between the wing and fuselage, where the end grids of three fuselage frames are connected, in the inboard-outboard direction only, to grids on the wing lower skin.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 35 – Primary Fore Aft Connection between Wing and Body。
![](images/4c661dd7da412b5f88b6eb6a38eb8295c6a32d48dab50a76cdbd70fd694ce546.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 36 – Primary Lateral Connection between Wing and Body.

# 3.2.8 Nonstructural Mass and Fuel/Payload Cases

Material density of structural elements in the FEM determines the weight that is optimized during structural sizing. As in previous TTBW studies, an increased density for the composite material is used to represent cutouts, fasteners, and other unmodeled features. Density weight, or structural weight, applies to the wing, strut, and jury. All the remaining weight is applied to the FEM to match the vehicle weight statement, and is spread on the model as concentrated mass items. The weight statement specifically breaks out the structural, or load bearing material, weight for the wing, strut, and jury, so that these values can be replaced by the density weight from the FEM. For the other components, including the fuselage, tails, and control surfaces, the entire mass of each item is applied by spreading concentrated masses over the component in the FEM. For the engines, nacelles, and pylons, single-point concentrated masses with inertia terms were used. For other discrete items, like the wing fold joint, landing gear, and auxiliary power unit, the concentrated masses were spread over multiple grids in the locality of the item.

In the present FEM, the weights of joints and splices are captured in either the increased density of composite material, or in the general nonstructural mass spread over each component. As the structural concepts and designs develop, the locations and design details of joints and splices will be defined well enough to be represented by discrete masses in the FEM. Such refinement will reveal any impacts to the local structure surrounding these features.

To represent a range of flight weights, five fuel and payload combinations were defined. They are listed in Table 11. Concentrated masses were spread in the regions of the payload bay, a forward and aft fuselage or body tank, a center tank in the wing box, and a main or outboard tank in each wing, to represent the mass conditions.

Table 11 – Payload and Fuel Combinations.   

<table><tr><td>Mass Case</td><td>Fuel</td><td>Payload</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>Fuel to reach MTOW from MZFW</td><td>Max to Reach MZFW</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>Full</td><td>Max to reach MTOW with Full Fuel</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>Full</td><td>None</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>Reserve</td><td>Max to Reach MZFW</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>Reserve</td><td>None</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3">MTOW = Maximum Takeoff Weight; MZFW = Maximum Zero Fuel Weight</td></tr></table>

# 3.2.9 Aerodynamic Model

The doublet lattice aerodynamic model from the 2016 FEM was adjusted to fit the geometry of the refined structural FEM. The planforms of the horizontal and vertical tails, and the strut, were modified to fit the configuration geometry of the structural FEM.

Some refinements were made to improve the accuracy of the model, which is depicted in Figure 37. Various minor adjustments were made to aerodynamic panel coordinates to improve alignment and symmetry among panels. To improve the representation of the spanwise load distribution for the wing and strut, panels were added to extend the wing and strut to the vehicle centerline. These carry-through panels are visible in Figure 38. Small panels were added to connect the strut panel to the wing panel as shown in Figure 39. Aerodynamic panels for the jury strut were added to the model. The mesh density was increased to maintain the aspect ratio (span/chord) of the aerodynamic boxes between one and three, and to ensure that box chords were appropriate for the reduced frequencies involved in flutter analysis. Care was taken to ensure that the intersections of aerodynamic panels aligned with box edges.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 37 – Doublet Lattice Aerodynamic Model with Refined Mesh and。
![](images/6d3c592e86936a905d2536c490472fd17eef37a952d66aa0c8560a950f446017.jpg)  
Figure 37 – Doublet Lattice Aerodynamic Model with Refined Mesh and Updated Panels.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 37 – Doublet Lattice Aerodynamic Model with Refined Mesh and。
![](images/54a8bcbd1d2e0026bb3a395e5c0d926f829eb8937da0c3f7a06ccdb39c0c8ec3.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 38 – Detail of Aerodynamic Model Showing Carry-through Panels and Updated Tail.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 38 – Detail of Aerodynamic Model Showing Carry through Panel。
![](images/f9d17894abb6dd0916ac64974126d5fa10feec1e59f5a50dc3eb57da98942dc5.jpg)  
Figure 39 – Detail View of Aerodynamic Model of Jury and Strut-to-Wing Connection.

In the Phase III Mach 0.745 TTBW aeroelastic study, correction factors were calculated for the doublet lattice aerodynamics to match computational fluid dynamics (CFD) results. The 2016 FEM did not use aerodynamic corrections because CFD analyses had not been completed at the time. In addition, previous studies showed better correlation with the uncorrected analysis results. The present study was initially performed with theoretical doublet lattice aerodynamics.

# 3.2.10 Maneuver Load Conditions

Load conditions investigated include symmetric 2.5g and -1g balanced maneuver load conditions. Balanced maneuver load conditions are FAA-required symmetric pitch conditions with a specified load factor and tail loads calculated to produce zero pitch acceleration. Flight loads were calculated in Nastran Sol 144 with the horizontal tail used to trim the vehicle.

The external loads and the structural sizing are interdependent, so an initial sizing of the model was done with assumed load conditions. The wing, strut, and jury were sized for strength and buckling using 2.5g and -1g balanced maneuver conditions at a Mach 0.80 and dynamic pressures of $2 . 0 \ \mathsf { I b } / \mathsf { i n } ^ { 2 }$ and $4 . 0 \ \mathsf { l b } / \mathsf { i n } ^ { 2 }$ . These initial cases were used to check out the model and the optimization parameters, and to provide a sized structure for subsequent maneuver loads analysis.

Next, a maneuver loads survey was performed to determine critical load conditions. A $2 . 5 8$ pull up, a -1g push over, and 1g steady flight conditions were analyzed at the six flight conditions shown in Figure 40, and the five mass cases listed in Table 11.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 40 – Flight Conditions for Maneuver Loads Survey。
![](images/c585edecf4db813307ede924c1a28bc28fe6e1e8f185385dcfeab3b5b59f483e.jpg)  
Figure 40 – Flight Conditions for Maneuver Loads Survey.

After running the analyses for the 90 load conditions, the loads at each of the wing and strut monitor points were analyzed for the maximum and minimum of shear load, vertical bending load and torsional load. For an intial selection of critical loads, a total of 30 conditions were identified that caused either a maximum or a minimum load at one or more monitor points.

Among them, four conditions were identified that were critical for the most monitor points. They were two pairs of $2 . 5 8$ and -1g maneuvers. Both pairs occur at Mach 0.38, at an airspeed of 250 knots, at sea level, which was the slowest airspeed analyzed, and resulted in relatively high angles of attack and tail deflections for trim. One pair is for Mass Case 2, full fuel with payload to reach MTOW. For this pair, most of the critical loads are on the outer wing, probably a result of having the least local inertia relief despite the highest applied loads. The other pair is for Mass Case 5, reserve fuel with no payload. This case has the least weight, and most of the critical loads are on the strut. With no fuel in the wing there is less inertia relief, and the strut has to carry more load. To fully understand the interplay of competing factors that makes these load conditions the most critical would require more in-depth investigation than has been done as of this writing.

The deformation of the wing and strut under a $2 . 5 8$ pull-up condition at Mach 0.85 and altitude of 26,468 feet is shown in Figure 41. The influence of the strut is clearly visible; inboard of the strut connection, the wing deforms relatively little, and the majority of the deflection is outboard of the strut. The wingtip deflections are about $6 \%$ of the semispan, suggesting that linear structural analysis is appropriate at least for preliminary sizing.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 40 – Flight Conditions for Maneuver Loads Survey。
![](images/bfea608a39437689aeaa6b94fe3505060c27bac5bb5e983b1d469b575022592d.jpg)  
Figure 41 – Structural Deflection for a 2.5g Steady Pull-Up at Mach 0.85, Altitude 26,468 feet.

Aerodynamic pressures associated with rigid body motion are shown in Figure 42 and Appendix Figure A.1. The pressures match expectations for a symmetric pitch motion and provide verification of the doublet lattice model implementation. Incremental aerodynamic pressures associated with airframe flexibility are shown in Figure 43 and A.2. Negative lift on the outboard wings (indicated in blue) as is expected per swept wing aeroelastic effects, providing further model validation.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 41 – Structural Deflection for a 2.5g Steady Pull Up at Mach。
![](images/40f5800bae8f7765f4c045a9ebc9a7dee37826352495896ce5530e67f9cc09d4.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 42 – Rigid Pressure Distribution for a 2.5g Steady Pull-Up at Mach 0.85, Altitude 26,468 feet.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 42 – Rigid Pressure Distribution for a 2.5g Steady Pull Up a。
![](images/0932218e90a8b5e70bb19fdfefd19f1cab984c9709d22940e0e7d760407a1347.jpg)  
Figure 43 – Flexible Pressure Distribution for a 2.5g Steady Pull-Up at Mach 0.85, Altitude 26,468 feet.

Plots of internal loads for the aircraft configurations and flight conditions analyzed are shown for both wing and strut in Figure 44 and Figure 45. The loads plotted are limit loads for the $2 . 5 8$ and -1g maneuvers. Inboard of the strut-to-wing connection, the wing and the strut react with the external loads together, and the monitor points integrate internal loads to properly identify the loads in each component. The large effect of the strut in reducing wing shear and bending loads is evident as expected. The significant effect of engine inertia loads on wing internal loads is also noted. The large tension and compression forces in the strut due to symmetric maneuvering are quantified in the plot. Just as the strut reduces wing shear and bending loads, the jury strut reduces strut shear and bending loads. The inboard strut bending loads arise due to the curved shape of the strut in the vicinity of the fuselage attach.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 43 – Flexible Pressure Distribution for a 2.5g Steady Pull U。
![](images/e912d44aff50957dd57b215c98ebd0cc8149a318f33e86b4bcccd12f1f7bd3ea.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 44 – Wing Critical Design Load Selection.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 44 – Wing Critical Design Load Selection。
![](images/f24c6e1578817a40f714a3bc81c7fbfbbe1ae7507fffddc45b653363319c581a.jpg)  
Figure 45 – Strut Critical Design Load Selection.

To select a manageable subset of design load conditions for structural sizing optimization, a combinatorial optimization problem is solved. This process identifies the N load cases that best capture the minimum and maximum loads at each of the monitoring points along the wing and strut. As can be seen in Figure 46, a particular combination of four load conditions was selected

because it captured the minimum and maximum of all 123 component loads for all aircraft mass conditions, flight conditions, and maneuvers to within less than $5 \%$ .

图片摘要：该图主要展示 45 – Strut Critical Design Load Selection。
![](images/af47ba79921ef67b7eaeac5fe45f7a7d7e77fd86302b29813ea45552afafd0ba.jpg)  
Figure 46 – Structural Sizing Critical Design Load Selection.

The aeroelastic load conditions for the present study are a limited initial set of conditions, representing the critical loads for most of the wing and strut structure. They are useful for establishing the overall structural weight. They do not necessarily produce critical internal loads for every structural part, and a broader loads survey is required to further refine the weight estimate. This is especially true as more of the airframe is included in the sizing optimization. Antisymmetric load cases, maximum control surface deflections, ground loads, crash loads, fuel overpressure loads, and more should be checked as the vehicle design matures. Any of these load cases have the potential to drive the sizing of particular regions of the airframe.

# 3.2.11 Preliminary Structural Sizing and Optimization

The wing, strut, and jury structure was optimized using Nastran Solution 200 to minimize weight and meet strength and buckling constraints, for the four most critical 2.5g and -1g balanced maneuver conditions from the initial selection of critical loads. The maneuver loads were scaled by a factor of 1.5 to represent ultimate load conditions. Forces from the Nastran aeroelastic loads analysis were extracted and applied as static loads for the optimization. This approach provides faster optimization and more reliable convergence than the ideal approach of performing the loads analysis within the optimization loop. The approach of including the aeroelastic loads analysis in the optimization loop is ideal because it allows the optimizer to change the design to

favorably redistribute internal loads, and to favorably reduce external loads. However, the ideal approach increases the optimization time significantly (by a factor of ten, in some cases), and makes convergence unpredictable. This approach is best used when there is plenty of time to nurse the optimizer to a converged solution. When using static forces to represent external loads, the loads analysis should be updated after the structure is sized, and the process should be repeated to convergence. As of this writing, a second loads cycle has not been performed.

For the four most critical load conditions, a converged solution was found that met all strength and buckling constraints. Table 12 shows a summary of overall weight, comparing the present Phase IV results to the 2016 Mach 0.80 vehicle study, and to Phase II and Phase III results for the Mach 0.745 vehicle. All the results compared in the table are from sizing analyses that did not include flutter. Overall, the new weights are similar to previous studies, indicating that the vehicle design is likely to close and a feasible structure can be achieved. These results should be considered preliminary. To draw further conclusions, gust loads and flutter need to be included in the sizing considerations, and results need to be compared with prior studies that included flutter.

Table 12 – Structural Weights Sized for Strength and Buckling at Critical Maneuver Loads.   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2" colspan="2"></td><td colspan="2">Mach 0.745</td><td colspan="2">Mach 0.80</td></tr><tr><td>Phase II</td><td>Phase III</td><td>2016</td><td>Phase IV*</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">Wing</td><td>7420</td><td>7149</td><td>6782</td><td>5812</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Skins</td><td>5558</td><td>5439</td><td>5331</td><td>3662</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Spars</td><td>996</td><td>1095</td><td>671</td><td>767</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Webs</td><td>766</td><td>880</td><td>593</td><td>761</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Caps</td><td>230</td><td>215</td><td>78</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Ribs</td><td>866</td><td>615</td><td>780</td><td>466</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Webs</td><td>705</td><td>515</td><td>696</td><td>460</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Caps</td><td>161</td><td>100</td><td>84</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Leading Edge</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>916</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">Strut</td><td>787</td><td>1165</td><td>2578</td><td>2514</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Skins</td><td></td><td rowspan="4">914</td><td></td><td>1108</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Spars</td><td></td><td></td><td>110</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Ribs</td><td></td><td></td><td>101</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Edges</td><td></td><td></td><td>868</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Splitter</td><td></td><td>251</td><td></td><td>327</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">Jury</td><td>21</td><td>44</td><td>56</td><td>61</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Skins</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>38</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Spars</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Ribs</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>1</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Edges</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>20</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">TOTAL (Ibs)</td><td>8228</td><td>8358</td><td>9416</td><td>8386</td></tr></table>

* Sizing results from 6/14/2018. Should be considered preliminary.

The distribution of material in the optimized structure is illustrated by a plot of skin thickness in Figure 47. For this illustration, the stringer base and stringer web layers have been removed from the laminates, and the total thickness of only the skin plies has been plotted. The critical areas are where the strut connects to the wing, and the root of the wing and strut. Because the same scale is used for all components, the variations of thickness in the noncritical areas are obscured. The thickness plot is just an initial indication of material distribution.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 12 – Structural Weights Sized for Strength and Buckling at C。
![](images/7ceddc2aa47254de1f610fe105d17230402c4ebe53ec4c8c4f8db203e2ac9cdb.jpg)  
Figure 47 – Skin Thickness in Initial Optimized Structure.

An additional optimization run with different parameters indicated potential to reduce the weight of the structure, but more investigation is needed to understand if the solution is reasonable. In general, there is no way to guarantee a global minimum from the optimization process, and trying various optimization parameters (step sizes, convergence criteria, etc.), different starting designs, and different optimization sequences (such as sizing only the wing or strut before sizing the entire structure), can lead to different converged solutions. It is only after exploration of the design space that it becomes clear whether a particular design is among the best. Such exploration has not been done as of this writing.

A sizing optimization was attempted with all 30 critical load conditions from the maneuver loads survey, and it ran into cycle time and storage space issues. It is likely that optimizing for this many load conditions will need to be done a few conditions at a time.

The structural sizing from the initial optimization was used in the flutter assessment and gust loads analysis that are discussed next.

# 3.2.12 Initial Flutter Analysis

Flutter analysis was initially performed with the FEM wing, strut, and jury sized for the initial set of critical maneuver loads. The first step was a modal analysis (without aerodynamic effects). Modal analysis results for Mass Case 4 are given in Table 13. Because the TTBW configuration enables higher wing aspect ratio, the wing structural mode frequencies are low in general, and bending modes in both the normal and in-plane directions result. Among the list of flexible modes are several that are closely spaced in frequency, indicating a potential for the modes to coalesce under unsteady aerodynamic loading.

The six flight conditions selected for flutter analysis are shown on the flight envelope in Figure 48. At each condition, all five mass cases from Table 11 were evaluated for aeroelastic stability. The flutter analysis was performed using the Nastran Solution 145 p-k flutter method.

The results of the flutter analysis are summarized in Table 14. The reported flutter margins are based on modal damping of $\scriptstyle { \mathtt { g } } = 0 . 0 2$ , which is conservative based on decades of ground vibration test experience. At all flight conditions, and for all mass cases, adequate stability margins are predicted. Although the flight test required margin is 15 percent, at this early stage in the development and analysis of a new vehicle configuration, a margin of 25-30 percent is more appropriate.

The results of flutter analyses performed at the lower (below 0.92) Mach numbers are similar in the appearance of three critical flutter mechanisms, at roughly 3, 5, and 7 Hz. The 5 Hz mechanism is critical for mass configurations 1, 2, and 3. The 3 Hz mechanism is critical for mass configuration 5 and has slightly higher margins. The 7 Hz mechanism is critical for mass configuration 4 with yet higher margins. The results at 0.92 Mach are somewhat different, however. The 5 Hz mechanism is still critical for mass configurations 1, 2, and 3, but the $3 H z$ mechanism is now critical for mass configurations 4 and 5, with configuration 4 now producing the lowest margins.

Table 13 – Modal Analysis Results – Mass Case 4.

Detailed flutter analysis results for the lowest margin case are given in Figure 49, where the variation of damping and frequency versus airspeed is given for each of the airframe vibration modes included in the analysis. The frequency and airspeed stability margin are noted for the five most critical flutter mechanisms. The most critical mechanism becomes slightly unstable and then regains stability at higher airspeed while the next four mechanisms are each more violently unstable.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 14 – Initial Flutter Analysis Results。
![](images/e2effb22ba779ef1fc5b40867bc3d586ad78cfdc97a7a177c3a42fecb90e172e.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 14 – Initial Flutter Analysis Results。
![](images/b2348167d021d3f5404c43b7da6099eaeef554d2138ab0ff4f4a7f3165e6bc94.jpg)  
Figure 49 – Damping and Frequency Plots for Flutter Analysis at Mach 0.92, 26,000 feet, Mass Case 4.

Eigenvector analysis was performed on the critical mechanism to identify the constituent modes, and the results are given in Figure 50. Notable is the strong contribution of the rigid-body yaw mode (mode 5) and of multiple elastic modes (modes 7-13), which are both symmetric and antisymmetric, and yield a more complex flutter mechanism than the classical 2-mode bending-

torsion coupling. Mass and stiffness asymmetry built into the airframe FEM results in this case, in airframe vibration modes, which are not purely symmetric or antisymmetric, and thus an asymmetric flutter mechanism, a snapshot of which is given in Figure 51. The rigid-body motion of the fuselage is evident, and the motion of the right wing traces an ellipse as it moves both vertically and longitudinally.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 49 – Damping and Frequency Plots for Flutter Analysis at Mac。
![](images/9b952c9d7e6c3df5ca55c8562b1e3cdb635fac506afb56931628b0cffb23b9aa.jpg)  
Figure 50 – Critical Flutter Mechanism Eigenvector Analysis at Mach 0.92, 26,000 feet, Mass Case 4.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 50 – Critical Flutter Mechanism Eigenvector Analysis at Mach。
![](images/a1f18ce4a67f71fbbd42c2eac6d870c04d4b12667d4f6f61ddb1ca5424a8b11b.jpg)  
Figure 51 – Critical Flutter Mechanism at Mach 0.92, 26,000 feet, Mass Case 4.

These initial flutter analysis results suggested structural sizing for maneuver loads could also satisfy aeroelastic stability requirements, and this question was revisited as more comprehensive

and higher fidelity analyses were performed, the results of which are presented later in this report.

# 3.2.13 Gust Loads Analysis

A thorough investigation of gust loads was desired, as they are more likely significant on high aspect ratio wings, and had not been checked since the Phase II study, in which they were not found to be critical. Gust loads analysis began with the wing, strut, and jury sized for the initial set of critical 2.5g and -1g balanced maneuver conditions.

Gust loading is evaluated at critical conditions to capture the maximum vehicle response over the design flight envelope through tuned discrete gust (TDG) analysis in line with requirements of FAR 25.341. Analysis takes into account unsteady aerodynamic effects and all significant structural degrees of freedom contributing to the component loads of interest. Vertical gust loading is considered and the aircraft is assumed to encounter gusts of the shape defined by the following equation:

$$
U = \frac {U _ {d s}}{2} \left[ 1 - \operatorname {C o s} \left(\frac {\pi s}{H}\right) \right] \tag {1}
$$

$$
f o r 0 \leq s \leq 2 H
$$

where: $\mathsf { s } = \mathsf { g u s t }$ penetration distance in feet

$U _ { d s } =$ positive or negative design gust velocity in feet/second equivalent airspeed $H =$ the gust gradient, which is the distance in feet parallel to the airplane's flight path for the gust to reach its peak velocity

The critical dynamic response for each component load monitored is determined by evaluating gust gradient distances from 30 feet to 350 feet. This is done to ensure the gust wavelength producing maximum dynamic load is captured. Based on prior experience, 11 gradient lengths of 30, 35, 40, 50, 75, 100, 150, 200, 250, 300, and 350 feet were chosen to fully encompass potential vehicle response. Typical gust shapes are shown in Figure 52.

The design gust velocities are defined by the expression:

$$
U _ {d s} = U _ {r e f} F _ {g} (H / 3 5 0) ^ {1 / 6} \tag {2}
$$

where: $\begin{array} { r } { U _ { r e f } = } \end{array}$ the reference gust velocity in equivalent airspeed

$F _ { g } =$ the flight profile alleviation factor

Additionally, $U _ { r e f }$ for positive and negative gusts is typically interpolated linearly from Table 15, which shows reference gust velocities for level flight maximum speed $( \mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { H } } )$ and limit speed $( \mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { L } } )$ . At $\mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { L } } ,$ the gust velocities are reduced by half compared to their values at $\mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { H } }$ . Whether critical gust loads are produced at $\mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { H } }$ or $V _ { \mathrm { L } }$ depends on the relative positions of $\mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { H } }$ and $V _ { \mathsf { L } }$ on the flight

envelope of the aircraft. For the TTBW aircraft, the operational speed is taken as $\mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { H } }$ and the max operational speed as VL. As these lines are relatively close together, $\mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { H } }$ has been selected for the critical gust conditions. The reduced gust velocities at $\mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { L } } ,$ at which dynamic pressures would increase only about five percent, are unlikely to produce larger dynamic response than analysis along the operational envelope at an unscaled reference gust velocity. This approach has been verified on previous programs with similar flight envelopes.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 52 – Typical Gust Shapes。
![](images/78f0dafade85e24218535fd97871f1db0c67e4c07d4df0331f199695eb6a8cdd.jpg)  
Figure 52 – Typical Gust Shapes.

Table 15 – Tuned Discrete Gust Reference Velocity as a Function of Altitude.   

<table><tr><td>Altitude (ft)</td><td colspan="2">Uref (feet/second equivalent airspeed)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>VH</td><td>VL</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>56.0</td><td>28.0</td></tr><tr><td>15,000</td><td>44.0</td><td>22.0</td></tr><tr><td>50,000</td><td>26.0</td><td>13.0</td></tr></table>

The flight profile alleviation factor, $F _ { g } ,$ varies linearly from the value at sea level to a value of 1.0 at the maximum operating altitude of 50,000 feet. The sea level value of $F _ { g }$ is determined by the following equation:

$$
F _ {g} = \frac {1}{2} \left(F _ {g x} + F _ {g m}\right) \tag {3}
$$

where:

$$
F _ {g x} = 1 - \frac {Z _ {m 0}}{2 5 0 0 0 0}; F _ {g m} = \sqrt {R _ {2} T a n \left(\frac {\pi R _ {1}}{4}\right)} \tag {4}
$$

$$
R _ {1} = \frac {\text {M a x i m u m L a n d i n g W e i g h t}}{\text {M a x i m u m T a k e o f f W e i g h t}} \tag {5}
$$

$$
R _ {2} = \frac {\text {M a x i m u m Z e r o F u e l W e i g h t}}{\text {M a x i m u m T a k e o f f W e i g h t}} \tag {6}
$$

For the TTBW aircraft: $Z _ { m o } =$ Maximum operating altitude $= 5 0 { , } 0 0 0$ feet

Maximum Landing Weight = 145,000 lb

Maximum Takeoff Weight $= 1 4 5 , 0 0 0$ lb

Maximum Zero-Fuel Weight $=$ 115,972 lb

Two weight configurations were selected for analysis to cover the effects of both wing loading and inertia relief: maximum payload with fuel to reach MTOW (Mass Case 1 from Table 11) and maximum payload with reserve fuel (Mass Case 4). Also, six points in the sky (PITS), were selected for evaluation, as shown in Figure 53.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 53 – Tuned Discrete Gust Analysis Points in the Sky。
![](images/af001d63eebc87e243f3bce0f86c6b256edc027baf9f83f30b0707cbeb5f6cc5.jpg)  
Figure 53 – Tuned Discrete Gust Analysis Points in the Sky.

Transient aeroelastic gust response analysis was performed in MSC Nastran and loading was tracked along the wing and strut at reference stations through monitored load sets (Nastran

MONPNT entries). Both positive and negative gust conditions were considered and superimposed with static loading corresponding to trimmed level flight. Component loads at each time point from these analyses were surveyed and considered for use in structural sizing.

The millions of potential load conditions associated with individual time points of the tuned discrete gust loads analyses were first filtered according to whether they produced a minimum or maximum component load. These results were then combined with critical maneuver load conditions and a combinatorial optimization problem was solved to identify the N conditions that would most closely approach the minimum and maximum component loads at all reference stations along the wing and strut, as shown in Figure 54. Nine gust conditions (all at sea level, four at Mass Case 1, and five at Mass Case 4) and two maneuver conditions were selected for use in structural sizing. For the gust conditions, balanced FEM load distributions were generated using inertia loads from modal accelerations associated with the critical gust response time point.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 53 – Tuned Discrete Gust Analysis Points in the Sky。
![](images/ee364d5a971bcf005ea4cf0900b60541f6760e723bc3f12df54b3f0267ed025c.jpg)  
Figure 54 – Design Load Case Selection.

A skyline comparison of critical maneuver and gust loads is given in Figure 55. While the maneuver shear and bending loads are higher over portions of the outer wing, the gust loads are

dominant over most of the inboard wing. Overall, gust loads are more severe, and this is why more of the selected sizing conditions came from the gust loads analyses.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 54 – Design Load Case Selection。
![](images/daff5b36c70897ce09be94ad8d9d9db34b48a2f1cb1b3c1ece720bdf1bfb2037.jpg)  
Figure 55 – Design Flight Loads Comparison.

# 3.2.14 Structural Sizing Optimization

The gust loads analysis resulted in a new set of critical load conditions for structural optimization, consisting of nine gust cases and two maneuver cases. The two maneuver cases were a pair of 2.5g and -1g maneuvers for Mass Case 2, full fuel with payload to reach MTOW. This pair was previously used in the preliminary structural optimization, along with another pair for Mass Case 5. Instead of the other pair of maneuver cases, the new set of critical loads included the nine gust cases. Forces from the Nastran aeroelastic loads analyses were extracted and applied as static loads for optimization with Nastran Solution 200. For optimization, both the gust loads and the maneuver loads were scaled by a factor of 1.5 to represent ultimate load conditions. As before, the wing, strut, and jury structure was optimized to minimize weight and meet strength and buckling constraints.

During the preliminary round of optimization, a set of optimization parameters was found that resulted in a lighter weight design. This set of parameters was used for the new optimization runs. With the number of load conditions for the optimization increased from four to 11, the run time increased from less than two hours per design cycle to nearly eight hours per cycle. Fortunately, the runs converged in six to eight cycles, and feasible designs were obtained. Runs

that started from different initial designs converged to optimum designs having weights within 100 pounds of each other, or less than one percent apart. This result gave confidence that the optimization was working properly.

Table 16 compares the structural weight breakdown from the initial optimization for maneuver loads only (see Table 12), to the updated optimization for maneuver and gust loads. The fact that the total wing weight decreased from 8,386 to 8,300 pounds, despite the addition of gust loads that were critical for the inboard wing, is probably due to the improved parameters providing better optimizer performance. It is noticeable that the wing skins got heavier and the strut got lighter with the new set of critical loads, which is consistent with the added gust loads being critical for the inboard wing.

Table 16 – Structural Weights Sized for Strength and Buckling at Critical Gust and Maneuver Loads.   

<table><tr><td rowspan="3" colspan="2"></td><td colspan="2">Mach 0.80</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">Phase IV*</td></tr><tr><td>Maneuver</td><td>Maneuver and Gust</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">Wing</td><td>5812</td><td>5917</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Skins</td><td>3662</td><td>3854</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Spars</td><td>767</td><td>702</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Webs</td><td>761</td><td>696</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Caps</td><td>6</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Ribs</td><td>466</td><td>473</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Webs</td><td>460</td><td>466</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Caps</td><td>6</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Leading Edge</td><td>916</td><td>889</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">Strut</td><td>2514</td><td>2323</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Skins</td><td>1108</td><td>959</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Spars</td><td>110</td><td>108</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Ribs</td><td>101</td><td>93</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Edges</td><td>868</td><td>837</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Splitter</td><td>327</td><td>327</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">Jury</td><td>61</td><td>60</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Skins</td><td>38</td><td>37</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Spars</td><td>2</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Ribs</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Edges</td><td>20</td><td>20</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">TOTAL (Ibs)</td><td>8386</td><td>8300</td></tr></table>

* Sizing results from 6/14/2018 and 8/28/2018 should be considered preliminary.

The distribution of material in the optimized structure is illustrated by a plot of skin thickness in Figure 56, which is comparable to Figure 47. The distribution from the new optimization closely resembles the previous one. Some increases in thickness are noticeable on the lower wing skin

where the engine and strut are attached. In other areas, the weight gain of the inboard wing skins that was shown in Table 16 probably corresponds to thickness increases that are within the resolution of the scale of the plot, and therefore appear the same in the two figures. The weight reduction in the strut can be observed by comparing the new plot to the previous one.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 56 – Skin Thickness in Optimized Structure。
![](images/8f158e982c0b3e863a9284a8328033655bcfc22160b9f5af527c397b77fdfab2.jpg)  
Figure 56 – Skin Thickness in Optimized Structure.

The thickness plot is just an initial indication of material distribution. In future rounds of optimization, the critical constraint for each design zone should be checked as well, to identify the critical load case and failure mode. The running loads at interfaces, such as the wing to strut connection, are also important to check, relative to the load limits of joint designs. Many refinements to the structural definition, load conditions, and optimization process are possible. For the present effort, the purpose was to provide an updated weight for the wing and strut, so further refinements were not pursued.

The structural sizing from the updated optimization was used in subsequent aeroelastic analyses, which are discussed next.

# 3.2.15 Aerodynamic Adjustments

In order to improve flutter analysis fidelity, aerodynamic adjustments based on CFD analyses were developed and applied to the unsteady aerodynamic wing loads predicted by the doublet lattice model. The improved analysis enabled a more accurate capture of compressibility effects and flow separation that can have a significant effect on overall spanload (defined herein as $\sf C _ { \sf I } ^ { \sf * } c / c _ { \sf r e f } )$ . This is particularly important in regions of the flight envelope where these factors play an increasingly important role in determining accurate estimates of aerodynamic loads. An outline of the process used is as follows:

1) For a given flight condition, calculate DLM wing loads for unit angle-of-attack (angle of attack)   
2) Calculate strip normal force and pitching moment coefficients from DLM wing loads   
3) Perform CFD analysis at the same flight condition at two selected values of angle of attack   
4) Calculate strip normal force and pitching moment per unit angle of attack coefficients from CFD loads   
5) Identify DLM strip load adjustment factors by comparing DLM and CFD results   
6) Resolve DLM strip load adjustment factors into DLM box load adjustment factors

An example of the CFD analyses mentioned in Step 3 is shown in Figure 57 and A3. The difference in the solutions from two different angle of attacks is used to estimate the sensitivity of loads to angle of attack variation.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 57 – Aerodynamic Pressures from CFD Analysis (M=0.85, CL=0.2。
![](images/6f72d3e130518749b0934952c9203cceb4b21e5dc0c39a287a13303fca4faca8.jpg)  
Figure 57 – Aerodynamic Pressures from CFD Analysis (M=0.85, CL=0.264, Remac = 25.0M).

A complete list of all CFD analyses performed for development of DLM aero adjustments for loads and dynamics analyses is given in Table 17. Cases 1-12 were generated for loads analysis aero adjustment development, and the angle of attack increment between these pairs of solutions at each flight condition is larger, roughly consistent with the load factor range to be evaluated with the design maneuvers selected. Cases 13-20 were generated for flutter analysis aero adjustment development, and are relevant to the present discussion. Note the angle of attack increment between these solution pairs at each flight condition is smaller, as flutter is essentially a small perturbation problem.

Because the CFD analyses were performed on a multizone overlapping grid resulting in nonunique surface load distributions, some post-processing of the solutions was required. Surface pressures from the Overflow structured overlapping solution were mapped to the unstructured grid shown in Figure 58 and then converted into force vectors for integration into strip load coefficients for comparison with DLM results.

Table 17 – CFD Analysis Conditions.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 17 – CFD Analysis Conditions。
![](images/c5abd2389098e9686c49a3fc082ceca757156f571947ecc40defe7f437944cda.jpg)  
Figure 58 – CFD Results Processing.

DLM aerodynamic adjustment development results are presented herein for two of the four Mach numbers evaluated. The results given for Mach 0.76 are generally representative of those obtained at Mach numbers 0.59 and 0.85, while those given for Mach 0.92 will be shown dissimilar to those at the three other Mach numbers.

Wing pressure contours for the Mach 0.76 flight condition are shown in Figures 59 through 61 and Figures A4 though A6 for DLM and CFD analyses, and the integrated CFD strip load coefficients are given in Figure 62. The aerodynamic interference effect of the strut and the nacelle/pylon are evident in both the strip normal force and pitching moment distributions. In addition, the strip normal force is more sensitive to angle of attack variation than is the strip pitching moment. DLM and CFD strip load coefficients per degree angle of attack are compared in Figure 63. The DLM and CFD strip load distributions are of similar shape, with the CFD results again more clearly showing the aerodynamic interference effects of the strut and nacelle/pylon. The CFD strip normal force per degree angle of attack is greater than that predicted by the DLM, and this result is consistent with similar comparisons made previously on other aircraft programs. The strip pitching moments per degree angle of attack are small, with the DLM results more positive than those from CFD analysis.

The analysis results at the Mach 0.92 flight condition are markedly different from those at the three lower Mach numbers. Wing pressure contours are shown in Figures 64 through 66 and Figures A7 through A9 for DLM and CFD analyses. The integrated CFD strip load coefficients are given in Figure 67. Note the DLM pressures vary smoothly and are higher in value than those at Mach 0.76. The CFD pressures at both angle of attack conditions, however, exhibit abrupt changes on both upper and lower surfaces of the outer wing. The aerodynamic interference effect of the strut in particular is more dramatically evident in the strip normal force distribution. Note the wing, outboard of the strut juncture, is generating negative lift at both angle of attack conditions. DLM and CFD strip load coefficients per degree angle of attack are compared in Figure 68. The DLM and CFD strip load distributions clearly are no longer of similar shape. The DLM normal force per degree angle of attack is now larger than that predicted by CFD, and the CFD result drops sharply over the outer wing, where increasing angle of attack actually results in loss of lift in the 845-940 butt line region. The CFD results also predict a sudden increase in pitching moment due to angle of attack over the outer wing.

CFD section cut pressures are shown in Figure 69 and appendix Figure A.10 for both the Mach 0.76 and 0.92 flight conditions at equivalent lift. The sharp pressure drops at roughly $7 5 \%$ chord on both upper and lower surfaces of the outboard wing at the higher Mach number flight condition are indicative of shock formation. The difference between the pressure distributions at the two Mach 0.92 angle of attack conditions as shown in Figure 70 and appendix Figure A.11 suggest the outer wing strip loads due to angle of attack are heavily driven by shock motion. Looking at the butt line 886 section cut pressure distributions of Figure 71 and appendix Figure A.12, we see the upper surface shock moves forward and the lower surface shock moves aft as angle of attack increases. The net effect of this shock motion is to increase lift somewhat over the forward $62 \%$ of the airfoil and to decrease lift much more over the aft $38 \%$ . This is the physical mechanism by which an angle of attack increase results in both lift loss and pitching moment gain for the outer wing at the Mach 0.92 flight condition. Due to the strong nonlinear flow features present at Mach 0.92, the DLM aerodynamic adjustment methodology employed here is not capable of improving analysis fidelity at this flight condition. This consideration will be discussed further in the next section.

Figure 71 – Delta Pressure Distributions between α 0.795° and 0.495° at Station 886 at Mach 0.92, $\mathsf { R e } _ { \mathsf { m a c } } = 2 7 . 5 \mathsf { M }$ .

As outlined at the beginning of this section, the last step in the aerodynamic adjustment process is to resolve DLM strip load adjustment factors into DLM box load adjustment factors. This is done to modify raw DLM predictions to match CFD results for strip normal force and pitching moment per unit angle of attack. Since there are typically more than two DLM boxes per strip, this is mathematically an underspecified problem, and the mimimum-norm least-squares solution is employed. Results are given for two strips at the Mach 0.76 flight condition in Figure 72. Note the box load adjustment factors are reasonable in both magnitude and distribution shape, and result in reasonable modification to the raw DLM pressure prediction. Results are given for the same two strips at the Mach 0.92 flight condition in Figure 73. The box load adjustment factors are no longer reasonable, with some actually being negative, and the resulting modification to the raw DLM pressure predictions is highly suspect. To resolve this discrepancy, future analysis should examine the use of unsteady RANS codes as a means for resolving the effects of unsteady shock-induced separation and its ssociated unsteadiness.

# 3.2.16 Flutter Analysis

The DLM box load adjustment factors calculated were implemented in MSC/Nastran WKK format and the initial flutter analysis described in Section 3.2.12 was rerun. A basic assumption of this approach is that the aerodynamic adjustments calculated based on comparison of steady aerodynamic results may also be applied to unsteady DLM predictions at all reduced frequencies.

Results from flutter analyses performed both with and without aerodynamic adjustments are summarized for the three lower Mach number flight conditions in Table 18. Note first that in the majority of cases, the aero adjustments do not change the critical flutter mechanism. Furthermore, at Mach 0.59 and 0.76, the aero adjustments result in lower margins for the 5 Hz mechanism, but higher margins for the more critical 3 Hz mechanism. At Mach 0.85, the adjustments result in higher margins for both mechanisms. Overall, the aero adjustments raise the minimum aeroelastic stability margins by $10 \%$ for analyses conducted at the three lower Mach numbers of 0.59, 0.76, and 0.85.

Results from flutter analyses performed both with and without aerodynamic adjustments are summarized for the Mach 0.92 flight condition in Table 19. Clearly, the aero adjustments have completely changed the character of the analysis results with the introduction of multiple low and high frequency mechanisms, many of which have negative stability margins. The detailed flutter analysis results of Figures 74 and 75 support this conclusion. At Mach 0.76, the adjustments result in reasonable modifications to modal frequency and damping versus airspeed,

while at Mach 0.92, the raw and adjusted results differ significantly due to the inherent unsteadiness of the flow at this condition.

In conclusion, aeroelastic stability analyses performed with CFD-based DLM adjustments at flight conditions up through 0.85 Mach predict healthy airspeed margins, the minimum being $3 5 \%$ for the max payoad plus MTOW fuel configuration at the Mach 0.76 13,200 ft flight condition. Because the 0.92 Mach dive speed flight condition is well beyond the 0.80 wing aerodynamic design cruise Mach number, strong shocks on both upper and lower outer wing surfaces are predicted. The flutter analysis with raw DLM unsteady aerodynamics is therefore of limited value, and the CFD-based aerodynamic adjustment method employed herein is unable to improve flutter analysis fidelity at this transonic flight condition. A flutter analysis with the unsteady aerodynamic modal loads predicted with CFD directly is required to do this, but is beyond the scope of the work described herein.

Table 18 – Flutter Summary – Mach 0.59 to 0.85.
