# 3.2.17 Aeroelastic Analysis Conclusions

Loads and dynamics analyses performed thus far suggest the TTBW aircraft configuration is feasible. Due to the availability of geometry at the time of analysis, the aeroelastic FEM was updated to the 1104-001-RC configuration and used to generate a set of airframe structural design loads corresponding to critical maneuver and gust conditions. Structural sizing optimization using these design loads was performed and yielded reasonable wing and strut weights in line with the predictions of previous TTBW development work. Aeroelastic stability analyses incorporating CFD-based aerodynamic adjustments suggest healthy flutter margins at Mach numbers up through 0.85. This investigation should be revisited in a subsequent analysis to ensure that changes made to the final configuration (1104-001-RG ) are still valid.

To complete this round of analysis with the current aeroelastic FEM, further work is recommended. As discussed at some length, the flutter analysis performed to date at the 0.92 Mach flight conditions is incapable of capturing for the nonlinear flow features predicted. To more confidently assess stability margins at this transonic flight condition, a flutter analysis employing CFD calculation of unsteady aerodynamic forces directly is required. In addition, because the TTBW configuration results in a redundant wing load path unlike that of a catilevered wing, flutter analysis should be performed with prestressed modes and potentially about a large deformation operating state. This has been investigated in the past, and should be revisited for the current TTBW configuration. Lastly, maneuver and gust loads analyses should be updated with CFD-based aerodynamic adjustments as was done for flutter analyses.

It bears noting that both the Mach 0.745 analyses and the current Mach 0.80 analyses are limited in scope. They are focused on the wing, strut, and jury structures only, use preliminary flight design load conditions, and size the structure to generic criteria. The structural layouts and constraints were created by structural analysts, with limited structural design development. To continue maturing the TTBW airframe, future work should include the following tasks:

Design development to define structural concepts, manufacturing limits, and structural layouts of airframe components.   
Inclusion of more of the airframe in the aeroelastic sizing, particularly the gear pylon. This effort addresses the risk of unexpected weight increases late in airframe development, when key components are finally analyzed.   
Definition of more detailed airframe requirements and sizing criteria, and incorporation of these criteria into the sizing optimization. This effort addresses the risk of not knowing the weight impacts of criteria that may be unique to the TTBW airframe.   
• Addition of more load conditions in the aeroelastic analysis, such as antisymmetric maneuvers, maximum control surface deflections, ground loads, crash loads, fuel

NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report

overpressure loads, and flap torsion loads. This work addresses the risk of missing critical conditions, and therefore the weight, of specific portions of the airframe.

# 4.0 Aerodynamic Design

# 4.1 High-Speed Aerodynamic Design

This section outlines the design process and results of a high-fidelity study aimed at maturing the TTBW concept for operation at a higher cruise Mach number than previously studied. Updating the design from the $\mathsf { M } = 0 . 7 4 5$ design (created in SUGAR Phase III) to a cruise Mach number of $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ will enhance the commercial viability of a potential product by enabling an increase in fleet productivity. The primary design objectives of this study are consistent with the objectives from the Phase III aerodynamic design effort, such as efficient cruise operation, and minimizing wing-strut interference. However, the design effort also sought to increase the scope of the vehicle-level design considerations as compared to previous studies by tracking the implications of high-speed design changes on low-speed (high-lift) performance. This included items such as: low-speed (clean) aircraft performance, high-lift system compatibility, and the inclusion of flap hinge fairings into the high-speed design.

In addition to the similarities in design objectives, the $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ design study also shares many of the same constraints as the $\mathsf { M } = 0 . 7 4 5$ design study. The configuration layout was frozen at the start of the study – wing sweep and planform remained unchanged throughout the design effort. The engine nacelle and pylon were modeled as a flow-through geometry, so power effects were not considered. Also, aeroelastic twist as a function of flight condition was not changed – rather, the geometry represents an ideal $\prime _ { 1 } { \sf G } ^ { \prime }$ geometry, which was used to determine the performance potential of a TTBW aircraft operating at transonic Mach.

Following the completion of a revised high-speed design, the geometry developed in this phase of work will be tested and validated in a transonic wind tunnel test at the NASA Ames 11-ft TWT beginning in January of 2019.

# 4.1.1 Design methodology

The design methodology used for the development of the $\mathsf { M } { = } 0 . 8$ high-speed lines is based on the experiences and lessons learned during the previous phases of the SUGAR program. Reliable tools and processes were used in the same capacity as previous design efforts, while tool improvements were made where a high return on investment was possible. For example, the 2D aerostructural strut optimization procedure used in Phase III was updated to a 3D procedure using the TRANAIR design tool. The new procedure allowed for design optimization across the wing and strut span (rather than at discrete 2D locations) and introduced 3D flow interactions into the optimization that were previously unaccounted. The results from the modified design tool was then validated in the RANS CFD code OVERFLOW to ensure design viability.

# Design and Analysis toolset

Various software tools were used in the design, development, and analysis of the SUGAR TTBW concept. Key tools included but were not limited to FLO-22, CDISC, TRANAIR, OVERFLOW and BUFFET. Each tool is briefly described below with an explanation as to how it was used for the detailed SUGAR design:

FLO-22 (6) is a full potential, 3D transonic flow code used for the aerodynamic analysis of wings modeled out of a wall. The code captures body effects by mathematically modeling the change in upwash as a function of span. It was used for initial wing design, wingspanload evaluation and preliminary dragrise estimation of the SUGAR TTBW wing design.   
The TRANAIR (7) tool consists of a nonlinear, full potential compressible flow Newton solver with fully coupled 2D integral boundary layer (IBL) models for aerodynamic analysis at subsonic, transonic and supersonic freestream Mach numbers. Aeroelastic and powered propulsion models offer additional capabilities, as well as transpiration models to represent boundary layer displacement and design modifications. Structured surface grids represent the discretized model geometry (along with wakes) and output-based Cartesian mesh adaptation is used to generate the volume domain, as illustrated in Figure 76. A design mode is also available to model various surface modifications, such as twist, camber and thickness on lifting surfaces, dictated by a gradient-based optimizer. Numerous objective functions and inequality constraints are available for design across multiple design conditions that involve geometry assessments, solution features and functionals of various aerodynamic quantities.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 76 – TRANAIR surface and volume discretization。
![](images/d54c7a195991dc4a5e30f0cae2bce3138696882cf564c84f9426b3f8d46423a0.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 76 – TRANAIR surface and volume discretization。
![](images/d512af1789b553f5a27f6f565bfb69d545907962827273a27b90796e20fd69a6.jpg)  
Figure 76 – TRANAIR surface and volume discretization.

• CDISC (8), which stands for Constrained Direct Iterative Surface Curvature, is an inverse design tool developed at NASA Langley for 2D or 3D configurations. This tool adjusts the geometry such that the resulting pressure distribution matches the distribution specified

by the designer. CDISC was used to reduce shock strength by smoothing pressure distributions throughout the SUGAR TTBW design.

• OVERFLOW (9) is a node-based RANS code specifically designed for structured, overset grid systems. It is capable of computing steady or unsteady flow about an arbitrary body across a wide range of Mach numbers using a number of different approaches. Depending on the type of simulation, the solver can be run in 2D or 3D, thin-layer or full Navier-Stokes, central or upwind differencing, static or moving body. OVERFLOW 2.2l was used to solve for the full 3D viscous solution over the entire SUGAR TTBW configuration and was the primary tool used to estimate the aerodynamic performance characteristics. A consistent solver setup was maintained throughout the project with the Spalart-Allmaras one-equation turbulence model, the Quadratic Constitutive Relation (QCR) and $5 ^ { \mathrm { t h } }$ -order HLLE $^ { + + }$ /SSOR differencing schemes employed.

• BUFFET is a Boeing-proprietary code that estimates a configuration’s sensitivity to buffet by computing shock strength and position and comparing it against a database of 2D high Reynolds number data from conventional and supercritical airfoil tests. The method provides an indication of when shock-induced separation leads to buffet onset. The BUFFET program has been historically shown to be quite accurate for predicting buffet onset for T-tailed twinjet transport designs and was used to assess buffet characteristics for the TTBW wing and strut.

# CFD grid generation and post-processing

CAD geometry was provided for grid generation using NX (Unigraphics). MADCAP, a Boeing proprietary grid generation code, and the Ansys ICEM CFD meshing software package were used to import the CAD surface definition and generate the necessary surface grids. Additional surface grid generation, volume grid generation and OVERFLOW input file creation was accomplished using scripts contained within NASA’s Chimera Grid Toolset (CGT) (10). The CGT script BuildVol was used in conjunction with required input files to build all of the volume grids using one of two hyperbolic grid generators: HYPGEN (11) or LEGRID. All grids were built within a common model directory, which was broken up into subdirectories that were tied to the major components of the configuration. This use of the CGT script system facilitated configuration control throughout the SUGAR design process and provided a clear history of how each grid was built.

After the volume grids were built for each configuration, the BuildPeg5i CGT script was used to build a configuration-specific input deck for Pegasus5 (12). Pegasus5 was used to cut holes in grids that penetrate solid walls as well as to establish communication between the individual volume grids. This is a step in the process known as domain or grid connectivity. Next, a force and moment integration surface was created using an internal Boeing code called POLYMIXSUR. This program eliminates grid overlap on the surface and connects neighboring zones with zipper

grids comprised of triangles. POLYMIXSUR produces a closed integration surface used to compute forces and moments. Finally, the BuildOveri CGT script was used to create a configuration-specific input deck for OVERFLOW.

Both Tecplot and Fieldview were used for the generation of flow visualization images from the OVERFLOW results. A Boeing internal tcl script system facilitated the efficient generation of a common set of images across the entire polar. Common images were collected inside PowerPoint presentations and were viewed in flipbook mode in order to understand how the flow field changes as a function of angle-of-attack. These images helped to qualitatively explain the behavior of quantitative results and showed the designer where modifications were required to improve the design.

# Design Optimization using TRANAIR

TRANAIR was used in several different modes during the design optimization process. The first mode was a purely aerodynamic-focused multipoint design optimization, while the second mode consisted of a detailed aerostructural design optimization. The latter mode was used to ensure that the aerodynamic loft had a viable structural solution and corresponding weight, and was used to investigate various general modifications to the strut structure as well as jury strut location.

# Aerodynamic Optimization with TRANAIR

As the wing and strut lofts evolved throughout the SUGAR Phase IV tasks to improve aerodynamic performance, automated aerodynamic shape optimization was conducted using the TRANAIR tool to identify how additional performance improvements could be achieved. The goal for this effort was to increase maximum L/D at the vehicle cruise condition and ensure operability across the flight envelope. In addition, various constraints were included through the optimization process to address concerns over manufacturability.

A baseline Mach 0.80 wing-body-strut configuration resembling the updated Phase III model was provided as a seed for aerodynamic shape optimization. TRANAIR analysis settings for this model were determined using best practices and information from a grid refinement study. Small regions of complex geometry were not modeled with IBL to facilitate solver convergence to machine-zero, thus absolute drag measurements contained a small offset compared to 3D RANS simulations of the seed geometry. Nonetheless, performance improvements obtained in TRANAIR design translated into similar increment values when optimized geometry was analyzed with OVERFLOW.

The seed model was parameterized with surface deformation modes that accomplished both chordwise and spanwise camber, twist and thickness changes to the seed wing and strut grids. Only camber and twist deformation modes were used in the aerodynamic shape optimization

cases. The strut attachment location remained at the same wing spanwise station and was modified by the deformation modes while remaining attached to the wing. Both the wing and strut planforms did not change throughout the optimization studies. Instead of morphing the analysis grids, the designed deformation modes were transformed into transpiration boundary condition updates that affect the local flow field as if a new grid were used. Conservative bounds were chosen for the deformation modes to ensure transpiration errors did not accumulate substantially during an optimization case. A new loft of optimized geometry was generated for analysis to confirm transpiration errors were not exploited by the optimizer.

Gradient-based optimization was initially conducted at the design condition with fixed-lift coefficient. Numerous weighted combinations of drag functionals and boundary layer parameters were explored as the minimization objective to understand different objective spaces. The impact of constraining geometry or boundary layer parameters on the wing and strut were also studied. Multiple parameterization setups were also studied to understand the effect of twist, camber and thickness changes on the design trajectory taken by the

图片摘要：该图主要展示 77 – Typical drag improvement seen in early design iteration。

Figure 77 – Typical drag improvement seen in early design iterations for a single-point optimization.

optimizer. Figure 77 shows a typical drag history in the early design iterations taken by the optimizer. As expected, different levels of drag improvement were obtained depending on the problem setup. Close inspections of the resulting wing and strut pressure profiles and new deformation modes (Figure 78 and Appendix Figure A.13), as well as spanload and sectional lift distribution (Figure 79), were executed to understand how further drag improvements could be included in the CAD model.

Multipoint optimization followed with an updated seed strut planform and the lessons-learned from design space studies with single-point optimization. With the design condition unchanged, various Mach and lift-coefficient pairs were added to the design problem across three, four or five total design conditions. Each condition contained a corresponding lift-coefficient constraint with angle of attack varying independently to satisfy it. The overall minimization objective was also defined in multiple ways to reflect different weightings of drag functionals in each design condition. Boundary layer parameters were investigated for some conditions as well, either as part of the objective function or as a constraint, in addition to surface geometry constraints. Figure 80 illustrates a drag history seen over 15 iterations for the last case influencing the highspeed design. Figure 81 shows a comparison of seed and optimized geometry for that case. As with the single-point optimization cases, inspections of the resulting wing and strut pressure profiles and new deformation modes (Figure 82 and Appendix Figure A.14), as well as spanload and lift distribution (Figure 83), were done to understand how further drag improvements could be included in the CAD model.

# Aerostructural Optimization with TRANAIR

The TRANAIR tool suite was augmented with a low-fidelity structural sizing capability to conduct trade studies between aerodynamic drag and structural weight. The structural model consisted of airfoil-conforming spars and skin thickness components of arbitrary material properties. This simple structural box definition was automated within 2D airfoil slices at user-specified spanwise stations along the wing and strut planforms, as shown in Figure 84. With the addition of thickness deformation modes to the camber and twist modes in the design variable vector, the box definitions were sized with an inner-loop gradient-based optimization for each outer mold-line design iteration selected by the outer-loop aerodynamic optimizer. The fast structural optimizer minimized the local box weight by changing its component dimensions and enforcing local stiffness constraints for EI and GJ, as well as minimum and maximum gage limits. The spanwise distribution of EI and GJ constraints were inputs extracted from a high-fidelity FEM sizing study. The drag minimization objective functional used by the aerodynamic optimizer was penalized by a converted weight estimate for the combined wing and strut structural boxes. An additional penalty was appended for any spanwise stations where the structural box optimization was unable to meet stiffness constraints. Typically this occurred when the aerodynamics optimizer reduced airfoil thickness excessively. In this manner, a compromise between aerodynamic performance and weight could be studied, as depicted in the typical drag and feasibility history shown in Figure 85.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 83 – Comparison of seed and optimized spanload and lift dist。
![](images/b03bfc42ce703e959917a2515a3ab0076c49b7b39897fd716fa678d6c67ca3da.jpg)  
Figure 84 – Spanwise stations where structural box sizing was conducted during optimization.

Single-point aerodynamic optimization was conducted with the structural sizing inner-loop for a variety of minimization objective definitions that included structural penalty functions. The impact of different geometric and structural feasibility constraints were also investigated. General optimizer behavior included thickness increases in span locations where structural feasibility could become an issue, and thickness decreases in other locations where the stiffness constraints were less stringent and drag benefits could be obtained, as shown in Figure 86.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 84 – Spanwise stations where structural box sizing was condu。
![](images/a763839127f8ea71b1a418a728784f06fe73e9cd3c7648dddb27af4627de5f50.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 84 – Spanwise stations where structural box sizing was condu。
![](images/f617021107213bee69a9d1732d27c2f037957786c60362231450c6529e7b4030.jpg)  
Figure 85 – Typical aero-structural optimization results showing drag improvements and structural feasibility maintained for a single-point case.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 85 – Typical aero structural optimization results showing dr。
![](images/def02a03dfa8939cae8dc7e7fc7ea7171b5f07ee459703e8fa8136f07cbc8ff8.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 85 – Typical aero structural optimization results showing dr。
![](images/3e3349a051d37c2632543d2ef94dcd94418d51079b8aa50b7b0e7dc47f8d5687.jpg)

# 4.1.2 Wing-Strut Design Overview

This section outlines the design progression from the starting $\mathsf { M } { = } 0 . 8$ conceptual geometry developed during Phase III up to the finalized 1104-001-RG high-speed lines.

# Configuration 1104-001-NEW

The design of a $\mathsf { M } { = } 0 . 8$ variant of the TTBW concept began during SUGAR Phase III. In this phase of the program, the vehicle underwent a change from the $\mathsf { M } { = } 0 . 7 4 5$ design, and was adjusted using conceptual design methods. These methods adjusted the design by modifying wing sweep and thickness according to simple sweep theory.

# Configuration 1104-001-RA – Conceptual Design

The –RA configuration was analyzed as a baseline for the current higher-Mach design effort using OVERFLOW. The results of the analysis, shown in Figure 87 and Appendix Figure A.15, show that while the basic features of the design have been updated, the preliminary layout was not intended to produce a high-performance design without additional design effort. The starting

design showcases the regions that will require the most attention to bring a higher-Mach design to fruition – the wing-strut juncture has a healthy shock, and the transition from the larger chord inboard strut (which houses the landing gear attachment) to the smaller-chord outboard strut is too heavily loaded. In actuality, the primary purpose of extending the high-speed design up to a higher Mach number (and up to a cruise Mach number that airlines are typically interested in) is largely based on the lack of available knowledge of strut-braced designs operating at high transonic Mach. It is therefore the objective of this design effort to determine the sensitivities and synergies of TTBW aircraft operating at more desirable cruise Mach numbers.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 87 – Configuration 1104 001 RA, M=0.8, 40kft, CL=0.695, Rema。
![](images/f25818c50bc235d2b24ad19393b5644821160b57c00cb6dd15118240a8408c98.jpg)  
Figure 87 – Configuration 1104-001-RA, M=0.8, 40kft, CL=0.695, Remac = 14M.

The starting point for vehicle spanload (shown in Figure 88), indicates a nonoptimal strut loading in which the inboard strut is not highly loaded, while the outboard strut is more heavily loaded than desired. In SUGAR Phase III, it was discovered that the optimal strut loading is higher inboard, and tapers to a negative sectional lift coefficient (and contribution to spanload) at the outboard end of the strut. This loading reduces the amount of aerodynamic interference between the wing and strut, and prevents the channel flow from resulting in a strong shock that severely limits vehicle performance (13).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 87 – Configuration 1104 001 RA, M=0.8, 40kft, CL=0.695, Rema。
![](images/ee12c417050fa1bd10c57aae6ad8e35a97299e064060e5d5fa4e322f2bb77fdd.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 88 – Configuration 1104-001-RA Spanload, M=0.8, 40kft, CL=0.695, and $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = \mathtt { 1 4 M }$ .

Even though the optimal wing-strut combination would be designed in conjunction with each other, based on the analysis of the starting design point it was decided to focus initially on the wing design alone to ensure viability of the basic wing design.

# Configuration 1104-001-RB – Preliminary Wing Design

Based on the OVERFLOW results of the –RA geometry, the wing underwent several modifications to update for the new design. First, the local modifications to the wing thickness distribution were removed, the wing thickness distribution was smoothed, and the outboard wing thickness was decreased slightly. Since the effects of the change in vehicle planform and the wing-strut channel’s sensitivity to operation at higher Mach number was unknown, it was preferred to remove the local wing design tailoring and start from a geometrically continuous thickness distribution. A comparison of the wing twist distributions between the initial wing design (_NEW) and revised design (RB_FINAL) are illustrated in Figure 89 below; the corresponding changes to the wing thickness distributions are shown in appendix Figure A.16.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 88 – Configuration 1104 001 RA Spanload, M=0.8, 40kft, CL=0.。
![](images/57f3270541cb750e590f194bf8bb4d20899e084bf5e07b57ddc9b9a8f28db1e3.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 89 – Configuration 1104-001-RB Wing Twist Modification.

In addition, a significant feature of the airfoil – the divergent trailing edge (DTE) - was removed from the starting airfoil stack. The inclusion of the DTE in the previous wind tunnel model posed significant manufacturing challenges given the small chord of the model. In addition, the strong adverse pressure gradients in the airfoil cove posed design challenges during Phase III due to the structural necessity of full-chord engine pylon attachments. The combination of trying to closeout an engine pylon at the same chordwise locations as the wing airfoils required significant attention to prevent the formation of large regions of separation. The solutions to this problem likely had a locally-negative impact on wing spanload. It was therefore decided that the use of DTE airfoil technology would only be used on an as-needed basis, and would not be used if possible. This is not to say that DTE technology would not be potentially beneficial to the TTBW design, but rather that at present the design challenges associated with inclusion of a DTE outweighs the potential benefits.

The end result of the wing-alone design activity (shown in Figure 90 and Appendix Figure A.17) was a wing with acceptable performance across the design envelope and drag divergence Mach of 0.8. Modifications to the inboard wing airfoils and wing-body fairing (covered in more detail in Section 4.1.3.1) moved the inboard wing shock forward significantly. The angle of attack of the wing-body system increased from $2 . 2 5 ^ { \circ }$ (at the design condition) to $3 . 8 9 ^ { \circ }$ -- this would be rectified in subsequent designs by an alteration to the wing-body rigging angle.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 89 – Configuration 1104 001 RB Wing Twist Modification。
![](images/1afe2d5a30757ddf3ca011cb452f1debbbe64b906bbec7ff63cb18c69c23d464.jpg)  
Figure 90 – Comparison of the 1104-001-RB wing-alone design wing pressures as compared to 1104-001-RA reference.

Upon completion of the wing-alone design activity a strut was added to the wing-body geometry to determine a new baseline for design activity. The focus of this design activity was primarily on the interaction between the wing and strut, and would ultimately culminate in the creation of a geometry (1104-001-RC) that could be used as a starting point for design optimization using the TRANAIR tool. This new configuration would also be used for flutter analysis.

Prior to the addition of the strut, strategic design decisions were made regarding the strut structure and desired aerodynamic loading. Previous design iterations of the TTBW concept utilized strut planforms that varied considerably in chord as a function of span. This was done primarily to limit the wetted area of the strut by reducing chord where structural requirements were less stringent. This followed the basic design observation that the strut was most critically loaded in the negative 1.0g-pushover maneuver, in which the strut became a column subjected to buckling loads. Since the strut attachment concept consisted of a pin-joint at either end of the strut, this meant that the strut structure could taper down significantly in size when approaching the pin joints. Minimizing the strut chord in this region thereby resulted in the characteristic ‘bow-tie’ strut planform found in the earlier phases of TTBW study. However, although the structural requirements were thereby significantly decreased near the pin joints, the severe changes in strut thickness and chord could lead to significant fluctuations in the strut’s contribution to vehicle spanload, and contributed to both additional complexity in the manufacture of the strut as well as in its cost. In addition, a component of the current TTBW design effort was to entail a significant amount of aero-structural optimization. This optimization

would have improved flexibility in design if the planform of the strut could be maintained throughout the study. Should the location of the jury-strut tie-in location be varied, the strut aerodynamic design could be considerably maintained. In addition, updated concepts of the strut-wing attachment suggested a larger spacing between the strut-wing attachment clevises, that would drive an increase in strut chord at the wing-strut attachment location. It was therefore decided that the strut planform would be significantly simplified from its earlier counterparts, with increased chord at the wing-strut attachment. The end result was a planform that remained relatively constant for much of its span.

The geometry made by combining the Revision B wing with the planform-modified strut design (which was at this point undesigned in an aerodynamic sense), referred to as 1104-001-RB, was analyzed in OVERFLOW. The results (shown in Figure 91 and Appendix Figure A.18) highlight general improvement in the wing design, but indicate that much improvement will be possible once detailed strut design begins. The two most obvious areas for improvement are the general strut loading and strong shock located in the wing-strut juncture. When examining the vehicle spanload Figure 92), it is clear that the strut has considerably too much load outboard near the strut junction, which is a significant contributor to the strong shock located in this region. The vehicle spanload is far from the idealized elliptical shape, which although the wing itself was twisted to produce this distribution on its own, has been strongly affected by the presence of the strut both inboard and outboard of the strut attachment.

It is worth reiterating herein that although the design effort began with a wing-alone study, the performance of the vehicle can only be refined when considering the wing-strut system as a whole. Consequently, the next step in the design process was to focus on wing-strut interactions.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 91 – OVERFLOW results for 1104 001 RB, M=0.8, 40kft, CL=0.69。
![](images/4a15f8caf4c1754ff98bbc99fbf2cb25129502c1abfbbf03ed32f4f1e64fb60e.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 91 – OVERFLOW results for 1104-001-RB, M=0.8, 40kft, CL=0.695, Remac=14M.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 91 – OVERFLOW results for 1104 001 RB, M=0.8, 40kft, CL=0.69。
![](images/9e5c811dd3709df0bb9d51602db49a2614d8386f808c16fb39ab9ec97d128527.jpg)  
Figure 92 – 1104-001-RB Spanload at Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = 1 4 \mathsf { M }$ .

# Configuration 1104-001-RC – Wing-Strut Interaction

The design of the combined wing-strut system began with the 1104-001-RB geometry, which consisted of a designed wing and undesigned strut. As shown previously in Figure 91, the strut

was initially carrying too much load, particularly outboard where high strut loading led to the formation of a strong juncture shock. Through a series of design iterations in which strut thickness, twist, and camber were manually adjusted along with adjustments to wing twist, the system was substantially improved in performance.

In this configuration, a strategic decision was made regarding the strut loading – the inboard strut was intentionally aerodynamically loaded to a much greater extent than the previous designs. Since most of the strut sizing constraints are the result of structural considerations, it is not a trivial task to reduce it in size without adversely affecting the vehicle structure. The structural constraints have driven the strut to a considerable size; the inboard strut chord and thickness are sized primarily by the landing gear integration, while the outboard end of the strut is sized by failsafe spacing requirements of the strut-wing attachment. The gear constraint has driven the inboard strut to be greater in chord and thickness than the wing root, and therefore represents a significant lifting surface. While the previous design philosophy had sought to keep strut loading down in order to avoid interference with the lower inboard wing surface, the $\mathsf { M } { = } 0 . 8$ planform enabled a greater degree of loading without adversely affecting the inboard wing lift. This was primarily the result of a localized ‘de-stacking’ of the wing and inboard strut brought about by changes to the wing sweep. In so doing, the inboard strut was longer underneath the wing, and therefore able to carry significantly more load than the lower Mach TTBW designs without significant aerodynamic interference. It was therefore decided to make use of this structure to the extent that the strut could carry increased lift so long as it did not culminate in the creation of a significant amount of wave drag. This allowed the strut to contribute to the aircraft lift, rather than being a simply parasitic element. The ideal objective would be to reduce the loading required on the wing, and thereby reducing the strength of the wing shock, which would in turn reduce wave drag.

The wing thickness was changed only in the regions that had a direct impact on wing-strut interaction. The regions closest to the wing-strut junction were manually ‘flattened’ in curvature so that the flow acceleration through the channel could be limited, and the formation of a strong shock avoided. While this curvature change would ideally be completed without a net change to the wing thickness, it was found that both curvature manipulation and cross-sectional area progression control were both required to produce an operable design.

When placing the previously-designed wing in the proximity of the strut, twist changes (illustrated in Figure 93) were required to achieve the desired wing-strut loading balance. In this case, the wing loading was significantly increased relative to the strut loading. The corresponding wing $\mathrm { ( { t } / { c } ) _ { \mathrm { { m a x } } } }$ distributions for the –RB and –RC configurations are compared in Appendix Figure A.19.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 93 – Wing twist evolution from 1104 001 RB to 1104 001 RC Co。
![](images/45ea4f63e4a0eb6b6649bbf357a87463efaaf15a5e2c11718225dad32a0eb18d.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 93 – Wing twist evolution from 1104-001-RB to 1104-001-RC Configuration.

Further to the point of strut planform normalization described in Section 4.1.2.3, in addition to the strut planform, the strut thickness and twist had to be normalized as the design moved away from the ‘bowtie’ planform concept as well. Therefore, as the design evolved from –RB to –RC configurations, the strut thickness was smoothed considerably. The remaining aspects of the bowtie planform were thereby removed.

Figure 94 shows the design evolution of the strut twist as the integrated effects of wing and strut were considered together. The dramatic decrease in strut incidence near the wing-strut juncture is evident outboard of eta $\mathtt { \mathtt { = 0 . 4 } }$ . Here the strut is locally at a negative incidence angle so that the flow into the wing-strut channel is significantly reduced in speed. The design situation here is somewhat analogous to controlling mass flow into an engine by closing down the exit area of the nacelle. The strut airfoil sections are rigged about a common twist axis (at strut $\times / { \mathsf { c } } { = } 0 . 4 )$ in order to keep the strut elastic axis straight so that eccentric loading of the strut can be minimized. Therefore, locally negative strut incidence angles drive the strut trailing edge up, rather than maintaining the strut trailing edge location and moving the leading edge down, which would open the channel capture area. The evolution of the strut $\mathrm { ( t / c ) } _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ distribution is illustrated in Appendix Figure A.20.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 94 shows the design evolution of the strut twist as the inte。
![](images/3f48f09dcfaf2bb7733fc5a427450cc326ded942ef4d41e1308ace79742df702.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 94 – Strut twist evolution from 1104-001-RB to 1104-001-RC Configuration.

The end result of the design changes made in configuration –RC are shown in Figure 95 and Appendix Figure A.21. The wing loading is more consistent across the span, the inboard wing shock weakened and the outboard strut loading has been reduced significantly. Also apparent is a visible increase in inboard strut loading, consistent with the strategic decision to carry a larger percentage of the aerodynamic load. Also of note, the shock in the wing-strut channel has been virtually eliminated at this flight condition.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 94 – Strut twist evolution from 1104 001 RB to 1104 001 RC C。
![](images/3505442efbdee8d3164089d6323bdc80c18049de482707633ec983b1fcb79f3a.jpg)  
Figure 95 – Comparison of surface Cp contours from 1104-001–RB Configuration to 1104-001–RC (Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, ${ \mathsf { R e } } _ { \mathsf { m a c } } = 1 4 { \mathsf { M } } )$ .

Figure 96 shows the change in spanload as the design evolved to the –RC configuration. In this figure, the total spanload (black curve) has been substantially improved with a closer resemblance to the idealized elliptic shape. Strut loading inboard (blue line) has been maintained at a high level inboard but decreases as a function of span eventually reaching a locally downloaded state near the wing-strut juncture. This download is primarily the result of reducing curvature of the strut in the juncture region, which naturally results in an ‘upside-down’ airfoil camber. The red line, which denotes the wing contribution to spanload, shows a relatively flat loading on the inboard wing with a local increase in loading at the wing-strut juncture region in which the wing is used to offset the strut download. Similar to the strut, the wing experiences a local increase in lift coefficient as the underside of the wing airfoil is also locally flattened to reduce flow acceleration – these elements combine to create a local increase in wing lift.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 96 shows the change in spanload as the design evolved to the。
![](images/a85e3c910cce775da3c7df8d0b464c3b5f2befbe7ec0f272cf7febc867bbd127.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 96 shows the change in spanload as the design evolved to the。
![](images/6622948ed680580d04f6ec02d44a2e5c8ee96cf95aeb52842975ef7900d9236c.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 96 shows the change in spanload as the design evolved to the。
![](images/202e9529090afb9eadc8ebfe99a7e71a63779b5c0271dc779eed5a532520ca98.jpg)  
Figure 96 – Comparison of Spanload from 1104-001-RB Configuration to 1104-001-RC at Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = \mathtt { 1 4 M }$ .

The net aerodynamic performance effect of the design activities for the –RC configuration are shown in Figure 97. The L/D of the configuration has been increased considerably from the starting design. Overall, the system is sufficiently well-behaved enough at this point to be used as a seed geometry for aerodynamic and aero-structural optimization efforts.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 96 – Comparison of Spanload from 1104 001 RB Configuration t。
![](images/99559ddede9c42dce13c9f22b669806d10a755d340afb6b9aef8fe7c0d5c3f38.jpg)

# Configuration 1104-001-RD – Aerodynamic and Aerostructural Optimization

This phase of the aerodynamic design effort utilized a combination of manual design adjustments and TRANAIR optimization. Results from the optimization studies completed in TRANAIR were validated for drag using OVEFLOW so that constant monitoring of the vehicle performance could ensure continual progress. Eventually a point was reached in which the differences in the flow physics being modeled between the two codes was not resolvable. These differences, however, constituted very small levels in drag and were most concentrated in areas in which there was a high degree of 3-dimensionality in the flow, such as in the wing-strut juncture and at the wingtip.

Initial optimization runs starting with the –RC geometry studied the varied effects of changing the physical design constraints and flow conditions to be used in the optimization. Flow conditions ranged from low-lift conditions to high-lift conditions near the required buffet boundary, and various weightings and combinations of these conditions. Both boundary layer and surface geometry limitations were also placed on the models. The effects of these types of modifications were described in Section 4.1.1.3.

# Strut Planform Modification

In the course of the design optimization, structural models began to show that the outboard strut was not successfully meeting the EI and GJ structural design requirements determined by the FEM analysis. The aerostructural models determined a significant strut airfoil thickness change was required to meet the requirements. The bending and torsion structural requirements for the strut box and the required thickness changes to the strut necessary to meet the requirements are illustrated in Appendix Figure A.22.

Since the aerodynamic penalty for changing the strut thickness so close to the wing-strut juncture was so high, an alternate approach was used to reduce the strut load by moving the jury strut outboard to reduce the structural requirement (Figure 98). The outboard strut length L1 was reduced by $1 5 \%$ , resulting in a new outboard strut length L1A. Since buckling load is proportional to length squared, this resulted in a peak EI/GJ load reduction of $\sim 4 0 \%$ for the outboard strut. The inboard strut length was increased by $1 5 \%$ from L2 to L2A, which increased inboard strut EI/GJ loads by $\sim 3 2 \%$ . Since the jury strut would react to the column buckling loads as a column in buckling as well, the jury was mounted perpendicular to the strut to decrease the eccentricity of the loading. This had the additional benefit of keeping the channel area as high as possible since a vertical jury would enclose a smaller area between the wing-strut-jury channel.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 98 – Movement in the jury strut to strut connection was move。
![](images/f35f2ea9c5ddcccc4d142eb66b2428e041e7c0f503f4cad2d52369f16cb9db26.jpg)  
Figure 98 – Movement in the jury strut-to-strut connection was moved outboard to reduce the load on the outboard strut.

However, while the outboard jury strut movement decreased the load on the outboard strut, it increased the load experienced by the inboard strut. Therefore, the inboard strut was scaled with a combination of increased thickness and chord to provide required strength. In this process, there ended up being a large discrepancy in the chord requirements at the strut structural transition region where the landing gear enclosure meets the region of the strut dominated by buckling requirements. Since a rapid transition from a large to short chord in the transition region would result in a significant effect on spanload, it made sense from an aerodynamic perspective to blend the structure more continuously and take the opportunity to increase the sweep at the root of the strut. The planform view of the resulting modification is shown in Figure 99, and the resulting strut twist distribution is shown in Figure 100; the strut thickness distribution is shown in Appendix Figure A.23.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 98 – Movement in the jury strut to strut connection was move。
![](images/7e08587420d4480f1c9aa36442c402cc4c02ba59b0271fb7d53f7957aad0e3d3.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report

图片摘要：该图主要展示 98 – Movement in the jury strut to strut connection was move。
![](images/ca01f1343c8bc042d21fbe03a61577677be6ef7b897ef738721eb915d6181c2f.jpg)  
Figure 99 – Strut Planform Modification (1104-001-RD_des30).   
Figure 100 – Modified –RD_des30 strut twist distribution as a function of wing semi-span.

When the modified strut planform geometry was analyzed in OVERFLOW, the resultant effect on the strut loading was a significant reduction in the peak ${ \mathsf { C } } _ { \mathsf { p } }$ levels in the structural transition region on the strut (Figure 101 and Appendix Figure A.24). While the manual modification of the strut did not have a significant impact on drag, the modified strut would benefit from subsequent aerodynamic design with –RD_des30 becoming the new seed geometry to be used in the TRANAIR optimization process.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 100 – Modified –RD des30 strut twist distribution as a funct。
![](images/8cf2b9924e0d3b26d1b9fbd77ea265a5807a686885bcb95c4fa97423ff1eef1d.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report

图片摘要：该图主要展示 100 – Modified –RD des30 strut twist distribution as a funct。
![](images/8a2c36834024ee97a429364467a77a5c6de60e758d1d492e18143a1b317e0bd9.jpg)

# Aerostructural Investigation of Jury Strut Material

In the course of the –RD configuration optimization, a more detailed study of the strut structure was initiated in order to determine the impact on vehicle weight. In this study, the aerostructural sizing capability in TRANAIR was used to vary jury-strut spanwise location for a variety of different strut materials. EI and GJ stiffness constraints were updated from a baseline set as the jury-strut was relocated, then structural sizing was conducted throughout the span of the strut with different materials defined on each side of the strut to identify the lowest weight, stiffnessfeasible jury-strut location. All composite, titanium, and steel structural boxes were analyzed outboard of the jury-strut. In addition, forward and aft spar locations were varied as well. Based on the results of the study (shown in Figures 102 and 103), an updated jury-strut location range was identified: an all-composite outboard strut had a feasible span location range from ${ \tt Y } { \approx } 4 2 5 ^ { \prime \prime }$ to $4 3 5 ^ { \prime \prime }$ , while the steel outboard strut was feasible inboard of ${ \tt Y } { \approx } 4 3 5 ^ { \prime \prime }$ .

The finalized jury strut location $( \mathsf { Y } = 4 2 5 ^ { \prime \prime } ,$ ) was determined based on a combination of the structural design requirements (detailed in this section) in combination with the aerodynamic design considerations detailed in Section 4.1.3.4.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 101 – Strut Planform Modification Reduced Peak Cp on Strut i。
![](images/e660ab564c77f71aa88fdc35dd75850564c88dc42f8b58fc62b46ee0744aff0e.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 102 – Aerostructural optimization results for a composite st。
![](images/e5fc8330698bfafc965d899ba35d51461a9447159486f94d592aeb567bc074e7.jpg)  
Figure 102 – Aerostructural optimization results for a composite-steel strut and composite wing comparing the seed and optimized geometry structural sizing.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 102 – Aerostructural optimization results for a composite st。
![](images/8f4bd56303c5d8361ea76600c2bf39bdd98987f03041b813a9abfb44f9100783.jpg)  
Figure 103 – Jury-strut location study results with multiple materials and structural box sizing. Weight and stiffness feasibility results are shown for forward/aft spar locations different x/c locations along the local chord.

# Configuration 1104-001-RE – Offset Strut Aerodynamic Design

One of the most challenging aspects of the strut-braced wing design is the junction region between the wing and strut. The design challenges in this region are both aerodynamic and structural. From an aerodynamic perspective, at transonic cruise speeds, the high-velocity channel flow in the corner between the wing-strut juncture has a tendency to develop a strong shock. To mitigate this concern, the juncture is aerodynamically shaped in such a way as to reduce the flow velocity and acceleration within this channel. However, while the Baseline Strut concept

(Figure 104a) is the simplest and most light weight structural concept from a wing-strut attachment concept, the wing structure is locally compromised by a ‘scalloping’ of the lower wing surface proximate to the strut attachment. As a result, there are practical limits to how much wing scalloping is acceptable from a structural standpoint. An alternative concept to the straight (baseline) strut attachment concept would be to offset the strut from the wing and design a curved beam/fitting to transfer the strut load into the wing (Figure 104b). This concept would ideally preserve the continuity of the wing structure. However, the curved fitting required to transfer the load from the wing into the strut would have substantial strength requirements of its own. Therefore, it is necessary to investigate the implications of these attachment concepts from both a structural and aerodynamic perspective. The aerodynamic aspects of the offset strut design are described below.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 103 – Jury strut location study results with multiple materi。
![](images/a2d74db1d7b9e71b231df4312d1e9494c3d6a3526dd134c2725bcdf2e70bebd1.jpg)  
(a)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 104 – Strut Concpt a) Baseline Strut and b) Offset Strut (ca。
![](images/e9faee3c9f79b6679b52bce519b1c269aafecfc09a473074165e187cc618cc3d.jpg)  
  
Figure 104 – Strut Concpt a) Baseline Strut and b) Offset Strut (cartoon)

In order to make a preliminary investigation of the offset strut’s effect on the aerodynamic performance of a TTBW aircraft, a structure-generated volumetric constraint was required, around which an aerodynamic fairing could be sized and designed. The constraint used in the analysis was based on the working offset strut concept at that time, which was comprised of a standing leg that extended $9 . 1 ^ { \prime \prime }$ below the wing surface, which then connected to a curved beam/fitting with a clevis located ${ 2 0 . 0 ^ { \prime \prime } }$ below the wing surface. An aerodynamic fairing was tightly wrapped around this constraint (refer to Figure 105).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 104 – Strut Concpt a) Baseline Strut and b) Offset Strut (ca。
![](images/dba40785c8d282793b98bc0e4c8a23a7cfa766795b62de0770cd2dfa28ab0119.jpg)  
Figure 105 – Offset Strut Structural Requirement used in Design.

One of the features of the new offset strut fairing was the integration/collocation of the fairing with a flap hinge fairing. In order to wrap such a significant structural member with an aerodynamic fairing, an airfoil of high thickness/chord ratio must be installed. Ideally, the size of

the fairing is minimized. However, the location of the fairing makes the integration with the wing particularly challenging. It was shown in Phase III (5) that a secondary aerodynamic fairing (referred to in the previous design as a ‘typhoon’ fairing) was required to be installed on the back of the offset fairing in order to eliminate a region of flow separation behind the pylon, as shown in Figure 106.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 105 – Offset Strut Structural Requirement used in Design。
![](images/000161866175550c4823251e31a8ec43375929a89461af9b0868003b396b1dca.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 105 – Offset Strut Structural Requirement used in Design。
![](images/14c4345e4f74fa87bc671f02f55df38c8c7800f2642153241dba2b857f7aa4c7.jpg)  
Figure 106 – Alternate and Offset Strut Fairings from Phase III and Phase IV.

Once the initial aerodynamic fairing was created, it was analyzed in OVERFLOW. The initial design of the pylon (shown in Figure 107) had some shock-induced separation on the outboard side, a large region of flow separation on the inboard side, and a strong shock on the upper side of the strut. Similar to the approach used for 1104-001-RC, manual design adjustments were made to the fairing and local strut geometry to reduce the severity of these flow features – changes to theleading edge radius, local airfoil droop, toe-out of the strut attachment, and a large localized wing bump were all required to address these areas.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 106 – Alternate and Offset Strut Fairings from Phase III and。
![](images/880afe8727289baf42d6267142f78916e9c5755d557ddfd3bbf35df810b35cf3.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 106 – Alternate and Offset Strut Fairings from Phase III and。
![](images/ba97275c9d6358ad7eae83decc31fcf1c03810e1f9fc1e21c9d40057123befc5.jpg)  
Figure 107 – OVERFLOW analysis of starting offset strut fairing geometry showed significant flow separation on the inboard and outboard sides at Mach $\mathbf { \tau } = 0 . 8$ , $\mathsf { C } _ { \mathsf { L } } = 0 . 6 9 5$ , $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = 1 4 \mathsf { M }$ .

The end result of the changes to the design are shown in Figure 108. Although this design has not undergone significant design optimization, the regions of flow separation and the strut upper surface shock have been eliminated.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 107 – OVERFLOW analysis of starting offset strut fairing geo。
![](images/bc14b2d5e491f0c07cbe7ef4864d9f3f30ab2ab3300e1c4844cb49189793dc5f.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 108 – OVERFLOW analysis of final offset strut fairing geometry at Mach = 0.8, $\mathsf { C } _ { \mathsf { L } } = 0 . 6 9 \mathsf { \Omega }$ , $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = 1 4 \mathsf { M }$ .

When tracking these design changes at the configuration level, it is evident that the aerodynamic performance of the –RE offset strut configuration is essentially equivalent to the performance of the -RC baseline strut configuration. This performance comparison includes a flap hinge fairing that is not included in the baseline strut solution. Since the offset strut has a relatively thick airfoil section wrapped around it as a fairing, there is a high degree of synergy to include the flap hinge fairing in the offset strut to minimize drag of the vehicle as a whole.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 108 – OVERFLOW analysis of final offset strut fairing geomet。
![](images/5607f472d050d94e4a1cd2038c34c5026ad3959bf3440174a87caaeaf292b8ef.jpg)  
Figure 109 – Comparison of baseline and offset strut performance at Mach = 0.80 Remac = 14M.

One of the primary objectives of the offset strut design was a targeted reduction in the severity of the required wing shaping near the wing-strut juncture. While the baseline strut requires significant shaping (local thinning of the wing section), it was the hope that the offset strut could reduce this severity, restoring the structural depth of the section. The wing airfoil for the baseline strut configuration has been significantly thinned (as shown in Appendix Figure A.25) in order to prevent the formation of a strong shock in the wing-strut channel. Therefore, the basic premise that the offset strut can be at least aerodynamically similar to the baseline strut without significant thinning of the local wing section appears viable. That being said, performance at the vehicle level would have to trade the added weight of the offset strut attachment as compared to the increase in wing weight required to locally thin the wing with the baseline strut attachment concept.

Throughout this phase of work the offset strut concept has been in continual development. The latest revision to this concept is shown in Figure 110.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 109 – Comparison of baseline and offset strut performance at。
![](images/01fe35788a714eb62004ac48a34852a00a4ad8a067885d6068ff4882ccf54128.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 109 – Comparison of baseline and offset strut performance at。
![](images/731a373edfc44c24468faf88b15de40d2861df39d54c99404bb1085a58b104c6.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 110 – Updated Curved Beam Strut Attachment。
![](images/6edf7003b9ff87683a33ce9bd18a5fc34baea5f7730f8bf04ee01a6e57a8ed8a.jpg)  
Figure 110 – Updated Curved Beam Strut Attachment.

While the aerodynamic and structural performance of the offset strut configuration remains of interest, challenges in adapting the optimization process to this geometry along with the parity in performance relative to the baseline strut configuration suggested that it was sufficient to continue with the baseline strut when moving into the optimization phase of design. For this reason, the 1104-001-RF configuration was not investigated in detail.

# 4.1.3 Regions of Special Consideration

The TTBW configuration has several regions where special attention is needed in regard to design. Many of these regions are not typical areas of design focus for low-wing cantilevered aircraft, but can have a large effect on the performance of the aircraft.

# Wing-body fairing Design

For conventional cantilevered low-wing designs, the shape of the wing-body fairing, which nominally encapsulates the landing gear and additional equipment, does not have a first-order effect on the aerodynamic performance of the vehicle. For a high-wing aircraft in which significantly more lift can be carried over top of the body, this region takes on a higher importance. And, in the case of a TTBW configuration, that importance increases further still since the thin, high aspect ratio wing has considerably less volume to host systems, fuel, and equipment than a cantilevered wing design with a Yehudi on the inboard wing. The proper design of a wing-body fairing for a TTBW aircraft therefore provides a significant opportunity to increase aircraft performance, and provide volume for fuel and systems.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 111 – OVERFLOW solutions of the original wing body fairing d。
![](images/0efba0b2243dbf53145226158d53d02997c7b015093074631881968480951296.jpg)  
Figure 111 – OVERFLOW solutions of the original wing-body fairing design at the start of Phase III. Contours on the left of the symmetry plane are $\complement _ { \mathfrak { p } } ,$ contours on the right are Cf at Mach = 0.745, $\mathtt { C _ { L } } = 0 . 7 3$ , Remac = 12.3M (5).

The original loft of the wing-body fairing at the beginning of Phase III used a fairly minimalistic approach to closing the wing-body intersection. While this approach minimizes the wetted area of the vehicle, it has an obvious negative effect on the drag of the vehicle resulting from several regions of flow separation and pressure drag as illustrated in Figure 111 and Appendix Figure A.26. In addition, at cruise, the vehicle lift does not appear to carry over the body to the extent possible. The performance of the wing-body region at low speed was also examined to ensure compatibility of the design with high-lift systems integration.

The wing-body fairing was first examined for its performance at high-speed. As stated, the design objectives were to: a) remove any areas of flow separation, b) increase body carry-over lift without the formation of a strong shock across the vehicle symmetry plane, and c) provide additional volume for systems, fuel, etc. Fortunately, many of these objectives drive the design in a similar direction, producing a fairing of increased volume relative to the starting geometry. If this volume is allocated properly, the fairing can also have a net positive effect on the vehicle’s area ruling, providing additional benefit at high speed.

The first design of an updated fairing (shown in Figure 112 and Appendix Figure A.27) reduced the drag of the vehicle significantly. However, the shock on the top of the body remained locally strong, thus indicating room for improvement in the design. In addition to examining the design at cruise speeds, the vehicle was also analyzed at low speed $( \mathsf { M } = 0 . 2 )$ to ensure that the high-lift system would not be adversely impacted by the design. When analyzing the clean wing-strut geometry (no wing leading or trailing edge devices), a significant vortex was discovered emanating from the wing-body juncture. The flow had the characteristics of a strong junction vortex. Since this vortex had the potential to interact with the strut or tail surfaces downstream

or could cause substantial unsteadiness of the vehicle in the wind tunnel, a new series of designs sought to weaken or eliminate this vortex.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 112 – First Attempt at a re lofted wing body fairing (a) CAD。
![](images/7665e237ea97e4704be57276f0fbcfc6c6616fe5f5caf9273ec53d0c28ba72cd.jpg)  
(a)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 112 – First Attempt at a re lofted wing body fairing (a) CAD。
![](images/252730a1bb89a8d81324e629d5150b45a2704cfd260ea0f7cb95d5eee0df868b.jpg)  
(b)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 112 – First Attempt at a re lofted wing body fairing (a) CAD。
![](images/3a88bab4d3f6ba2960658351b72129e105b7f1aee98bf7dae41781199c8215b9.jpg)  
（c）  
Figure 112 – First Attempt at a re-lofted wing-body fairing (a) CAD geometry (b) Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, Remac = 14M (c) Mach $= 0 . 2 0$ , $a = 1 6 ^ { \circ }$ , $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = 1 3 \mathsf { M }$ .

The first set of designs analyzed employed a leading edge horn (or strake) to break up the formation of the vortex. One of those designs (shown in Figure 113 and Appendix Figure A.28) met many of the design criteria desired for the fairing (particularly low drag at cruise), but were unsuccessful in breaking up the vortex at low speed. The final design (Figure 114) employed a strake with a reversed curvature (when observed in a planform view). This geometry successfully reduced the drag of the vehicle at cruise, significantly weakened the junction vortex, had a larger internal volume (for fuel and aircraft systems), and also provided wiping/sealing surfaces for the wing’s leading and trailing edge high-lift devices.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 112 – First Attempt at a re lofted wing body fairing (a) CAD。
![](images/19194bc5a2faea161329d7a6b3cefec804434153bdbd5450887a851c7e480df9.jpg)  
(a)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 112 – First Attempt at a re lofted wing body fairing (a) CAD。
![](images/9501b49f46a1dbd1f939a04d95aad49a14396e4add83233d77bc49f68cf09d26.jpg)  
(b)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 113 – Revised fairing increases volume, reduces shock streng。
![](images/9866e2d31aa62f438fdac829ec7bd2d371c1b34f47480349a781c2100a26a119.jpg)  
（c）  
Figure 113 – Revised fairing increases volume, reduces shock strength, but strengthens the low-speed vortex (a) CAD geometry (b) Mach = 0.80, $\mathsf { C } _ { \mathsf { L } } = \mathsf { 0 } . 6 9 5$ , Remac = 14M (c) Mach $= 0 . 2 0$ , $\alpha = 1 6 ^ { \circ }$ , $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = 1 3 \mathtt { M }$ .

图片摘要：该图主要展示 113 – Revised fairing increases volume, reduces shock streng。
![](images/1e889bdfcf2a3f3df89c0393e503050ae18e04a8f715e5f70edb48959b82b950.jpg)
