![](images/89641d24e3cdbe48f945e4264d012bcaf7fc0f4b2339b27df442738abe859ab9.jpg)  
Figure 114 – Reverse planform wing-body strake.

# Baseline Strut Wing-Strut Juncture Design

In order to keep interference drag to a low level when operating at transonic conditions, the wing-strut juncture requires special attention in design. The wing and strut surfaces must be designed in such a way that at cruise conditions, the flow into the juncture does not culminate in a strong shock. The flow interactions between the surfaces results in a highly three dimensional design space. Previous design studies (Phase III) showed that a local downloading of the strut aerodynamic load near the wing-strut juncture – the result of localized wing and strut shape tailoring – is the most critical component to wing-strut juncture design. However, in the course of this contract, additional investigations have focused on the compatibility of this juncture offdesign, with special emphasis on low-speed operations and high- $\mathsf { \cdot } \mathsf { C } _ { \mathsf { L } }$ operation (near buffet).

# Low-Speed Compatibility

Preliminary high-lift analysis of the configuration (with high-lift surfaces deployed) showed that the original wing-strut fairing design had a significant negative effect on aircraft stall at low speed. The primary issue with fairing compatibility at low-speed was due to the forward extent of the fairing. In order to sufficiently blend the wing and strut together, the fairing extended forward on the underside of the wing, approaching the wing leading edge. While this design represented a low-drag solution at cruise, it resulted in a cutout (or spanwise gap) in the wing’s leading edge protection during high-lift operations. At low speed and high angle of attack, this cutout created a large region of separated flow, which dictated the maximum lift coefficient of the entire aircraft. For this reason, the design of the wing-strut fairing was revisited with special attention on highlift system compatibility.

While there are potential mechanical solutions that could ‘open’ up the forward extent of the fairing in order to deploy a leading edge device, it was believed that such a concept represented a degree of mechanical complexity that was not desirable. Therefore, the forward extent of the fairing was trimmed back to a chordwise extent that would allow for a conventional Krueger mechanism to deploy from the wing lower surface. (refer to Figure 115).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 115 – The wing strut fairing was trimmed back to enable comp。
![](images/b82cf86e509288c6351c8886a25fa7b0b7ed2c79e3d7558f1fe795c704f7d337.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 115 – The wing-strut fairing was trimmed back to enable compatibility with a leading edge Krueger.

The cutback fairing geometry was then analyzed throughout the aircraft’s operational envelope to determine its associated drag penalty as compared to a more blended solution. OVERFLOW analysis (results shown in Figure 116 and Appendix Figure A.29) determined that the drag penalty was $\sim 0 . 1$ cts at the design condition. Given the potential mechanical complexity (and associated cost), it was decided that this represented an acceptable compromise to aircraft performance.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 115 – The wing strut fairing was trimmed back to enable comp。
![](images/f8731606a2224fb400ded0ca758f52efbcd0c049b92c7ea92e2b118bc4f4f46e.jpg)  
Figure 116 – Revisions to the wing-strut fairing to ensure high-lift system compatibility.

# High-Speed Compatibility

In addition to high-lift compatibility requirements, the design of the wing-strut juncture at offdesign high-speed conditions was also considered. A study was undertaken to determine the effects of a plain flap deflection in the wing-strut juncture region (extent shown in Figure 117) to determine if such a device could alleviate any potential undesirable flow conditions within the channel (such as the formation of strong shocks) when operating across the flight envelope. The flap would achieve this end by providing the ability to alter the mass flow into the wing-strut channel, thereby affecting local flow conditions. If the internal channel flow was critical, the flap could deflect up to spill flow out of the channel. If the underside of the strut becomes critical

(such as at very low lift coefficients), the flap could deflect down to mitigate shock strength on the underside of the strut.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 116 – Revisions to the wing strut fairing to ensure high lif。
![](images/e93649d31c022910998cdd4cf9515146a3dd3fa580ddb6da6bbf1142ae5616a0.jpg)  
Figure 117 - Strut Flap Definition

This study compared the effects of three different spanwise flap extents across a range of deflection angles $( - 2 ^ { \circ } , - 4 ^ { \circ } , - 1 0 ^ { \circ } )$ : a ‘large’ flap (extending from $\yen 473$ to $5 7 1 ^ { \prime \prime }$ ), a ‘medium’ flap (extending from $\yen 12$ to 571”), and a ‘small’ flap (extending from $\tt Y = 5 4 7 ^ { \prime \prime }$ to 571”). The study assumed a $20 \%$ chord simple-hinge device of similar construction to an aileron. This device would be considered a fast-moving device that is a part of the aircraft control system, rather than part of the aircraft’s high-lift system. Appendix Figure A.30 illustrates the strut flap geometry and the three different span extents.

The various flap deflection angles and extents were analyzed in OVERFLOW across a range of lift coefficients and Mach numbers to determine aircraft performance sensitivities to device configuration. In general, the results showed that system performance could be improved offdesign, and that channel shock strength could be controlled via deflection of a strut flap. While the study did not seek to determine the ‘optimum’ size and deflection of a strut flap for this configuration, the flap was shown to provide a benefit across a wide range of flow conditions. The effects of the strut flap deflection at a high lift coefficient off-design condition are shown in Appendix Figure A.31 and the effects at a high Mach number off-design condition are shown in Appendix Figure A.32.

Analysis of the strut flap across a range of lift coefficients showed successful mitigation of channel shock strength via deflection of the strut flap. In the case of the $- 4 ^ { \circ }$ deflection, the channel shock was virtually eliminated. Varying lift coefficient produced trends showing a more localized benefit to system performance via flap deflection in that the smaller flaps were more effective at improving system performance than the large flap.

In contrast, when increasing vehicle Mach number the greatest performance benefit came from the large flap span. This suggests the configuration benefits from a more global effect, in which the channel Mach number is decreased across a larger region of wing-strut interaction.

The range of benefits shown by strut deflections of various span extents shows that a strut flap would be a valuable tool for inclusion in typical aircraft spanload control systems, providing improved flexibility in aircraft operations across a range of conditions. In addition, the deflection of a strut flap has been shown to mitigate shock strength in the wing-strut region, and may provide additional control for the mitigation or alleviation of buffet-causing flow features at the farthest extents of the flight envelope. It is therefore possible that given these findings the basic strut design could benefit from a relaxed set of design requirements in which more aggressive strut geometries could be examined.

# Flap Hinge Fairing Design

As the design of the TTBW vehicle has increased in maturity, additional details of a complete aircraft configuration have been included in the design. This phase of the TTBW design effort has sought to include low-speed (high-lift) design requirements into the final high-speed geometry. Flap Hinge Fairings (FHFs) provide an aerodynamic covering for the mechanisms that deploy the flaps during high-lift operations (Figure 117).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 118 – Flap articulation mechanism schematic (14)。
![](images/34922b5a2aebe8426509b01cbf74f8d97ffcabafd5fec1cb2089c62e5c0b6ff9.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 118 – Flap articulation mechanism schematic (14)。
![](images/61203044003a17d82dc457261c16d1f2d5fb35277aa7878df012163dafe0c54b.jpg)  
Figure 118 – Flap articulation mechanism schematic (14).

While at this time the final TTBW high-lift architecture has not yet been determined, a representative set of fairings has been analyzed in order to determine if any unique considerations need to be made in order to accommodate wing-strut interactions. In particular, the outboard-most fairings may have a significant interaction with the strut as it approaches the wing-strut join region. The fairings size (volume) and shape being utilized for the study are approximately representative of a simple-hinge with drooped spoiler trailing edge high-lift architecture. Given the uncertainty surrounding the final high-lift architecture, a detailed analysis of the flap high-lift structure has not been completed at this time. Therefore, the FHFs examined represent a ‘typical’ set of fairings for the control system planform detailed in Section 5.3.

However, while the strut poses some unusual possibilities for fairing-strut aerodynamic interaction, it also provides a unique opportunity at a system level. The short-chord high aspect ratio wing of the TTBW has less chordwise extent available for the closeout of the engine pylon. In SUGAR Phase III (5), it was shown that some substantial aerodynamic filleting was required to mitigate the adverse pressure gradients created by the superposition of the wing and engine pylon closeouts. This problem was also present in the Phase III alternate strut pylon design, which required the use of a ‘typhoon fairing’ in order to mitigate the closeout. However, in the case of the Phase IV design, it is possible to take advantage of design synergies coming from integration of the wing and engine pylon attachments with the FHFs. Both the wing-strut join (Figure 118) and the engine pylon (Figure 119) provide a benefit for system-level drag reduction by collocating these fairings, thereby reduced the wetted area and frontal area of the FHFs as compared to a an unintegrated solution. This also provides the potential for a structural benefit in the form of potential load-sharing between the support structures. The configuration with all FHFs installed (shown in Figure 120) highlights the proximity of the $2 ^ { \mathsf { n d } }$ -most-outboard FHF to the proximity of the strut.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 118 – Flap articulation mechanism schematic (14)。
![](images/32ea9c62fc88411ea62637d4085109cf4926f46a8e5019b3f99712fd893bdc4c.jpg)  
Figure 119 – Wing-strut fairing with integrated FHF.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 119 – Wing strut fairing with integrated FHF。
![](images/0d87746e6a8dab9aafc53720ff439fe9c4d29f6c9e798aeb317aba6a9c60caad.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 119 – Wing strut fairing with integrated FHF。
![](images/fa465c43784c86f7a85906b68f840a73a2449ebde882e7e99acbb79a50e7159b.jpg)  
Figure 120 – Engine Pylon fairing with integrated FHF.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 120 – Engine Pylon fairing with integrated FHF。
![](images/ad90bdb7ad5e153802ea735465c9e3b5e613cc9c7f2afb3d2936c94cb990eb83.jpg)  
Figure 121 – Configuration with all FHFs installed.

The FHFs were analyzed in OVERFLOW to determine if the strut-FHF aerodynamic interactions resulted in any undesirable flow features, such as a significant strengthening of the channel juncture shock. The OVERFLOW skin friction coefficient results with the unintegrated FHFs added to the configuration are shown in Figure 122 and Appendix Figure A.33.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 121 – Configuration with all FHFs installed。
![](images/d03ff470b7b56442ce4c6bfd4a6e79e3fc84ced0135dba7c2b5acbcdf6d0d2dd.jpg)  
Figure 122 – OVERFLOW Cf results of the flow around the middle (unintegrated) FHFs (Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, ${ \mathsf { R e } } _ { \mathsf { m a c } } = 1 4 { \mathsf { M } } )$ .

The results of the OVERFLOW analysis showed that the net effect of the FHF installation was a general slowing of the flow into the channel, particularly near the wing-strut juncture. This effect is indicated in Figure 123 (and Appendix Figure A.34) by the reduction in severity of flow acceleration around the upper surface of the strut leading edge. The installation also had the net effect of increasing the lift of the wing (creating a corresponding decrease in the strut lift), which resulted in a decrease in vehicle angle of attack of ${ \sim } 0 . 0 4 ^ { \circ }$ at the design condition.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 122 – OVERFLOW Cf results of the flow around the middle (uni。
![](images/2210ca64d71884717a73988662009796a0710fea7b5d38ec3b2d2d814c2962ac.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 122 – OVERFLOW Cf results of the flow around the middle (uni。
![](images/23717eea3e15795c9a85a637a1c3147e9e1cd1607e41bdcf9a2d62449150432d.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 122 – OVERFLOW Cf results of the flow around the middle (uni。
![](images/0a41e8e6a9b19d38a4f9c2e34d626c263407a3525d4b11e08a379efa771c0ab8.jpg)  
Figure 123 – Flap Hinge Fairing (FHF) installation on the wing-strut fairing surface Cp (Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, ${ \mathsf { R e } } _ { \mathsf { m a c } } = \mathtt { 1 4 } { \mathsf { M } } )$ .

In general, the integration of the FHFs into the solution were not found to have any significant negative effect of the cruise performance of the vehicle, with no special areas of concern related to FHF-strut interaction. The net change in drag for FHF installation was roughly consistent with drag increments of other aircraft operating at these conditions.

# Jury Strut Design

Over the course of Phase IV, the location of the jury strut has been under consideration. The jury location (determined in Phase III; inboard jury location shown in Figure 124), was based on a structural design laid out in SUGAR Phase II (2; 4), and was coupled to EI/GJ design constraints. The location and structural constraint of the jury position was maintained as a constraint during the $\mathsf { M } = 0 . 7 4 5$ aerodynamic design task completed in SUGAR Phase III. However, when the aircraft geometry was updated for a $\mathsf { M } { = } 0 . 8$ planform, the location of the jury strut location and structural constraint was revisited. During the design phase of the $\mathsf { M } { = } 0 . 8$ 1104-001-RD configuration, it became clear that a revision to the strut planform (and associated jury strut location) was

required (refer to Section 4.1.2.5 for details). This revision moved the jury strut $\sim 4 0 ^ { \prime \prime }$ outboard of the Phase III location (center jury location shown in Figure 123). Following the aerostructural design optimization (described in detail in Section 4.1.1.3), the jury-strut attachment was finalized at the outboard-most location shown in Figure 124 $( Y = 4 2 5 ^ { \prime \prime } )$ ).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 124 – Design Progression for Jury Strut Location。
![](images/3d1fcf25c320d834a3ff7c56f8896a7065256bf34bf61bebd827176b1600ba3e.jpg)  
Figure 124 – Design Progression for Jury Strut Location.

When the final jury strut-to-strut spanwise location was chosen, the wing attachment point of the jury was allowed to fluctuate within a small range. Inclusion of the flap hinge fairings into the final aerodynamic design created some location constraints for the jury-wing attachment location due to the potential for aerodynamic interference between the jury strut and FHF. It was therefore decided to collocate the two structures in order to minimize the potential for interference. While this also creates the potential for load-sharing between the jury support structure and flap structural hardpoints, the design of this joint has not yet been examined in detail.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 124 – Design Progression for Jury Strut Location。
![](images/ad5e6862e83b35ec139bca02d34232dc1ccb9fb6cf3825c55a16d8e17f6faa73.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 124 – Design Progression for Jury Strut Location。
![](images/b59d1f2e35bb6eecc9bb7cb5363a6a7d9a895eca32e61518bc29b31f5cb85e4d.jpg)  
Figure 125 – OVERFLOW results of the final jury strut installation surface Cp (Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, Remac = 14M).

The final jury strut location (collocated with the FHFs) was analyzed in OVERFLOW to determine the net change in aircraft performance, and to determine if any negative aerodynamic interference results from the intersection of the two structures. The results (highlighted in Figure

125 and Appendix Figure A.35) show a small high-velocity region at the root of the jury-to-strut intersection, with a more pronounced zone of flow acceleration on the inboard side of the jury. While some potential for a reduction in drag may still exist for the jury strut installation, the potential benefit was considered to be too small to warrant additional design effort.

# Nacelle-Pylon Design

The nacelle-pylon installation for Phase IV was initially derived from the nacelle and pylon geometry used during Phase III. However, the nacelle geometry and aft pylon closeout were modified to account for operation at higher freestream Mach number. The nacelle outer mold line was optimized using the Boeing optimization tool MDOPT. The nacelle orientation was then adjusted using MDOPT to reduce total system drag by determining the optimum toe and pitch engine alignment angles.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 126 – Nacelle Shape and Rigging were optimized using MDOPT t。
![](images/09e009f354fd90e53fae1ebea9105229d67f539c1980d08a08eb8c3fdb36b28c.jpg)  
Figure 126 – Nacelle Shape and Rigging were optimized using MDOPT to reduce installed drag (Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, Remac = 14M).

The MDOPT optimization process reduced the nacelle pitch angle by almost $3 . 0 ^ { \circ }$ from the original location, and toed-in the nacelle an additional $1 . 3 7 ^ { \circ }$ as shown in Figure 126 and Appendix Figure A.36. However, the results also showed that near the optimum drag location, the drag of the engine installation was relatively insensitive to small perturbations to the orientation of the nacelle, particularly in toe angle. Therefore, the final nacelle orientation was finalized based on a combination of the optimization results combined with design guidance from the propulsion group. The end result was a toe-in angle of ${ \sim } 1 . 9 2 ^ { \circ }$ and pitch angle of $\boldsymbol { - 0 . 7 6 ^ { \circ } }$ .

# 4.1.4 1104-001-RG – Final Configuration Aerodynamic Performance

The final assessment of the updated $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ high-speed OML was assessed in OVERFLOW for a variety of configurations, starting with the wing-body-strut (WBS), and ending with a ‘full-up’ trimmed configuration. Based on the confidence gained during Phase III in predicting component increments, the revalidation of the basic configuration building blocks (i.e. body only, bodysponson, etc.) of the configuration are not presented. In addition, the increment for a body-wingalone configuration (no strut) is not presented since the proper quantification of wing-strut interference would require the development of a wing designed in isolation (i.e. no strut

influence), combined linearly in drag with a strut designed in isolation (i.e. no wing influence to provide a fair assessment of wing-strut interference for a system in which the wing and strut have been designed in tandem). This is consistent with the planned wind tunnel test entry in which wing and strut will only be tested together.

# Configuration Details

Following completion of the highspeed design phase of the program, the configuration underwent a reloft and smoothing process to prepare the final lines. The process included airfoil smoothing, thickness and twist distribution smoothing, renormalization of wing and strut chords to the vehicle planform, and a reshearing of both the wing and strut airfoils. The wing reshear

图片摘要：该图主要展示 127. Configuration 1104 001 RG: Final Wing Twist Distributio。
![](images/e09135e5e57ff0bb0e5d75c72066bec79ca64822e1300352353427b64e26669c.jpg)  
Figure 127. Configuration 1104-001-RG: Final Wing Twist Distributions.

process was primarily focused on producing a geometry that was conducive to the cutout of the high-lift system. Since the leading edge device was more likely to be sensitive to variation in rigging (relative to the wing) across the span, the wing airfoils were resheared to a projection of the Krueger leading edge (in the stowed position) onto the wing reference plane. The reshearing therefore emphasized leading edge smoothness at the expense of a larger variation in trailing smoothness. The results of the reloft process resulted in new twist and thickness characteristics (new twist distributions are shown in Figure 127 and the thickness distribution is shown in Appendix Figure A.37).

# High-Speed Design Results

The final configuration build-up results for the 1104-001-RG design are presented herein. Results, as acquired from OVERFLOW, are shown primarily for the midcruise design point $\scriptstyle ( 1 = 0 . 8 0$ , Alt=40kft, $C _ { \mathsf { L } } { = } 0 . 6 9 5$ ), with excursions to high and low lift coefficients where stated. Results shown are for flight wind tunnel Reynolds number only – quantification of low-Re results for wind tunnel application will be presented in a subsequent report (Volume II) following the completion of the planned transonic wind tunnel test in January 2019.

# Wing-Body Strut (WBS) Results

The vehicle spanload for the WBS system is shown in Figure 128. The plot shows the breakdown in vehicle lift for the wing and strut individually (red and blue curves respectively) along with the

total spanload (black curve). An elliptical spanload guideline (dashed line) based on the wingstrut contribution to vehicle lift (no body effect) is shown for reference.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 128 – Configuration 1104 001 RG (WBS) Spanload at the Cruise。
![](images/388b27a72bb71427cb03dfe3997a7b529b4b3957f29fba665cb3dde3700800fb.jpg)  
Figure 128 – Configuration 1104-001-RG (WBS) Spanload at the Cruise Design Point $\left( \alpha = 1 . 5 1 ^ { \circ } \right)$ .

The results in Figure 128 highlight the change in design philosophy from the Phase III design approach. For the $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ design, an active decision was made to increase strut loading from previous levels. This approach was aided by the configuration changes when moving to a higher-Mach design. As highlighted in Figure 129, the increase in wing sweep in Phase IV had a net beneficial effect on the overall performance of the configuration. The most critical aspect of this was an aerodynamic decoupling of the inboard wing and strut flows. While the Phase III design had a more significant ‘stacking’ of the wing and strut flows, the Phase IV design offset the wing and strut root chords (relative to each other) so that the inboard strut was now located significantly behind the wing root. In addition, the change in overlap also propagated outboard – the regions in which the thickest portions of the wing and strut airfoils overlap near the wingstrut juncture was also significantly reduced. These changes allowed strut loading (particularly inboard) to be increased significantly from earlier designs.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 128 – Configuration 1104 001 RG (WBS) Spanload at the Cruise。
![](images/76f798b1d3b3b2949a3ca264054d4f3cda45a6440a07b1b1e9b6b97fae97e440.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 129 – Comparison of wing-strut overlap regions for M=0.745 design vs. M=0.80 design.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 129 – Comparison of wing strut overlap regions for M=0.745 d。
![](images/25513b6243301a53b225a88f5f212a3a21403a71fb05f3b913f475f8a436b145.jpg)  
Figure 130 and Appendix Figure A.38 show the surface ${ \mathsf { C } } _ { \mathsf { p } }$ contours for the WBS configuration (strut displaced for clarity). Although the final geometry was designed with the nacelle and pylon installed, the flow is well behaved over the wing. Inboard strut loading is relatively low as compared to previous designs with shorter chords. Some waviness in pressures on the inboard strut are the result of removal of the nacelle and pylon from the WBS solution.   
Figure 130 – Wing and Strut surface Cp results from OVERFLOW for WBS Configuration at Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, Remac = 14M.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 130 – Wing and Strut surface Cp results from OVERFLOW for WB。
![](images/4d918cb32e83b40b7384d576b91bc9a9b21463669c0233048aaae274cdcf989b.jpg)  
NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 131 – Drag Polar for 1104-001-RG (WBS) at Mach $=$ 0.80, $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = 1 4 \mathsf { M }$ .

图片摘要：该图主要展示 131 – Drag Polar for 1104 001 RG (WBS) at Mach 0.80,。
![](images/d19d709f2d15d0d9a2d8d170d385605f357aa0c4ac36f07da9fd646f7fb46abe.jpg)  
Figure 132 – L/D for 1104-001-RG (WBS) at Mach = 0.80, Remac = 14M.

Figures 131 and 132 (and Appendix Figures A.39 and A.40) document the performance of the WBS configuration. This configuration is used in the aircraft-level performance analysis to anchor the conceptual methods to the hard CFD data on the configuration. The maximum $\mathsf { L } / \mathsf { D }$ of the WBS system is just over 27.0 (untrimmed). Additional items added to the configuration (nacelle and pylon, tail, etc.) will reduce the system-level L/D as described in the following paragraphs. The majority of the aerodynamic design and optimization was performance using the WBS configuration, with both manual methods and inverse design tools CDISC (8) used to refine the optimized configurations for the effects of the installed nacelle and pylon.

# Wing-Body-Strut-Nacelle and Pylon (WBSN) Results

The final stage of the detail design effort focused on tailoring the WBS configuration results to minimize engine interference, and return the wing and strut pressures both on and off-design to desired targets. As shown in Figure 133, the optimized spanload distribution of the WBS configuration has been altered by the presence of the engine nacelle and pylon. While some of the ‘divot’ in the total configuration spanload is filled by the nacelle’s contribution to lift, the net effect of the installation is a decrease in wing load and corresponding increase in strut loading, particularly on the inboard strut. The end result for the WBSN configuration (Figure 133a) has been adjusted through the use of CDISC to the presented spanload distribution.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 s 131 and 132 (and Appendix Figures A.39 and A.40) document 。
![](images/ce657cdf4ac9d309825a23235abb9f5e4079cd4cfa9d53f04ecc7001d31de818.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 s 131 and 132 (and Appendix Figures A.39 and A.40) document 。
![](images/f098773e845f770215a61a0fd5d2310f16514b8932d01d188c23ac512e8e758e.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 133 – Configuration 1104 001 RG Spanload Effects of engine a。
![](images/ce4731620197d5386b95d661ac3278e9d8ac1479d0ec44cd74b785893080205e.jpg)  
Figure 133 – Configuration 1104-001-RG Spanload - Effects of engine and pylon installation (Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, Remac = 14M).

The performance of the final WBSN configuration is compared to the WBS geometry in Figure 134 and Figure 135 (and Appendix Figures A.41 and A.42). At flight Reynolds number, the drag of the configuration has increased at the cruise design point. Presented drag values (for all subsequent installations in which the nacelle is installed) have been adjusted from baseline CFD drag values to remove the skin friction from nacelle internal surfaces, core, and scrubbed areas of the pylon that are accounted for in the aerodynamic performance build-ups as part of the propulsion system. Peak L/D values of the fully turbulent results have decreased.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 133 – Configuration 1104 001 RG Spanload Effects of engine a。
![](images/d3b540ee8c2a1afc80d53cfb7c0437f881dafb14417157dc09d3eda2fbd77b05.jpg)

![](images/ae6a6e95d944af3133ec55f27cdc288456420d5e561d890e71df0813f0de5b15.jpg)  
Figure 135 – Configuration 1104-001-RG – Effects of engine installation on the vehicle L/D (Mach = 0.80, Remac = 14M).

OVERFLOW analysis comparing the wing and strut pressures for the same configurations are shown in Figure 136 and Appendix Figure A.43. The configuration with the engine installed showed several changes to the WBS configuration aerodynamically optimized by the TRANAIR design process. Inboard wing loading in the proximity of the engine decreased, while inboard strut loading increased. To compensate for a small loss in lift for the configuration, angle of attack

increased from $\alpha { = } 1 . 5 1 2 ^ { \circ }$ to $\alpha { = } 1 . 5 7 8 ^ { \circ }$ . Outboard wing loading remained essentially constant, as did the strut and wing loading proximate to the wing-strut juncture.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 135 – Configuration 1104 001 RG – Effects of engine installa。
![](images/4815673a26c7c716600bc8367e1bcd2c9ba8b994e7b6060b9b8ba4250da47bfc.jpg)

# Wing-Body-Nacelle and Pylon-Flap Hinge Fairing-Jury Strut (WBSNFJ)

Prior to the investigation of tail installation and trim effects, the configuration was finalized with the addition of flap hinge fairings (FHFs) and the jury strut. While these elements would ideally have no negative net effect on the aircraft performance, in reality these structures could potentially introduce localized areas of concern or have unanticipated global effects – the proximity of the FHFs to the wing-strut juncture were of particular concern. The spanload plot shown in Figure 136 highlights the changes in spanload caused by these installations. Wing loading in the wing-strut juncture has increased slightly, with a step-change in spanload distribution slope occurring outboard of the FHF-jury-strut intersection. To compensate, local strut download in the juncture has increased, resulting in a small angle of attack decrease from $\alpha { = } 1 . 5 7 8 ^ { \circ }$ to $\alpha { = } 1 . 5 2 7 ^ { \circ }$ .

图片摘要：该图主要展示 136 – Configuration 1104 001 RG Effect of engine installatio。
![](images/470ba6c805fddbdd12e12df2a05c9e735dde8b0202115e6e44c89ad307297c7a.jpg)

图片摘要：该图主要展示 136 – Configuration 1104 001 RG Effect of engine installatio。
![](images/8b7643030704102379b20c3b752eff0a4d0aaa24f14baf32a10e193032b8035e.jpg)  
Figure 137 – Configuration 1104-001-RG Spanload - Effects of FHF and jury strut installation (Mach = 0.80, CL = 0.695, Remac = 14M).

The performance of the WBSNFJ configuration is compared to the WBS and WBSN geometries in Figure 138 and A.44, Figure 139 and A.45, and Figure 140 and A.46. At flight Reynolds number, the drag of the configuration has increased at the cruise design point relative to the WBSN configuration. Peak L/D values of the fully turbulent results have decreased. Figure 140 also plots the aerodynamic performance of the configuration as a pseudo range factor, in which $\sqrt { M }$ is multiplied by the L/D of the vehicle. This relationship combines the typical metric for aerodynamic efficiency found in the Breguet range equation with an approximation of the slope of the SFC curve which is proportional to $\sqrt { M }$ .

Figure 141 and Appendix Figure A.47 show the wing pressures for the final WBSNFJ configuration operating at the cruise design point. The pressures on this turbulent wing design are the end result of a combined design process in which aerodynamic optimization via the TRANAIR tool was combined with manual changes to the geometry along with inverse aerodynamic design via CDISC. Although the wing was designed and analyzed as a fully turbulent geometry, the sectional wing pressures were intended to remain ‘laminar friendly’, in that the distributions did not deviate far from a pressure architecture that could be made more conducive to laminar, particularly considering the relatively short-chord (high aspect ratio) wing.

![](images/0148ce1c3f0b41143369636dc8af2d8e80ae95d809a054d2c2dcdb8287e084f1.jpg)  
Figure 141 – Configuration 1104-001-RG - Wing Pressures (WBSNFJ) M=0.80, CL=0.695, Alt=40kft, $\mathtt { R e } _ { \mathrm { m a c } } = 1 4 \mathsf { M }$ .

Appendix Figure A.48 shows the strut pressures for the cruise design point, as well as sectional wing pressures at the same spanwise locations. The figure highlights the increased inboard strut loading that was targeted for this configuration. Shocks on the strut upper surface are very weak, allowing the strut to contribute to the lift of the vehicle with very little compressibility drag. This level of loading enables the strut to avoid being a simply parasitic contributor to aircraft drag. The strut contours have primarily been a result of aerodynamic optimization, but have also been heavily dependent on aerostructural optimization to maintain the structural viability of the strut design. (details of this design procedure are shown in Section 4.1.1.3). In addition, while an increased level of strut lift has been a strategic goal from the beginning of the Phase IV design effort, the optimal contribution of strut lift to the vehicle spanload remains an area in which further study is required.

The net changes to the lift distribution is reflected in the wing and strut surface ${ \mathsf { C } } _ { \mathsf { p } }$ contours shown in Figure 142 and Appendix Figure A.49. With the exception of the localized flow acceleration on the upper surface of the strut at the jury strut attachment location, the differences to the surface pressure distributions are relatively small. The increased flow acceleration around the underside of the outboard strut on the WBSNFJ configuration suggest an increase in flow spillage from the channel between the jury-strut attachment and wing-strut attachment.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 141 – Configuration 1104 001 RG Wing Pressures (WBSNFJ) M=0.。
![](images/b706ca908eab339d926c7bef70720e1908f239b763b757e3ae4df5c75e266744.jpg)  
Figure 142 – Configuration 1104-001-RG - Effect of FHF and jury strut installation on wing and strut surface pressure contours (M=0.80, CL=0.695, ${ \mathsf { R e } } _ { \mathsf { m a c } } = 1 4 { \mathsf { M } } )$ .

# Drag Divergence Mach (MDD) and Long-Range Cruise (LRC)

The performance of the tail-off configurations was examined to determine an estimate for vehicle Long Range Cruise (LRC). The method assumes that LRC point is $1 \%$ down from the peak ‘range factor’ curve plotted as a function of operating Mach number. The results presented in Figure 143 and Appendix Figure A.50 show that the built-up configurations have essentially the same predicted LRC point – this suggests that no one component exhibits unusual interference as a function of operating Mach.

Drag rise curves of the configuration component build-up are shown in Figure 144 and Appendix Figure A.51. The calculated drag divergence Mach $( M _ { \mathsf { D D } } )$ of the configuration is presented for each configuration. As shown, the design is operating efficiently at the design cruise Mach number. A small amount of drag creep is found in the WBS solution; since the final configuration was designed with the nacelle and pylon installed, the WBS-only design is slightly compromised in design when analyzed in isolation.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 142 – Configuration 1104 001 RG Effect of FHF and jury strut。
![](images/2fd1f4d63074accaa29feea28f8b9438985b7fd5d5a35dcd63aa0c837247218e.jpg)  
Figure 143 – Configuration 1104-001-RG Long-Range Cruise (LRC) Estimates (CL = 0.695, ${ \mathsf { R e } } _ { \mathsf { m a c } } = 1 4 { \mathsf { M } } )$ .

图片摘要：该图主要展示 143 – Configuration 1104 001 RG Long Range Cruise (LRC) Esti。
![](images/9a52965d314ee558b2de403c0dd3eb5357495c9e76630b05e5c7b9e2ba228d79.jpg)

# Buffet Analysis

The BUFFET program was used to analyze the final design performance at the upper and lower lift coefficients to determine the potential for aerodynamic buffet. As stated previously, the TTBW has the theoretical possibility for buffet to occur and both high lift coefficients (where the wing and wing-strut juncture may be critical) and at low lift coefficients (where the strut lower surface and wing-strut juncture may be critical). The code quantifies shock strength on the critical surfaces and compares it against an estimated critical level. The critical surfaces for high and low lift coefficient cases are shown in Figure 145 (Appendix Figure A.52) and Figure 146 (Appendix Figure A.53) respectively. At high lift coefficient values, the only critical surface is the wing upper

surface inboard of midspan (i.e. the strut and wing-strut junction are not critical). At low lift coefficient values, there are actually no surfaces that are predicted to be close to buffet boundary. Figure 146 shows that there is significant margin to the critical Mach number on the strut lower surface.

It should be noted that that while the FAA regulations for “buffet free” flight are based on the perception of buffet created by a structural response to flow separation, it is unclear if the BUFFET results presented herein are a true indicator of (local) buffet on the wing or strut. It is also unknown if such local buffeting would be perceived by the pilot and deemed unacceptable. Further study and dedicating testing is needed to answer these questions.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 145 – Buffet analysis of wing upper surface at high lift coe。
![](images/b8657dc1239b55a334280e70bcb29b6296671d5d88c3389d29983f7c82b762b0.jpg)  
Figure 145 – Buffet analysis of wing upper surface at high lift coefficient (Mach=0.80, CL = 0.91, Remac = 14M).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 145 – Buffet analysis of wing upper surface at high lift coe。
![](images/a8cc125a7c9704986b60304b7c5c82adcdbf3285d101a9be919513e539083f3e.jpg)  
Figure 146 – Buffet analysis of strut lower surface at low lift coefficient (Mach=0.80, $\mathtt { C } _ { \mathrm { L } } = 0 . 5 0$ , Remac = 14M).

# Trimmed Performance

The performance of the finalized vehicle design, including tail and trim penalty were computed. OVERFLOW analysis of the full configuration were analyzed for a range of horizontal tail settings in order to determine the tail setting where trim occurs. The results of the analysis, presented in Figure 147 (Appendix Figure A.54) and Figure 148 (Appendix Figure A.55), show that the fully trimmed design is operating with the peak performance occurring very near the intended cruise $\mathsf { C } _ { \mathsf { L } }$ of 0.695. The drag offset from the fully turbulent, untrimmed WBS solution includes the drag

offset for the nacelle and pylon, flap hinge fairings, and both horizontal and vertical tail surfaces for a fully trimmed configuration.

# Final High-Speed Performance

The aerodynamic performance results presented herein detail the high cruise efficiency of the TTBW concept operating at $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ . Results from OVERFLOW indicate that the TTBW is aerodynamically well suited to operation at the design $\mathtt { M } = 0 . 8 0$ transonic cruise Mach number. And, although significant time and effort has been invested into maximizing the performance of this configuration, the design was performed under significant design constraints (fixed planform, wing sweep, etc.). Therefore, it is reasonable to assume that this configuration can be rightly considered a ‘snapshot’ of the possible design potential of the TTBW concept, and is not intended to suggest the vehicle’s upper limit of performance. The results obtained in this detailed study are a large component of the performance estimates outlined in Section 6.0. However, the analyzed configuration does not include potential performance increments for items such as laminar flow potential and excrescences that must be considered in order to determine aircraftlevel performance.

When the CFD data is folded into the aircraft-level performance, and adjusted as per the conceptual design build-up procedure, the demonstrated performance benefit of the TTBW concept (independent of other technologies applied on the SUGAR configuration) has been calculated to be a $7 . 2 \%$ improvement in fuel burn (per seat) for an economic mission of 900nm, and a $9 . 0 \%$ improvement in fuel burn (per seat) for a 3500nm mission with full passenger payload. The benefits quoted are relative to an aspect ratio 13 conventional tube and wing configuration of equivalent technology development. For full details of the aircraft level performance benefit, please refer to Section 6.0.

# 5.0 Configuration Analysis

This section includes the discipline analyses of the configuration documented in Section 2.0. These analysis data are representative of the 1104-001-RG configuration.

# 5.1 Aerodynamics

The aerodynamics analyses are divided into high speed and low speed sections.

# 5.1.1 High Speed

Computer Aided Sizing and Evaluation System (CASES) cruise drag bookkeeping is shown in Figure 149.

$$
C _ {D} = C _ {D o} + C _ {D i} + C _ {D c} + C _ {D t} \tag {7}
$$

Parasite drag represents the incompressible zero-lift drag $\left( \mathsf { C } _ { \mathsf { D } \circ } \right)$ . Induced drag $( { \mathsf { C } } _ { \mathsf { D i } } )$ accounts for the drag due to lift based on airplane efficiency factor at Mach 0.50. Compressibility drag $( C _ { \mathsf { D c } } )$ is a function of both Mach and lift coefficient while trim drag $( { \mathsf { C } } _ { \mathsf { D } \mathrm { t } } )$ accounts for pitching moment on the configuration.

Using CASES accounting and leveraging the wing-body-strut CFD solutions from 765-

Figure 149 – CASES Standard Buildup.

095 Rev-J (Mach 0.745 configuration), the high speed aerodynamic buildup for 1104-001-RG as compared to 765-095 Rev-J is shown in Table 20.

It should be noted that the aerodynamic technologies benefit (NLF/HLFC/Riblets) does not include the wing natural laminar flow (NLF) benefit. Wing upper surface NLF was book-kept under airplane compressibility drag during the Phase II study, and that convention is maintained here. CASES, like many performance routines, bookkeeps all drag due to Mach number as compressibility drag, which for this study, includes a change in drag due to natural laminar flow as illustrated in Figure 150. This explains why the compressibility drag appears as a negative number in the drag buildup.

The figure illustrates the maximum aerodynamic efficiency $( \mathsf { M } ^ { \ast } \mathsf { L } / \mathsf { D } )$ occurring at the design cruise Mach (0.80) and CL (0.695). # 5.2 Mass Properties

The group weight statement for the 1104-001-RG is shown in Table 21. These weights incorporate the results of the 765-095 FEA and aeroelastic wind tunnel work from Phase II and III, and have been cycled with Aerodynamic Performance. The mass properties data are based on 765-095- Revision H, which is similar to revision J and does not necessitate an update of the analysis.

Boeing’s weight estimation tools include sensitivity to wing sweep and thickness, however, on the TTBW, some additional weight must be accounted for in the fuselage. As the wing sweeps about its mean aerodynamic center (to maintain balance and loadability), the distance between the wing carry through and strut carry through lengthens. This portion of fuselage must carry the load imparted between these two structural elements in shear. Reviewing fuselage frame and stiffened panel data of existing aircraft led to adding 100 pounds to account for this increased loading.

A preliminary look at the vehicle balance diagram is shown in Figure 155. Understanding the vehicle’s loading characteristics is required to provide the attendant mass distributions for structural loads cases.

A couple of items are noteworthy. First, with the amount of fuel available with the wing as drawn, the vehicle cannot meet the same 150,000 pounds MTOW used for the 765-095 series, so the MTOW was capped at 145,000 pounds; this is born out in the cycled MTOW. Second, the next vehicle iteration would address the fact that this aircraft’s loading flexibility would be significantly improved by shifting the wing forward to encompass the typical forward biased passenger loading case, while also encompassing the typically loaded baseline mission case. Such a wing shift, without an envelope expansion, would likely reduce the required sizes for the tails, and, if the main landing gear remains in the same location, add weight to the fuselage shell, as described above.

# 5.3 Stability & Control

This section describes the work performed to evaluate the Stability and Control of the Mach 0.80 TTBW configuration.

# 5.3.1 Longitudinal Stability and Control Assessment

Longitudinal Stability and Control analysis was focused on defining the center of gravity (CG) envelope for the aircraft that encompasses the desired loadability cases of the aircraft. These loadability cases were defined by weights and reported in Section 5.2. In finalizing the S&C CG limits, an iterative process between the S&C team and Mass Properties occurs to converge on CG limits that both meet all requirements and encompass all desired loadability cases. For the purposes of the Mach 0.80 TTBW, only an assessment was made on the longitudinal Stability and Control. Any design changes required to meet the longitudinal requirements were not implemented for this design in order to keep a common empennage OML to the Phase III effort. Instead, recommendations are given on solutions that would potentially satisfy all longitudinal S&C requirements while maximizing the loadability of the aircraft.

# Longitudinal Stability and Control Requirements

The requirements by which to assess the longitudinal S&C properties of the aircraft are primarily derived from Boeing internal requirements. Boeing has historically shown that meeting these

internal requirements enable the aircraft to meet the requirements outlined in 14 CFR Part 25 by the FAA. It is necessary to meet these requirements for the FAA to certify the aircraft.

The forward CG limit is defined by the most critical (i.e. most aft) of the following requirements:

• Takeoff Rotation – At the most critical weight, thrust, and high lift settings, the airplane must rotate from ground attitude to the takeoff attitude with adequate pitch acceleration.   
• Go-Around – At the most critical weight at glide slope of -3 degrees, the aircraft must rotate from approach attitude to the go-around climb attitude with adequate pitch acceleration.   
Nose Wheel Hold Off – After the main gear touches down on landing with the most critical weight and high lift settings, the pilot must be able to hold the nose gear off the ground up to the stall speed of the aircraft.

The aft CG limit is defined by the most critical (i.e. most forward) of the following requirements:

• Stall Recovery – This requirement is captured in 14 CFR 25.145(a) which specifies that the pilot must be able to make a “prompt” nose down recovery during a stall maneuver. This translates to a required nose-down pitch acceleration that the vehicle must meet at stall at the most critical weight and high lift settings.   
• Nose Wheel Steering – For adequate steering capability, a certain percentage of the aircraft weight must be applied to the nose gear when the vehicle is on ground and static.   
Static Margin – Internal Boeing studies have determined a minimum static margin necessary that would allow the pilot to control the aircraft and land safely in the event of a failure in the pitch stability augmentation system.

# Longitudinal S&C Analysis

To evaluate these requirements for this aircraft, aerodynamic data is required for the vehicle. Typically, aero data from the highest fidelity source is used in these assessments. In this case, wind tunnel data from the Phase III Mach 0.745 vehicle is used. The data is then adjusted using results from a lower order code such as Athena Vortex Lattice (AVL) (14) to be representative of the Mach 0.80 TTBW vehicle. AVL models of the Mach 0.745 vehicle and the Mach 0.80 vehicle were created and run to produce ratios and increments that are applied to the Phase III wind tunnel data to create an aero database suitable for CG limit analysis.

This analysis was performed on the 1104-001-RCdes13 version of the vehicle. Since the wing planform has not changed since that version, this analysis is still generally applicable to the final design of the vehicle.

These representative aero data along with mass properties, thrust data, and vehicle geometry data are then analyzed using Boeing’s CG limit evaluation tools for conceptual design. The results of the analysis are typically presented in the form of a “scissor” plot. This plot (Figure 156) shows the CG limits of the vehicle for a given horizontal tail volume. Note that in the plot, the stall recovery and static margin lines overlap.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 156 – 1104 001 RG Scissors Plot。
![](images/7489574b1ea49522b9c4e02489926b3b575935e50d2629f63c457f7998af3be3.jpg)  
Figure 156 – 1104-001-RG Scissors Plot.

To achieve a 45 inch CG range identified in previous work, a tail volume of 1.4277 was be achieved. This translates to a tail area of 272.4 ft2 . Previously for Phase III this resulted in a tail area of 273.3 ft2 . Ideally, to maximize the CG range for a given tail, the nose wheel steering requirement line (NW Steering) should intersect the Static Margin requirement line at the point where the aft CG limit is located. For this vehicle, Nose Wheel Steering is the limiting case for the aft CG limit while the Nose Wheel Liftoff (i.e. Takeoff Rotation) requirement sets the forward CG limit. These limits were then applied to the Weight and Balance diagram for this vehicle to get a clear picture of whether this vehicle can meet all the loading missions. The Weight and Balance diagram from Mass Properties is detailed in Figure 155.

To read this diagram properly, each “balloon” is a separate scenario the aircraft may experience when loading payload and fuel. The “stick” of each balloon is the payload being loaded onto an empty airplane at Operational Empty Weight (OEW). The “balloon” part of each scenario represents the fuel loading. Any point within the balloon is an achievable loading by appropriately loading the fuel tanks. Thus, to successfully load the aircraft according to each scenario, the point at which the “stick” and “balloon” must fall within the CG limits. That point represents the aircraft in flight with zero fuel and that specific payload.

With the current CG limits of roughly $10 \%$ to $50 \%$ , the main mission, ferry mission, and Max Zero Fuel Weight scenario can be achieved. However, both the forward biased payload and aft biased payload mission are unattainable (these are not necessarily requirements). This is because the lower right point of those two scenarios (which represents that specific payload arrangement at zero fuel) does not fall within the CG limits. The forward and aft biased loadings represent two extreme scenarios that an airline may experience. However, airlines typically will ensure that the payload is loaded such that the CG of the aircraft falls within an acceptable range. Additionally, passengers typically don’t all sit in the front or back of an airplane, they tend to distribute themselves evenly among the cabin.

For the purposes of this project, the current horizontal tail will remain unchanged however, with detailed wing and landing gear shift trades, more loadability or smaller tail sizes could be attained.

# 5.3.2 Lateral Stability and Control Assessment

One of the primary goals of the Phase IV effort is to design and analyze a high lift system for the Mach 0.80 TTBW vehicle. Toward that end, a control surface layout was designed such that the high lift system can operate while meeting all roll requirements. The initial layout proposed in Phase II consisted of a high speed inboard flaperon and an outboard aileron with flaps and spoilers.

Five different control surface layouts were proposed as options for this vehicle. Each one was evaluated for roll authority against roll requirements. The planform that performed the best was baselined for this vehicle. The candidate planforms are outlined in Figure 157.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 157 – Candidate Wing Control Surface Layouts。
![](images/f07dc0f04ba6475220cc716a544a6a20baf987aedc92d85bd890851b75ebbf51.jpg)  
Figure 157 – Candidate Wing Control Surface Layouts.

The left wing is the layout as of the end of Phase III on the Mach 0.745 wing planform. The five layouts on the right are the candidate layouts for the Mach 0.80 TTBW aircraft.

# Roll Requirements

The two primary roll authority requirements are Maximum Steady State Roll Rate and Time-To-Bank. The minimum steady state roll rate required for the vehicle depends on the flight conditions. Under normal operation, 25 degrees per second is the minimum target. Above cruise Mach (MC) or cruise velocity $( \mathsf { V } _ { \mathsf { C } } )$ , the requirement is relaxed to 15 degrees per second.

The time-to-bank requirement is set by the time it takes the aircraft to bank 30 degrees. For the terminal flight phase, the aircraft must bank in less than 2.5 seconds to meet Level 1 flying qualities. In nonterminal flight phase, the requirement is relaxed to 3 seconds for Level 1 flight and 6 seconds for Level 2 flight. Note that above the max operating Mach $( \mathsf { M } _ { \mathsf { M O } } )$ , Level 2 flying qualities are allowed.

Other considerations for selecting the most feasible layout are that the aircraft must have the capability for lateral trim at high speed. Typically, this function is performed by inboard aileron/flaperon and is not used for roll.

Additionally, if the control surface experiences aeroelastic reversal at any point in the flight envelope, the surface is locked out in that flight regime and cannot be used for roll or trim. This is typically experienced by the outboard aileron, which can only be used for low speed roll.

# Roll Authority Analysis

To analyze the roll authority of the Mach 0.80 TTBW design, wind tunnel data from the Phase IV Retest of the Mach 0.745 vehicle (Ames 11ft. NASA Test designation AT0343) was used. This data was adjusted using AVL to represent aero data for a Mach 0.80 TTBW design. However, because the proposed layouts do not directly match up with the layout on which wind tunnel data was collected, control surfaces must be mapped from the original layout. The diagram below shows how the control surfaces were mapped to each of the candidate planforms (Figure 158).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 158 – Control Surface Layout Similarity。
![](images/965e02a8dde3c08828d37e5bfee98777a2931b2078b9044195651c737a6d0b79.jpg)  
Figure 158 – Control Surface Layout Similarity.

Estimates of the aeroelastic effects of the vehicle were taken from results from the Mach 0.80 FEM produced in Phase III. Spoiler aeroelastic effect trends were taken from legacy McDonnell-Douglas aircraft data.

The max deflections of all ailerons was 40 degrees trailing edge up and 30 degrees trailing edge down. The max deflections of spoilers were 60 degrees trailing edge up. In all scenarios, the max deflection of all surfaces were used. Note that only spoilers were used to roll the aircraft for all starred regions of the flight envelope in the tables that follow. This is because any surface that aeroelastically reverses at any part of the flight envelope is locked out from use. Reversal has

been observed in the outboard aileron. All available surfaces were used for Approach. The results are summarized Table 22 and Table 23.

Before discussing the results summarized above, some planform layouts can be eliminated immediately. Because the outboard aileron is locked out from use at high speed, there are no other devices with which to laterally trim the vehicle for Layout 3. Thus, Layout 3 can be eliminated as a viable option. All other planforms have some method of laterally trimming the vehicle at high speed provided that the inboard aileron does not reverse.

While all layouts do not meet both roll requirements at Approach, Layout 4 shows the best performance, particularly at low dynamic pressures where aircraft will be realistically flying (typical approach dynamic pressure is around 55 psf). This area of the envelope is the driving case for sizing surface areas and setting max deflections of control surfaces. This is due to the more stringent requirement at low speed combined with the low dynamic pressure. Depending on the flexibility of the wing, some small aeroelastics effects may also be present which might limit roll performance.

Even though Layout 4 is not predicted to meet all requirements, the design is feasible and may close with higher fidelity analysis and/or continued trade studies. Since this situation is a commonly encountered trade-space in aircraft design, there are a range of available potential solutions, such as: increasing the low speed surface deflections of the outboard ailerons, increasing the aileron area, increasing the stiffness of the wing, or utilizing more advanced technology to increase the effectiveness of the control surfaces. While the high AR makes the problem more challenging by shortening the chord lengths of roll control surfaces, shortfall in roll power at this point in the design cycle is not normally considered a showstopper especially when estimated with a low order analysis.

# 6.0 Performance

Boeing’s Computer Aided Sizing and Evaluation System (CASES) tool has been used here to calculate mission performance including payload, range and fuel consumption. The CASES tool consists of separate programs for analyzing mission, takeoff, and landing performance, respectively, all of which have been validated over time against empirical airplane performance data. These analyses may be conducted for various atmospheric conditions, and low-speed field length analyses include both all-engine and one engine inoperative (OEI) calculations.

Performance and sizing for SUGAR is performed within a ModelCenter environment, which serves as a wrapper for the various CASES performance analysis components. Aerodynamic, propulsive and mission data are specified, as are the basic empty weight and sizing derivatives. The ModelCenter environment provides a platform for running the parametric trade studies and optimizations necessary for performance and sizing in addition to providing some data visualization tools. Engine thrust and wing area were varied to minimize block fuel per seat while enforcing Takeoff Field Length (TOFL), Initial Cruise Altitude Capability (ICAC), climb, approach speed, and fuel margin constraints to size the latest SUGAR 1104-001 configuration. The sizing process is illustrated in Figure 159.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 159 – Airplane Sizing Using CASES / ModelCenter。
![](images/3ce1f56323c6e5c2b719ca1bb35c607c0198f615a56dbfbf910ae219d3c3e51f.jpg)  
Figure 159 – Airplane Sizing Using CASES / ModelCenter.

# 6.1.1 Sizing Requirements

A set of top level requirements for the SUGAR vehicles was generated from the future scenario previously reported in SUGAR Phase I (12). These top level requirements were turned into specific payload-range requirements, which are illustrated in Figure 160. The figure has several points of interest indicated and described below.

1. The airplane is required to fly the average range (900 nm) at the maximum payload condition (which is the same as the maximum zero fuel weight condition). The maximum payload is required to be 15,200 pounds heavier than the payload corresponding to Point 2.   
2. The airplane is required to fly the maximum range (3,500 nm) at the average number of filled seats using 200 pounds per passenger and no revenue payload. This point must be achieved using less than $90 \%$ of the useable fuel.   
3. Point 3 is used to calculate vehicle fuel consumption and take-off field length (TOFL) performance for the SUGAR program. This is the point that represents the most common operating condition for this vehicle class, and corresponds to a range of 900nm.

Both Points 1 and 2 are required for sizing because airplane characteristics may alter which of the two is critical. The mission profile for SUGAR 2035 concepts is shown in Figure 161. Advanced concepts studied under this program are evaluated using the illustrated mission while the reference vehicle SUGAR Free (2005 best-in-class) is flown with a non-advanced air traffic management system. These missions are further documented in the Phase I report (1).

图片摘要：该图主要展示 160 – Payload Range Requirements。
![](images/6d00e35edf4d2f3a7cc691b1e00633f2c56faaad08fd2340230dd512db0db069.jpg)  
Figure 160 – Payload-Range Requirements.

图片摘要：该图主要展示 160 – Payload Range Requirements。
![](images/f824e34d3a31d1296c33b0ea78489865c95f96225dcb77126188e7d95adf2606.jpg)  
Figure 161 – 2035 Mission Profile with NextGen Air Traffic Management.

# 6.1.2 Sizing Results

The performance of the SUGAR 1104-001-RG is shown in Table 24, compared against SUGAR 765- 095-RJ, which is the Mach 0.745 variant.

There are several different sizing cases shown; from left to right, the first two columns are referred to as ‘As-Drawn’ meaning the performance was run at the reference wing area, thrust, and MTOW used to generate the baseline vehicle data. The next set of columns represent sized data for minimum fuel consumption constrained by initial cruise altitude capability (ICAC) and fuel margins. The ICAC constraint requires that the aircraft be able to climb to the altitude yielding best specific range (its optimum altitude). It should be noted that the takeoff field length, climb performance, and approach speed constraints were inactive. These configurations were sized at maximum range cruise (MRC) Mach. The remaining two columns illustrate the corresponding fallout performance data for the MRC-sized configurations run at long-range cruise (LRC) Mach, which seeks to fly at $9 9 \%$ of the maximum specific range. Operation at LRC allows an increase in aircraft productivity with only a modest impact to fuel consumption.

The performance of the airplane is shown relative to the SUGAR Free baseline (configuration 765- 093 with mission performance run in BMAP, an alternative sizing and performance tool) in Table 25. This shows a 57.0 and 55.9 percent reduction in fuel consumption for the 765-095-RJ and 1104-001-RG respectively.

* Cruise Mach is set as MRC or LRC which is then calculated and reported by the performance routine

Performance was evaluated at various combinations of fuel and payload weights to generate the payload-range curve shown in Figure 162. This curve shows that the 1104-001 variant, like the 765-095-RJ, does not meet the stated range requirements at the maximum payload condition with only 798 nautical miles of range. The airplane was not sized to accommodate this maximum payload condition in an effort to maintain consistency with the 765-095-RD UDF (9) and clearly illustrate the improvements made during the Phase III study. The flat upper portion of the curve (at 46,000 lb. payload) represents the maximum zero fuel weight constraint of the airplane. The shallow sloped portion of the curve is set by Maximum Takeoff Weight (MTOW). The final far right region of the chart is set by the fuel volume limit.

Block Fuel for each configuration is compared to the SUGAR Free in Figure 163. The NASA goal is a $60 \%$ reduction. The fuel consumption per segment in comparison to SUGAR Free is shown in Figure 164 for the 900 nautical mile economic mission. Fuel consumption as a function of range is illustrated by Figure 165.

NASA Contract NNL16AA04B,NNL17AA46T – Subsonic Ultra-Green Aircraft Research Phase IV Final Report Volume I – Mach 0.80 Transonic Truss-Braced Wing High-Speed Design Report   
Figure 165 – Block Fuel vs. Range between SUGAR FREE (2005 Best-in-Class), 765-095-RJ Sized, and 1104-001 Sized.

It should be noted that in the sizing results presented thus far, both the 765-095-RJ and the 1104- 001-RG configurations contained 1,000 gallons of fixed body fuel tank volume. This decision slightly reduced fuel consumption as fuel volume constraints were relaxed on the wing planform. Because the body tank size was held constant between the two variants and the Mach 0.745 aircraft has lower total fuel consumption, this means a greater percentage of the fuel was stored in the body for the slower aircraft. As both wings are sized to accommodate the additional fuel required, this led to a slightly larger wing on Mach 0.80 aircraft (a slight discrepancy in sizing). To allow a more direct comparison, both vehicles were also sized without a body fuel tank. These variants are denoted 795-095-RJ-01 and 1104-001-01. The results are shown in Table 26 below, where they are referenced to the SUGAR Free aircraft. It can be seen that removing the body tank causes both variants to size up and increases fuel consumption. The fuel use difference between the Mach 0.745 and 0.80 aircraft is reduced from 1.08 lbs/pax to 0.93 lbs/pax when the aircraft are compared without body fuel, and it is believed that this is a more accurate depiction of the fuel consumption delta associated with higher Mach number cruise.

# 7.0 Summary

In Phase I of the SUGAR project, a truss-braced wing configuration was identified as having the potential to reduce fuel consumption and emissions for a domestic passenger transport mission. Two primary areas of risk were identified: novel aeroelastic effects and uncertainty in the assumed level of aerodynamic interference between the wing and strut at transonic conditions.

During Phase II, the first of these risk areas was addressed through the development of a detailed analytical model and an aeroelastic wind tunnel test in the NASA Langley Research Center Transonic Dynamics Tunnel. Both flutter speed and mechanism were found to be analytically predictable, and the weight increment associated with flutter margin was found to be small enough to maintain the aerodynamic advantage of increased span. Further work remains in introducing unsteady aerodynamic effects to better capture the aeroelastic boundary shapes with the theoretical model, but the results indicate that aeroelastic phenomena should not pose a major barrier to the development of transonic truss-braced wing aircraft.

During Phase III the second primary risk area was addressed through detailed aerodynamic design of a truss-braced wing aircraft with a nominal cruise Mach number of 0.745. A variety of modern computational fluid dynamics and optimization tools were used, including Navier-Stokes-based methods, and a series of transonic wind tunnel tests was performed to validate the predicted aerodynamic performance. Based on computational results, interference effects between the wing and strut were reduced to approximately $1 \%$ of total airplane drag at this Mach number and uncertainty in predicted aircraft performance was significantly reduced. Wind tunnel tests of the configuration were performed at the NASA Ames 11-ft TWT. Preliminary results are presented in the Appendix of this report. Detailed analysis of the results will be presented in a subsequent volume.

An additional task during Phase III was the preliminary conceptual design of a higher speed airplane, with a cruise Mach approaching 0.80. The higher cruise Mach number enables increased aircraft productivity, and is believed to more closely align to market demand in the single-aisle domestic passenger transport class. This activity became the basis for this Phase IV activity documented herein.

The results of the Phase IV design effort focused on the development of TTBW technology for a Mach 0.80 aircraft. Detailed high-speed design optimization, vehicle high-lift system design and performance, and structural concept development sizing tasks were completed. Validation tests were conducted both for transonic cruise performance as well as high-lift performance, with the results of these tests presented in a separate series of reports (13) (14) (15).

The performance of the updated Mach 0.80 TTBW design was quantified at the aircraft level, with the TTBW vehicle showing a significant improvement in performance relative to a conventional tube and wing aircraft. Based on detailed Reynolds-averaged Navier Stokes analysis obtained using the NASA code OVERFLOW, in combination with the conceptual design build-up procedure, the predicted performance benefit of the TTBW concept was calculated to show a $7 . 2 \%$ improvement in fuel burn (per seat) for an economic mission of 900nm, and a $9 . 0 \%$ improvement in fuel burn (per seat) for a 3500nm mission with full passenger payload. This benefit is relative to an aspect ratio 13 conventional tube and wing configuration of equivalent technology. When considering the TTBW as part of the suite of technologies that make up the SUGAR concept (as described in the Phase I report (12)), the Mach 0.80 TTBW aircraft was found to enable a $5 5 . 9 \%$ reduction in fuel use (for a 900nm economic mission) relative to a best-in-class single-aisle transport typical of the 2008 fleet. Although operating at Mach 0.80 has reduced the fuel burn benefit by $1 . 1 \%$ as compared to the Mach 0.745 TTBW SUGAR aircraft, the increase in cruise speed has significantly improved the aircraft’s productivity.

Structural concept development work completed in Phase IV has identified several strut attachment concepts that are worth additional development activity – the inboard strut attachment swivel joint concept enables the strut to carry axial loads while avoiding strut bending induced from wing twist as the wing lifts during flight, significantly simplifying the construction of the main strut. The outboard strut attachment offset (curved beam) strut concept holds potential for further reducing the aerodynamic interference in the wing-strut channel and further mitigating buffet concerns.

Based on work performed to date on Phase IV and the previous phases of the SUGAR program, the TTBW concept continues to remain a viable concept for significantly improving the performance of future aircraft. High-fidelity aerodynamic analysis of the Mach 0.80 TTBW aircraft has shown fuel burn improvement that has exceeded initial expectations despite the increase in cruise Mach number. Structural concept maturation has continued to refine the TTBW structure, and stability and control assessments of the vehicle have been made, with neither effort finding any immediate ‘show-stoppers’ to date. Therefore, the potential benefit of improved performance through the use of a very high aspect ratio strut-braced wing continues to warrant additional study.

# 7.1 Further Considerations

The aerodynamic performance of the Mach 0.80 TTBW at cruise currently exceeds previous expectations and maintains significant performance advantage over comparative cantilever aircraft of equivalent technology. As a part of the Phase IV effort, wind tunnel testing of both high- and low-speed performance took place starting in 2019. These tests helped to validate vehicle performance predictions, particularly near the cruise design point. While the high-speed

test largely achieved the planned objectives, challenges in the low-speed test delayed some desired objectives, particularly surrounding ice-effects testing and in-ground-effect performance. These objectives have been deferred to a later test entry.

There are therefore several additional areas post-Phase IV remaining in TTBW aerodynamic development which require attention in order to further reduce configuration risk. The most immediate risks regarding the TTBW vehicle aerodynamics are:

1) Uncertainty surrounding the prediction of buffet boundary.   
2) High-lift system performance and stall behavior under icing conditions and in-groundeffect.   
3) Low-speed performance at higher Reynolds Numbers

The ability to accurately predict aerodynamic buffet (item 1) as well as the structural response of TTBW structure to aerodynamic forcing functions remains uncertain. Current prediction methods for determining buffet boundary are empirically-derived from flight and wind tunnel tests of conventional cantilever wing aircraft. Applicability of the established boundaries to this species of aircraft is yet to be validated, and requires a focused study. Given the existence of a final set of Mach 0.80 high-speed TTBW loft, this could be initially addressed via a dedicated buffetfocused wind tunnel test. This model would have significant instrumentation requirements in excess of previous TTBW wind tunnel models, and would likely be achieved through the use of a transonic semi-span model.

Additional low-speed test data focused on icing effects and performance in-ground-effect continues to be an area of interest. These items both address potentially unique sensitivities of the TTBW configuration due to its high aspect-ratio design. In particular, the increased leading edge length and small leading edge radius of both the wing and struts could substantially increase anti-ice system energy requirements, while significantly decreasing the amount of space available for the integration of anti-ice systems. Also, the high wing aspect ratio is likely to have significantly different sensitivities to performance in ground effect as compared to a conventional configuration.

In addition, the data collected in the low-speed wind tunnel to date has been at very low Reynolds Numbers. Since vehicle stall behavior and $\mathsf { C } _ { \mathsf { L m a x } }$ levels are a function of Re, it will be important to quantify the vehicle low-speed performance in the tunnel at higher Re. This data is critically important for establishing the low-speed performance data needed for sizing the vision vehicle.

The Phase IV study was undertaken using a fixed planform defined by a multidisciplinary optimization carried out by Virginia Tech during Phase I. The significant performance advantage of the Mach 0.80 configuration studied in Phase IV again has illustrated that additional multidisciplinary planform optimization is required including variables such as strut and jury tie-

in location, cruise Mach number and strut tie-in strategy. This analysis needs to include detailed aeroelastic investigations, structural modeling of the vehicle’s non-linear structural reponse, as well as high-fidelity aerodynamic modeling in order to determine reliable system-level trades.

Perhaps the most significant remaining uncertainty in the TTBW concept is the maturity of structural concepts and associated vehicle weight. Investigations to date have focused primarily on either the development of conceptual concepts or global-level analyses. While many of these structures have been investigated in detail, true maturation of the TTBW structure will require a detailed, integrated structural design in which the various concepts are combined into a working model. These integrated structural concepts must be investigated, developed, and demonstrated to address requirements regarding damage tolerance, fail-safe design, and failure modes.

# 7.2 Recommendations

Based on lessons learned to date and the remaining configuration unknowns, the following recommendations are made:

 Perform a dedicated buffet-focused wind tunnel test. Use data to improve and/or validate buffet boundary prediction methods for TTBW configurations.   
 Perform a second high-lift wind tunnel test using the existing low-speed model to investigate icing effects and performance in-ground-effect.   
 Perform a high-Re Number low-speed wind tunnel test to determine performance sensitivitiest to Re. Establish vehicle maximum lift levels to be used for updated vision vehicle sizing analysis.   
 Launch a detailed structural integration and design effort to mature TTBW structural concepts. This activity should include design requirements documentation, preliminary structural design, and detailed design and integration of critical structure. The activity should also plan (and execute) structural tests focusing on damage tolerance (bird strike) of the wing, strut, and jury strut and consider other options such as a fuselage drop test for high wing aircraft.   
 Perform equivalent conceptual-level design, sizing and optimization studies of cantilever and truss-braced wing aircraft of the same technology level to allow a more fair and transparent comparison of the two configurations. Anchor studies using newly collected aerodynamic and aeroelastic data obtained during Phase II through IV, as well as data that will become available after the test of a Mach 0.80 TTBW aircraft.   
 Conduct the preliminary design phase of a possible transonic truss-braced wing demonstrator aircraft.
